<a id="paper-2605.21276"></a>
### ⭐ [Benchmarking a machine-learning differential equations solver on a neutral-atom logical processor](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21276v1)

**Highlighted author(s):** Antoine Browaeys  
**Authors:** Pauline Mathiot, Elio Garnaoui, Axel-Ugo Leriche, Evan Philip, Boris Albrecht, Clémence Briosne-Fréjaville, Lorenzo Cardarelli, Antoine Cornillot, Gwennolé Cournez, Luc Couturier, Julius De Hond, Rebecca El Koussaifi, Thomas Eritzpokoff, Florian Fasola, Antonio Andrea Gentile, Casper Gyurik, Clotilde Hamot, Loïc Henriet, Gaétan Hercé, Michael Kaicher, Lucas Lassablière, François-Marie Le Régent, Edgar Leroux, Yohann Machu, Hadriel Mamann, Luis Ortiz, Annie Paine, Thomas Pansiot, Arnaud Peloquin, Francisco Ponciano, Julien Ripoll, Raja Selvarajan, Adrien Signoles, Henrique Silvério, Siddhy Tan, Marie Taouzinet, Selim Touati, Louis Vignoli, Antoine Browaeys, Pascal Scholl  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21276v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **5/5** · 🔥 `Rydberg arrays` **4/5**

L is then minimized using a classical algorithm to update the...</sub>

</details>

Distortions in the kernel shape can be traced back to coherent errors in the gates execution. d. Circuit used to compute the logical quantum kernel. </details>

<sub>FIG. 4. Solving differential equations using an experimentally-computed quantum kernel. a. In the space S spanned by all solutions of differential equations, up to a certain accuracy quantified by some residual, there is a subset K that can be expressed by the kernel, which is ex- pected to be enlarged by an increase in quantum volume and shrunk by processor error. **Summary.** This paper demonstrates that a logical quantum processor can solve differential equations more accurately than a physical processor by using error detection to suppress kernel distortions. By implementing a [[4, 2, 2]] code on a neutral-atom platform, the researchers showed a significant reduction in the error of the final application-level solution. This highlights the importance of fault-tolerant architectures for practical quantum machine learning tasks.

**Why it may be interesting.** It demonstrates a practical application of error detection in a neutral-atom platform, showing how logical encoding can specifically target and suppress coherent errors that degrade algorithmic performance in quantum machine learning.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Benchmarking the performance of a machine-learning differential equation solver on a neutral-atom logical processor to determine if logical qubits can mitigate kernel distortions caused by hardware noise.

**Main result.** The logical implementation using a [[4, 2, 2]] error-detecting code systematically outperforms the physical implementation, significantly reducing kernel shape distortion and achieving much higher accuracy in solving non-linear differential equations.

**Method.** A hybrid quantum-classical workflow using quantum kernel methods and mixed-model regression to solve differential equations, compared across physical and logical qubit encodings.

**Model / system.** A neutral-atom quantum processor based on Rubidium-87 atoms in an optical tweezer array, utilizing Rydberg blockade for two-qubit gates and a zoning architecture for atom transport.

**Key observables.** Quantum kernel quality (probability p00), kernel distortion (shape and amplitude alterations), and the residual (NRMSE) of the differential equation solution.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coherence times (T1 approx 5s, T2 approx 1.49s), gate fidelities (single-qubit approx 99.96%, CZ approx 98.7%), and the [[4, 2, 2]] error-detecting code.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The benchmark differential equations are chosen such that their solutions are expressible within the kernel's feature space; error models assume errors can be decomposed into Pauli and atom loss channels.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the end-to-end benchmark workflow; Figure 2 details the quantum kernel circuit and regression process; Figure 3 illustrates the atomic hardware, imaging, and error-corrected circuits; Figure 4 compares the distribution of residuals for linear and non-linear DEs; Supplementary figures detail error models, hardware stability, and proxy-phasing.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the benchmarking goal, describes the hardware platform and error-correcting encoding, details the machine-learning algorithm for solving DEs, presents the comparative results between physical and logical kernels, and concludes with an error analysis and discussion of future scaling.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We report on a performance comparison between physical and logical computations on a prototypical machine-learning application: solving differential equations using quantum kernel methods. The algorithm is implemented on an atom-based logical quantum processor, both at the physical and logical levels. We show that the kernel estimated from the logical implementation performs better than its physical counterpart on relevant metrics. We observe how such performance improvement can be traced back to specific noise-induced errors detected by the chosen encoding. We apply the computed quantum kernel to the task of solving differential equations, confirming how the superior performance of a logical quantum kernel is retained also at an end-to-end applicative level. Our findings show that experimental validation of end-to-end protocols can already highlight the positive impact of fault-tolerant implementations despite their higher quantum resource count, and guide application-informed architectural choices.

</details>

### [Multi-Qubit Entanglement of Unit Cell Pairs in SiMOS](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20781v1)

Electrons are accumulated at the Si/SiOx interface under the plunger (P) gates. The device is operated in the (3,5,5,3) charge configuration. c, Single-qubit Rabi oscillations of the qubits Q1-Q4 corresponding to qubits under gates P1-P4 as a function of applied microwave pulse duration. d, Decoupled controlled phase oscillations...</sub>

<img src="2605.20781_figures/2605.20781_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2 | Device operating sequence and readout techniques. a, Schematic diagram showing the sequence structure for a single-shot experiment using the sequential readout technique. The four-qubit heralded initialisation uses a real-time logic state filter that re-initialises the processor unless blockaded parity is measured in both DQDs. The order of both the initialisation and terminating readouts are interchanged each measurement shot. The total measurement time is displayed below, assuming a 10 µs control operation Uc. b, Two-dimensional readout histogram from GST experiments using sequential readout method. State classification thresholds are displayed as dotted black lines. See Extended...</sub>

</details>

<img src="2605.20781_figures/2605.20781_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3 | Three-qubit entangled state measurements. a, Circuit used to generate a three-qubit cluster state (equivalent to the GHZ state up to local single-qubit gates) and perform state tomography. {P1Q} are the set of single-qubit pre-measurement rotations required to perform state tomography. <details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.20781_figures/2605.20781_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4 | Entanglement lifetime. a, T Hahn 2 -style measurement on the three qubit state |+++⟩measured by removing the CZ operations from the circuit in Fig. 3a and adding idle times. b, Mermin value as function of idle time, representing the longevity of the entanglement between the three qubits. This is measured by inserting wait times on either side of the refocusing pulses. c, Measurement of the oscillations in Mermin value when the exchange time is extended. Here the exchange time for both J2 and J3 are increased proportionally with each other. Error bounds on T2 values represent 1σ uncertainty.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a four-qubit SiMOS quantum processor that successfully generates and verifies three-qubit GHZ and cluster states. It achieves this by implementing parallelized readout and a specialized gate sequence that protects entanglement from dephasing. This work provides a significant step toward scaling silicon-based quantum computing using industry-standard CMOS techniques.

**Why it may be interesting.** The use of a laddered decoupling sequence to extend entanglement lifetime toward the Hahn-echo limit and the demonstration of multipartite entanglement in a scalable semiconductor architecture are highly relevant to open quantum systems and quantum network research.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Scaling SiMOS quantum dot processors beyond isolated double quantum dot unit cells by achieving high-fidelity coupling between unit cells and implementing efficient, parallelized initialization and readout.

**Main result.** Demonstrated a four-qubit SiMOS processor capable of generating maximally entangled three-qubit GHZ and cluster states, with entanglement lifetimes extended toward the Hahn-echo limit using a laddered decoupling sequence.

**Method.** The researchers used a decoupled-CZ (dCZ) gate set, FPGA-based heralded initialization, and parallelized Pauli spin blockade readout, while employing Gate Set Tomography and Mermin-witness operators for state verification.

**Model / system.** A four-qubit SiMOS (Silicon Metal-Oxide Semiconductor) processor consisting of a linear array of quantum dots in two coupled double quantum dot (DQD) unit cells, utilizing isotopically enriched silicon.

**Key observables.** Single- and two-qubit gate fidelities, Mermin witness values, coherence times (T2* and THahn), Rabi frequencies, and readout fidelity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Larmor frequencies ~11.4 GHz, T2* between 4.8-6.2 us, THahn between 76.3-87.2 us, and two-qubit dCZ fidelities ~95.5-95.8%.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The dephasing is primarily driven by charge noise rather than hyperfine coupling; SPAM errors can be modeled and corrected via a uniform depolarizing channel.

**Figures summary.** Figures demonstrate the comparison of sequential vs. simultaneous readout, the calibration of detuning for parallel operation, the implementation of the laddered gate sequence, and the decay of Mermin values as a function of idle and exchange time.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the SiMOS platform, describes the architecture and control/readout hardware, details the gate implementation and decoupling strategies, presents the generation and verification of multi-qubit entanglement, and concludes with a discussion on scalability and noise sources.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Spin qubits in silicon-MOS (SiMOS) quantum dots have recently demonstrated compatibility with existing industry standard CMOS fabrication techniques. These devices have routinely achieved single- and two-qubit gate fidelities above 99% and demonstrated highly entangled two-qubit Bell states in isolated double quantum dot (DQD) unit cells, however coupling between unit cells has remained challenging. In this work, we present a two unit cell, four-qubit SiMOS processor with universal controllability and fully parallelised state initialisation and readout. We use this processor to generate maximally entangled three-qubit states, including the Greenberger-Horne-Zeilinger (GHZ) state, and certify multipartite entanglement through violation of the classical Mermin-witness bound. By using a fully symmetric dynamically decoupled gate sequence to create our entangled states, we are able to preserve the lifetime of the entanglement beyond $T_2^*$, to a time limited instead by $T_2^\textrm{Hahn}$. These demonstrations pave a road to the scalable operation of larger SiMOS processors, and achieving high purity, long-lived multi-qubit entangled states in them.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.20930"></a>
### [Symmetry-Protected Fast Relaxation and the Strong Quantum Mpemba Effect](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20930v1)

**Authors:** Zijun Wei, Mingdi Xu, Yefeng Song, Yangqian Yan, Lei Pan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20930v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **4/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `Rydberg arrays` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20930_figures/2605.20930_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic illustration of symmetry-protected Liouvillian relaxation. Left: A highly symmetric initial state couples exclusively to an isolated fast-decay Liouvillian mode in the same symmetry sector, producing single-channel exponential relaxation. Right: A symmetry-broken initial state projects onto multiple Liouvillian sectors, including slow modes, leading to slower relaxation and a hierarchy of dissipative timescales. This symmetry-filtered mode accessibility underlies the strong quantum Mpemba effect discussed below.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.20930_figures/2605.20930_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Emergence and disappearance of the strong quan- tum Mpemba effect. The dashed line denotes the universal symmetry-protected trajectory D(t) ∼e−2t of the ground state. Solid curves represent finite-temperature initial states for different interaction exponents α. For α &gt; 0, the initially farther ground state relaxes faster than thermal states and overtakes them, demonstrating a strong quantum Mpemba effect. In the all-to-all limit (α = 0), the relaxation trajec- tories collapse because the enlarged permutation symmetry generates a degenerate Liouvillian manifold at λ = −2. Inset: Spectral overlap showing both T = 0 and finite-temperature states occupy the same degenerate λ = −2...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.20930_figures/2605.20930_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Ground-state relaxation dynamics in the long-range XXZ model (L = 8, J = 1). (a) In the SU(2)-symmetric XXX limit (∆= 1), the relaxation dynamics collapse onto the universal exponential law D(t) ∼e−2t for all interaction exponents α. The inset shows that the ground state overlaps exclusively with Liouvillian modes at λ = −2, demonstrating symmetry-selected access to an isolated fast-decay channel. (b) Breaking the symmetry through anisotropy (∆= 1.1) fragments the Liouvillian spectrum and activates multiple de- cay sectors, including slow modes. The resulting slow-mode overlap suppresses the relaxation dynamics and produces a pronounced interaction-range-dependent slowdown.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.20930_figures/2605.20930_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Interaction-range evolution and symmetry-breaking control of relaxation. The relaxation dynamics of the ground state (T = 0), thermal state (T = 10), Z2-symmetric state, and domain-wall state are shown for different interaction exponents: (a) α = 1, (b) α = 2, (c) α = 3, and (d) α = 4. The highly symmetric ground state remains dominated by the isolated fast-decay Liouvillian channel at λ = −2, whereas lower-symmetry states develop finite overlap with slow modes and therefore relax more slowly. The interaction range controls the separation between relaxation trajectories, while the hierarchy is fixed by symmetry-filtered Liouvillian accessibility.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.20930_figures/2605.20930_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Matrix representation of the analytically constructed SU(2)-symmetric operator O(2) (left) and the numerically ob- tained Liouvillian eigenmode at λ = −2 (right). The identical structure confirms that the universal fast-relaxation channel is uniquely governed by the symmetry-protected mode O(2).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper identifies a mechanism where SU(2) symmetry protects a fast-decaying channel in a long-range XXZ spin chain. This protection causes a strong quantum Mpemba effect, where highly symmetric states relax much faster than less symmetric thermal states. The findings suggest that symmetry can be used to engineer and control relaxation dynamics in open quantum many-body systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fundamental mechanism for controlling nonequilibrium pathways and many-body lifetimes in open quantum systems using symmetry as a control knob, which is highly relevant to the stability of states in trapped ions or Rydberg arrays.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding how symmetry constrains dissipative relaxation in open many-body systems and identifying the microscopic origin of the strong quantum Mpemba effect.

**Main result.** The authors discover a symmetry-protected fast-decay channel in an XXZ spin chain where SU(2) symmetry allows certain states to bypass slow decay modes, leading to an anomalous relaxation speed where states farther from equilibrium relax faster than closer ones.

**Method.** Liouvillian spectral decomposition, symmetry analysis using SU(2) and permutation groups, and analytical derivation of exact Liouvillian eigenmodes.

**Model / system.** A long-range spin-1/2 XXZ spin chain subject to local dephasing noise, described by a Lindblad master equation with an interaction exponent alpha.

**Key observables.** Trace distance (D(t)) from the steady state and the spectral weight/overlap of initial states with Liouvillian eigenmodes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Anisotropy parameter (Delta), interaction range (alpha), and temperature (beta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is governed by a Markovian Lindblad master equation with local dephasing jump operators.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a schematic of the mechanism showing how symmetric states couple to a single fast mode while symmetry-broken states project onto slow modes; Figure 2 shows the ground state relaxation dynamics.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of symmetry-constrained relaxation, presents the analytical identification of a fast-decay mode at the SU(2) point, explores the effect of symmetry breaking and interaction range, and concludes with the emergence of the strong quantum Mpemba effect.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Understanding how symmetry constrains dissipative relaxation in open quantum many-body systems remains a central challenge in nonequilibrium physics. Here we uncover a symmetry-selective Liouvillian mechanism that protects an isolated fast-decay channel in a long-range XXZ spin chain subject to dephasing noise. At the \(SU(2)\)-symmetric point, highly symmetric initial states couple exclusively to an exact Liouvillian eigenmode with decay rate \(λ=-2\), producing universal exponential relaxation independent of system size and interaction range. Breaking the symmetry restores overlap with slow Liouvillian modes and substantially suppresses the relaxation dynamics. This symmetry-filtered mode accessibility naturally gives rise to a strong quantum Mpemba effect, where a state farther from the steady state relaxes anomalously faster than closer thermal states. Our results establish symmetry-protected fast relaxation as a mechanism for controlling nonequilibrium pathways in open quantum systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.20542"></a>
### [Mean-field and fluctuation dynamics in off-resonant two-mode atom-field interactions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20542v1)

**Summary.** This paper presents an analytical approximation for the dynamics of a two-level system interacting with two off-resonant electromagnetic modes. By separating mean-field dynamics from quantum fluctuations, the method captures essential entanglement and back-action effects that standard semiclassical models miss. This allows for efficient study of complex multi-mode light-matter interactions.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a computationally efficient way to study complex atom-field interactions and entanglement generation in multi-mode cavity QED and circuit QED without requiring full quantum simulations.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The two-mode Jaynes-Cummings model lacks an exact analytical solution because its dynamical Lie algebra is infinite-dimensional, unlike the single-mode case.

**Main result.** The authors develop an approximation method that decomposes the dynamics into a solvable semiclassical component and a residual part treating quantum fluctuations, maintaining high fidelity even during entanglement.

**Method.** The approach uses a semiclassical reduction (replacing field operators with c-numbers) combined with a sequence of unitary transformations and the Magnus expansion to capture quantum correlations and back-action.

**Model / system.** A two-level system (atom) coupled to two independent quantized electromagnetic modes within the Jaynes-Cummings framework under the Rotating Wave Approximation.

**Key observables.** Atomic inversion, average photon number, quantum fidelity, atomic coherence, and linear entropy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Off-resonant regime (large detunings), large field amplitudes (coherent states), and coupling strengths (g1, g2).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes sufficiently large photon numbers to justify the semiclassical substitution and is specifically optimized for the off-resonant regime.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 compares different analytical approximations against numerical simulations; Figure 3 shows how two modes cooperatively achieve atomic inversion; Figure 4 and 5 illustrate the evolution of fidelity, linear entropy, and photon numbers.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the algebraic complexity of the two-mode JCM, introduces a decomposition strategy into semiclassical and fluctuation components, applies the Magnus expansion and Wei-Norman theorem for solutions, and validates the method via numerical comparison with the full quantum model.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study a two-level system coupled to two quantized electromagnetic modes within the Jaynes-Cummings framework. While the single-mode model is exactly solvable due to its conserved excitation number, yielding finite-dimensional invariant subspaces, the two-mode model extension presents a fundamental challenge: although the total excitation number remains conserved, each invariant subspace is infinite-dimensional, preventing a closed-form analytical solution. Our scheme separates the dynamics into a dominant, exactly solvable semiclassical component, the atom interacting with the mean fields of both modes, and treats the remaining quantum fluctuations through a sequence of unitary transformations that preserve essential quantum features. We validate our approach through direct comparison with numerical solutions, focusing on the non-resonant regime where multiple detunings give rise to rich interference effects and multi-timescale dynamics inaccessible to standard approximations. The method accurately reproduces atomic inversion, field observables, and fidelity over relevant timescales, while remaining computationally efficient.

</details>

### [Resource generation and dynamical complexities in open random quantum circuits](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20350v1)

**Summary.** This paper explores how environmental memory influences the growth of quantum resources and complexity in random circuits. It finds that while memoryless environments destroy entanglement, memoryful environments can sustain non-classical features and even enhance pseudorandomness compared to closed systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides deep insights into how controlled environmental interactions (memory) can be used to manipulate the generation of non-classicality and chaos in open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how environmental memory affects the generation of quantum resources (entanglement, magic) and dynamical complexity (Krylov complexity, k-designs) in open random quantum circuits.

**Main result.** Environmental memory (MFORC) preserves entanglement and complexity, even outperforming closed systems in generating low-order k-designs, whereas memoryless environments (MLORC) cause entanglement to decay to zero while non-stabilizerness persists.

**Method.** Numerical simulations of random circuits using the Lanczos algorithm for Krylov complexity, trace distance for k-designs, and Rényi stabilizer entropy for non-stabilizerness.

**Model / system.** Three classes of random circuits: Random Unitary Circuits (RUC) for closed systems, Memoryless Open Random Circuits (MLORCO) with fresh auxiliary qubits, and Memoryful Open Random Circuits (MFORC) with retained auxiliary qubits.

**Key observables.** Logarithmic negativity, non-stabilizerness (magic), Krylov complexity, quantum state k-designs, mutual information, and fluctuations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size (Ns), number of auxiliary qubits, and the presence/absence of environmental memory.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is performed on relatively small system sizes (up to Ns=8) and focuses on accessible timescales for randomness generation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares circuit architectures and metrics; Figure 2 shows circuit diagrams; Figure 3 displays growth of entanglement and mutual information; Figure 5 shows SRE growth; Figure 7 shows Krylov complexity growth; Figure 8 shows trace distance for k-designs; Figure 9 illustrates GHZ-like vs W-state-like entanglement structures.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces three circuit models, analyzes resource generation (entanglement and magic) from a resource-based viewpoint, evaluates dynamical complexity (Krylov and k-designs) from a statistical viewpoint, and concludes with the impact of memory as a control parameter.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Realistic quantum devices are inherently open and often involve environments with memory. Here, we investigate quantum resource generation in two classes of random circuits, namely, memoryless open and memoryful open random circuits, and compare their behavior with the well-explored random unitary circuit model. We show that environmental memory qualitatively alters the dynamics: while unitary and memoryful circuits exhibit sustained growth and saturation of entanglement and non-stabilizerness (magic); memoryless dynamics leads to a distinct behavior where entanglement decays to zero after transient growth, even though non-stabilizerness remains non-zero, indicating the persistence of nonclassical features beyond entanglement. Consistently, Krylov complexity reveals suppressed spreading of quantum states in memoryless circuits, in contrast to strong growth in unitary and memoryful dynamics, which saturates at the maximum value. Finally, we show that memoryful circuits more effectively approach low-order quantum-state k-designs than the other two circuits. Closed dynamics are therefore usually the most resource-generating, but are ideal; realistic dynamics are open and seem to generate less, but if they possess memory, they can sometimes even outdo closed dynamics.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper studies how a disordered, liquid-like environment modifies high-order harmonic generation by inducing decoherence. It demonstrates that scattering from the environment leads to a secondary spectral plateau and the emergence of 'dynamic scars' where the electron density localizes around classical periodic orbits.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a mechanistic link between structural disorder and decoherence in a time-dependent framework, extending the concept of quantum scars from static systems to driven, open-system dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how a stochastically structured, disordered environment (simulating a liquid phase) affects high-order harmonic generation (HHG) and drives the transition from quantum to classical behavior.

**Main result.** The disordered environment induces global decoherence and a secondary HHG plateau due to off-site recombination, causing the electron probability density to localize around unstable classical periodic orbits (dynamic scars).

**Method.** Numerical solution of the 1D time-dependent Schrödinger equation using a symplectic integrator, combined with an ensemble average over stochastic configurations to represent the density matrix.

**Model / system.** A single atom with a soft-Coulomb potential in a 1D environment containing a stochastic distribution of Gaussian-well perturbers, driven by a strong laser field.

**Key observables.** HHG intensity spectra, harmonic amplitudes/cutoff, system purity, and photoelectron probability density.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Laser intensity (7.896e14 W/cm2), wavelength (1030 nm), mean inter-scatterer distance (10 a.u.), and ionization potential (0.90 a.u.).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is modeled in one dimension to isolate elastic scattering, and the environment is treated via ensemble averaging rather than a Markovian reservoir.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the pair-correlation function of the scatterers; Figure 6 illustrates the decay of purity over time; Figure 9 shows the spatial component of the flow for classical trajectories.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of HHG in liquids, describes the 1D stochastic model and numerical methods, analyzes the resulting HHG spectra and decoherence dynamics, and concludes by linking the results to the concept of quantum scars.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Using one-dimensional simulations analyzed through the lens of open quantum systems, we study the photoelectron's strong-field dynamics from an atom surrounded by a scattering environment stochastically structured. We theoretically investigate high-order harmonic generation from this situation. We show that local dephasing of the photoelectron wavepacket induced by elastic scattering leads to global decoherence. This drives a transition from quantum to classical behavior, as witnessed by the photoelectron probability density localizing around specific trajectories of the classical analog system: unstable periodic orbits. This phenomenon mirrors quantum scars traditionally observed in the eigenfunctions of time-independent systems, such as quantum billiards. Here, it emerges in-situ within a time-dependent framework, manifesting directly in the real-time dynamics from the ground state rather than solely through spectral analysis.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper reports the experimental observation of a tripartite quantum phase where extended, localized, and critical states coexist in an orbital optical lattice. By using a two-stage Floquet protocol, the authors successfully prepare and distinguish these states using momentum-space and real-space diagnostics. The work demonstrates how inter-orbital coupling can be used to engineer complex localization physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a versatile platform for studying unconventional critical phenomena, localization physics, and the transition between different types of quantum states using Floquet engineering.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The observation and characterization of a tripartite quantum phase where extended, localized, and critical states coexist over finite spectral windows in a quasiperiodic system.

**Main result.** The researchers experimentally realized and identified a tripartite phase in an orbital optical lattice, demonstrating that inter-orbital coupling creates incommensurately distributed zeros that drive the emergence of critical states.

**Method.** The study uses a two-stage Floquet engineering protocol (frequency and amplitude ramps) for state preparation, combined with Renormalization Group (RG) analysis for theoretical validation and expansion dynamics for transport characterization.

**Model / system.** Ultracold 87Rb atoms in a 1D quasiperiodically driven s-p orbital optical lattice, where s and p orbitals are coupled via Floquet modulation.

**Key observables.** Momentum distributions (via TOF), Fractal Dimension (FD), Spatial Coherence Length (L_Coh), Momentum-space Correlation Length (k_Cor), and expansion exponents (alpha).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Inter-orbital coupling (Omega), quasiperiodic modulation strength (delta_s, delta_p), shaking frequency (f), and shaking amplitude (A).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is treated as non-interacting (mean-field interactions are negligible), and the primary lattice is deep enough to justify a localized Wannier basis.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: Experimental setup and visual comparison of wavefunctions; Fig 2: Fractal dimension and phase regions; Fig 3: State preparation pathways; Fig 5: Phase diagram via dual-space analysis; Fig 7: Momentum distribution micromotion; Fig 8: Expansion dynamics.

**Paper structure.** Introduction to the tripartite phase problem, experimental setup and Floquet engineering description, development of the two-stage preparation protocol, identification of the three phases using dual-space correlation lengths, verification via expansion dynamics and RG theory, and discussion of micromotion and robustness.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The disordered quantum world hosts three fundamental types of states: extended, localized, and critical, of which the critical states are confined to fine-tuned critical points or mobility edges in randomly disordered systems. The tripartite phase, with all three types of states coexisting over finite spectral windows, represents a hallmark distinction between quasiperiodic and truly random systems in the localization physics. Here, we report the realization of this exotic phase in a quasi-periodically driven orbital optical lattice with ultracold atoms. The optical lattice with a quasiperiodic Floquet modulation coupling s and p orbitals is realized in experiment and shown to host the tripartite phase from exact theory. We develop a two-stage protocol to precisely prepare and detect the three types of quantum states. The characteristic exponents of these states are determined from expansion dynamics, showing their distinct universal transport properties. Our study marks a significant advancement in exploring unconventional critical phenomena and localization physics with ultracold atoms.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.20661"></a>
### [Entangling Power: A Probe of Symmetry and Integrability in Quantum Many-Body Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20661v1)

**Authors:** Ian Low, Pallab Goswami  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20661v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20661_figures/2605.20661_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Instantaneous ep ep(U(t)) of the two-site Hamiltonian (6) as a function of time at/π for the XXX (solid), Ising (dashed), XX (dash-dotted), and XYZ (dotted) models. (a) Spin-1/2 (two-qubit, d = 2). (b) Spin-1 (two-qutrit, d = 3). For the XYZ model we use (ax, ay, az) = (1, 0.7, 0.3). Thin dashed horizontal lines indicate the time-averaged values ep.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.20661_figures/2605.20661_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Time-averaged entangling power of the two-site spin- 1 XXZ model as a function of anisotropy ∆. The solid curve is the generic analytic formula, Eq. (18). At ∆= ±1 (SU(2) symmetry) and ∆= 0 (U(1) × Z2 symmetry), eigenvalue de- generacies result in the true ep (filled circles) dropping below the generic curve (open circles). Dashed lines indicate the magnitude of each dip.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.20661_figures/2605.20661_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Time-averaged entangling power of the spin-1/2 isospectral family, Eq. (29), as a function of q = C2(|z⟩)/2, where C is the concurrence of the projector state |z⟩. The product-state limit (q = 0, ep = 1/9) and the XXX/Bell-state limit (q = 1/2, ep = 1/12) are marked, together with the max- imum at q = 1/6. The XXX model sits at the minimum of ep over the isospectral family.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.20661_figures/2605.20661_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Time-averaged entangling power ep for the spin-1/2 XXZ chain as a function of anisotropy ∆. (a) L = 10 chain, shown on a scale to resolve the residual dip structure near saturation. (b) Overlay of L = 4 (solid blue), 6 (dash-dotted green), 8 (dashed purple), and 10 (solid red). The L = 2 case was already shown in Fig. 2. Root-of-unity fine structure at ∆= ±1/2 becomes visible for L ≥6.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.20661_figures/2605.20661_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5: Dense scan of ep for the spin-1/2 XXZ chain at L = 10 over ∆∈[0.25, 0.85], resolving all four root-of-unity dips. Vertical dashed lines mark the exact algebraic locations.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the ability of a quantum operator to generate entanglement can act as a probe for symmetry and integrability. By studying Heisenberg spin chains, the authors show that higher symmetry leads to a suppression of entangling power. The results bridge the gap between small-scale quantum gates and the thermodynamic limit of many-body systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a new operator-based diagnostic for detecting symmetry and integrability, which is highly relevant for characterizing the dynamics of programmable quantum simulators.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether the entangling power of a unitary operator can serve as a dynamical diagnostic for symmetry and integrability in quantum many-body systems.

**Main result.** The study establishes that increasing symmetry suppresses entangling power, showing sharp dips at SU(2) and free-fermion points in finite chains, while the two-magnon S-matrix shows vanishing entangling power at SU(2) points.

**Method.** The authors use an efficient algorithm for computing time-averaged entangling power via the diagonal ensemble, alongside analytical derivations using the operator-state correspondence and the Bethe Ansatz.

**Model / system.** Anisotropic Heisenberg (XXZ, XXX, XYZ) spin chains of various sizes (two-site, finite-size, and thermodynamic limit) and the two-magnon S-matrix.

**Key observables.** Time-averaged entangling power (e_p), linear entropy, and the S-matrix scattering amplitudes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Anisotropy parameter (Delta), spin magnitude (s=1/2, 1), system size (L), and rapidity (theta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes Haar-random product states for the definition of entangling power and focuses on integrable sectors of the XXZ model.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows instantaneous entangling power oscillations; Figure 3 illustrates the non-monotonic dependence of e_p on the isospectral parameter q; Figure 4 shows the scaling of the SU(2) dip with system size.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the entangling power concept, analyzes two-site models to establish a symmetry hierarchy, extends the study to finite-size XXZ chains, and concludes with the thermodynamic limit via the two-magnon S-matrix.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The entangling power of a unitary operator quantifies its ability to generate entanglement from product states and provides a natural probe of quantum many-body dynamics. Entanglement extremization at points of enhanced symmetry has previously been observed in high-energy scattering. In this work we compute the time-averaged entangling power of anisotropic Heisenberg spin chains across two-site models and finite-size systems, as well as the entangling power of the two-magnon $S$-matrix in the thermodynamic limit. For two-site models we establish a monotonic hierarchy: the entangling power decreases as the symmetry group grows, reaching its minimum at the $SU(2)$ XXX point. Finite-size XXZ chains exhibit sharp dips at the $SU(2)$ points $Δ=\pm 1$ and the free-fermion point $Δ=0$, with the free-fermion dip decaying much more slowly with system size. In the thermodynamic limit, we decompose the two-magnon $S$-matrix into quantum logic gates -- Identity, SWAP, and $σ_z\otimesσ_z$ -- and show that the entangling power vanishes for all scattering energies at the $SU(2)$ points, where the $S$-matrix reduces to the Identity gate, while the free-fermion point achieves the maximum -- the opposite of the finite-size many-body behavior. The entangling power can serve as an {\em operator} diagnostic for symmetry and selected aspects of integrability in quantum simulations of spin-chain dynamics.

</details>

### [Strongly-coupled non-Markovian waveguide QED with input-output HEOM](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20703v1)

**Summary.** This paper introduces the 'input-output Hierarchical Equations of Motion' (io-HEOM) to simulate strong-coupling dynamics in waveguide QED. It demonstrates how the method captures non-Markovian effects from both spatial non-locality and non-linear dispersion. The work is significant for its ability to model the dynamics of virtual photon release and persistent oscillations in structured environments.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful, unified numerical tool (io-HEOM) for studying non-Markovian waveguide QED in regimes where standard approximations fail, specifically addressing complex phenomena like photon-atom hybridization and Van Hove singularities.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Modeling a single qubit in a 1D waveguide beyond the standard Markovian and perturbative approximations, specifically addressing non-Markovianity arising from non-local coupling and non-linear dispersion.

**Main result.** The developed io-HEOM method accurately captures non-Markovian dynamics, including the release of virtual bound photons during a qubit frequency quench and persistent oscillations due to Van Hove singularities in cavity arrays.

**Method.** The input-output Hierarchical Equations of Motion (io-HEOM) framework, supplemented by the Prony method for correlation function fitting and Silbey-Harris polaron theory for analytical validation.

**Model / system.** A single qubit interacting with a 1D waveguide, including both continuous waveguides with linear dispersion and 1D cavity arrays with non-linear dispersion.

**Key observables.** Photon density/occupation, qubit population, spatial photon profile, and renormalized qubit frequency.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Strong-coupling and ultra-strong-coupling regimes, spectral density (Ohmic and Lorentzian), and non-local coupling functions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The multi-exponential ansatz for the bath correlation function is truncated at a finite hierarchy level, and the Prony method fits the correlation function only up to a fixed finite time.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a schematic of the two studied waveguide scenarios and the io-HEOM framework; Figure 2 shows photon density, qubit population, and steady-state profiles for an Ohmic waveguide; Figure 3 illustrates photon emission following a frequency quench; Figure 4 shows dynamics for a Lorentzian spectral density; Figure 5 demonstrates the Prony method for correlation function fitting; Figure 6 shows photon occupation and qubit dynamics in a cavity array.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the io-HEOM framework, applies it to a continuous waveguide with non-local coupling to study bound photons and photon release, extends the study to cavity arrays with non-linear dispersion, validates results using polaron theory, and discusses the numerical implementation via the Prony method.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider the problem of modeling a single qubit in contact with a one-dimensional waveguide beyond the standard perturbative and Markovian approximations. Using the recently developed input-output hierarchical equations of motion (io-HEOM), we investigate multiple examples of such waveguides, characterized by different spectral densities. Our examples highlight that the io-HEOM method can accurately capture non-Markovianity in waveguide QED from two distinct origins. The first source of non-Markovianity is spatially non-local coupling between the qubit and the waveguide. By examining two examples with non-local coupling, we show how the coupling function affects the steady-state bound photons, and demonstrate the release of these photons when the qubit energy is quenched. The second source of non-Markovianity is non-linear dispersion. We illustrate this scenario using the example of a cavity array with point-like coupling, where the non-linear dispersion leads to persistent oscillations due to Van Hove singularities in the spectral density.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.20656"></a>
### [Entanglement Growth from Structured Initial States in Many-Body Localized Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20656v1)

**Authors:** Chen Xu, Pengfei Zhang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20656v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20656_figures/2605.20656_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic illustration of our setup. We consider the random-field XXZ model. In particular, we focus on struc- tured initial states prepared by evolving a product state un- der a chaotic Hamiltonian HTh for a finite time T0, followed by a quench to the target Hamiltonian Ht. We then compute both the entanglement entropy and the Wehrl-R´enyi entropy.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.20656_figures/2605.20656_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. The evolution of the HCEE, nQEE, and WRE start- ing from the initial state prepared with T0 = 4.5, for a single realization of random fields. (a-b) Dynamics in the thermal- ized phase (W = 0.5) for system sizes L = 12 and L = 14, respectively. (c-d) Dynamics under the MBL Hamiltonian (W = 8) for system sizes L = 12 and L = 14, respectively.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.20656_figures/2605.20656_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. (a) The averaged local magnetization mav. and nearest-neighbor correlation Gav. in the initial state |ψini⟩for different preparation times T0 at system size L = 14. (b) A comparison between numerical results and the predictions of the generalized Gibbs ensemble and the Scrooge random-state ensemble for the net growth of the WRE at different T0. The dashed lines separate two distinct regimes of entanglement growth, as in Fig. 3.</sub>

</details>

The initial-state entanglement is tuned by varying T0. In the numerics, the saturation value is estimated by evolving to a long time T = 1012. The black dashed line indicates the result for Haar-random states, while the gray dot-dashed lines serve as guides to the eye, separating two distinct regimes.</sub>

</details>

<img src="2605.20656_figures/2605.20656_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. The total entanglement growth of both the HCEE and the WRE is shown for different Hamiltonians and initial states at system size L = 12, where we choose |ψ0⟩= | + x, +y, · · · , +x, +y⟩. Despite the oscillations observed in the MBL regime, the saturation values for both the MBL and thermal cases closely match those of Haar random states, as shown in the black dashed lines.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explains why entanglement growth in MBL systems can first increase and then decrease when starting from structured states. By analyzing Wehrl-Rényi entropy, the authors show that this behavior is tied to the relaxation of local magnetization and inter-site correlations. The findings demonstrate that the specific polarization of the initial state is a key driver of entanglement dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fine-grained understanding of how initial-state structure and local conserved quantities govern entanglement dynamics and complexity in non-equilibrium many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the physical origin of non-monotonic entanglement growth in Many-Body Localized (MBL) systems when using structured initial states.

**Main result.** The authors identify two dynamical regimes: the first is driven by the relaxation of Local Integrable of Motion (LIOM) magnetization, and the second is driven by inter-site correlations. They show that the non-monotonicity is linked to the polarization of the initial state, disappearing for x/y-direction product states.

**Method.** The study uses numerical simulations of the XXZ model and a phenomenological LIOM-based model, supplemented by a Generalized Gibbs Ensemble (GGE) and a 'Scrooge ensemble' approach to analyze entanglement dynamics.

**Model / system.** A 1D quantum spin-1/2 random-field XXZ model with open boundary conditions, including both the microscopic Hamiltonian and a phenomenological model based on Local Integrals of Motion (LIOMs).

**Key observables.** Rényi entanglement entropy (HCEE and nQEE), Wehrl-Rényi entropy (WRE), local magnetization, and nearest-neighbor correlations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Initial state preparation time (T0), disorder strength (W), and initial state polarization (z-direction vs. x/y-direction).

**Assumptions / limitations.** In the phenomenological model, higher-order many-body terms are neglected, and the LIOM operators are approximated by local spin operators.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the non-monotonic entanglement growth for z-polarized states and show that the distinct two-regime separation disappears for x/y-polarized states.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of non-monotonic entanglement, describes the model and initial state preparation, presents the analysis of entanglement growth regimes using WRE and EE, compares microscopic and phenomenological models, and concludes with implications and future directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Understanding how complex entanglement structures emerge is a central problem in quantum many-body physics. Recent work by Zhang et al. has considered structured initial states prepared by evolving a product state under a chaotic Hamiltonian for a finite time before quenching to the target Hamiltonian. In this setup, total entanglement entropy growth in many-body localized systems exhibits two distinct regimes, first increasing and then decreasing as the initial entanglement is tuned. In this work, we identify the physical origin of this behavior by analyzing the dynamics of both the Rényi entanglement entropy and the Wehrl-Rényi entropy in the random-field XXZ model, the latter of which characterizes multipartite entanglement. We show that a similar non-monotonic dependence on the initial entanglement also appears in the net growth of the Wehrl-Rényi entropy for product states polarized along the $z$-direction. The first regime is governed by a finite magnetization associated with local integrals of motion, while the second reflects inter-site correlations. In contrast, for product states in the $x/y$-direction, the entanglement growth exhibits a monotonic decay. Our results provide a more fine-grained picture of how distinct initial-state properties shape entanglement dynamics in many-body localized systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.20995"></a>
### [Wasserstein Space of Quantum Chaos](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20995v1)

**Authors:** Koji Hashimoto, Norihiro Tanahashi, Kentaroh Yoshida  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20995v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20995_figures/2605.20995_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. The time evolution of the microcanonical out-of-time-order correlators (OTOCs) for the double-well case (a) and the partly flattened oscillator case (b). In both cases (a) and (b), the red lines grow exponentially in time, which is the scrambling phenomenon. For (a), the plotted one is for n = 0 (blue), n = 2 (green), n = 5 (red) and n = 9 (black), where n = 0, 1, 2, · · · labels the energy level of even parity states counted from the ground state. The n = 5 state has E = 0.088, which is close to the top of the potential hill. For (b), the plotted one is for n = 0 (blue), n = 2 (green), n = 4 (red) and n = 9 (black). The n = 4 state has E = 13.07, which is close to the plateau...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.20995_figures/2605.20995_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. One-dimensional potentials used in this section: harmonic, double-well, and partly flattened oscillator.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.20995_figures/2605.20995_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Husimi Q-representation for 16 energy eigenstates: harmonic oscillator. From top-left to bottom right, the energy grows, and we labels them as n = 0, 1, · · · , 15.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.20995_figures/2605.20995_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. Husimi Q-representation for 16 energy eigenstates: double-well.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.20995_figures/2605.20995_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. Husimi Q-representation for 16 energy eigenstates: partly flattened oscillator.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the geometry of the Wasserstein space of quantum states undergoes a dimensional reduction as a system becomes chaotic. By showing that quantum scrambling induces a folding structure in this space, the authors propose a new diagnostic for chaos and support the idea that holography emerges from such dimensional reduction.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a new geometric language (optimal transport) to unify diverse phenomena like quantum scars, Lyapunov exponents, and scrambling, while offering a potential geometric mechanism for the emergence of holographic spacetime.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the relationship between quantum chaos, quantum scrambling, and the geometric structure of the Wasserstein space of energy eigenstates.

**Main result.** The effective dimension of the Wasserstein space decreases as a quantum system becomes more chaotic, a process driven by a 'folding' structure induced by quantum scrambling.

**Method.** The authors use Sinkhorn-regularized optimal transport on Husimi Q-representations and apply the Gram-spectrum method with Multidimensional Scaling (MGS) to embed the Wasserstein distance into Euclidean space.

**Model / system.** The study utilizes several models: 1D potentials (harmonic oscillator, double-well, and partly flattened oscillator) and a 2D coupled harmonic oscillator with a quartic mixing term controlled by a coupling constant g.

**Key observables.** Effective dimension of Wasserstein space, Out-of-Time-Ordered Correlators (OTOC), Lyapunov exponent, and Gram-matrix eigenvalue decay constant B.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling constant g (controlling integrability to chaos), Sinkhorn regularization parameter epsilon, and phase-space grid resolution.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The results are based on truncated harmonic oscillator bases and discretized phase-space grids; the authors note that higher energy levels may introduce more complex non-Euclidean geometry.

**Figures summary.** Figures show OTOC growth (scrambling), potential energy profiles, Husimi Q-representations in phase space, Wasserstein distance heatmaps, and 2D MDS embeddings showing line vs. folded structures.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of dimensional reduction, presents 1D models to demonstrate scrambling-induced folding, analyzes the 2D coupled oscillator to show chaotic dimensional reduction, and concludes with a discussion on the holographic manifold hypothesis and numerical robustness.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We find that the effective dimension of the Wasserstein space of energy eigenstates decreases as a quantum system becomes more chaotic. To demonstrate this, we study a quantum coupled harmonic oscillator system using Husimi Q-representations, to which Sinkhorn-regularized optimal transport is applied to construct an embedding geometry via the Gram-spectrum method. We also demonstrate that exponential OTOC growth, referred to here as quantum scrambling even in the absence of chaos, induces a folding structure in the emergent Wasserstein space, which may underlie the chaotic reduction of the Wasserstein dimension. At the separatrix (the scrambling point) of the inverted harmonic oscillator, the Wasserstein distance correctly captures the Lyapunov exponent. Furthermore, we discover that a branching structure in the Wasserstein space signals quantum scar states within the chaotic sea of phase space. Our optimal transport approach thus provides a new diagnostic for quantum chaos, quantum scrambling, quantum scars, and quantum Lyapunov exponents. The observed chaotic dimensional reduction also supports the recent conjecture [arXiv:2604.17649] that the Wasserstein space serves as an emergent holographic space through the manifold hypothesis, since chaoticity is a characteristic signature of black holes in holography.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.20902"></a>

**Summary.** The researchers successfully demonstrated Coherent Feedback Cooling of a mechanical membrane at room temperature, achieving a massive reduction in phonon number. This approach overcomes the limitations of standard laser cooling in the sideband-unresolved regime and provides a roadmap for reaching the quantum ground state without cryogenics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems as it demonstrates a practical implementation of coherent feedback control to manipulate the steady-state of a macroscopic mechanical mode, bypassing the limits of measurement-based feedback.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of cooling macroscopic mechanical resonators to the quantum regime at room temperature, specifically overcoming the fundamental limits of dynamical backaction cooling in the sideband-unresolved regime.

**Main result.** Experimental demonstration of Coherent Feedback Cooling (CFC) on an ultracoherent membrane at room temperature, achieving a phonon occupation reduction from 5.5e6 to 166 and an effective temperature of 9.1 mK.

**Method.** An all-optical coherent feedback scheme where the optical probe field is re-injected after an engineered delay and displacement to provide a feedback force proportional to mechanical momentum.

**Model / system.** An optomechanical system featuring a high-Q silicon nitride density phononic-crystal membrane in a Fabry-Perot cavity, involving two optical polarization modes (horizontal and vertical) due to birefringence.

**Key observables.** Phonon occupation number, effective temperature, mechanical position power spectral density (PSD), and phase-space uncertainty area.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mechanical frequency of 1.14 MHz, quality factor of 1.1e8, cavity linewidth of 3.7 MHz, and a cooling factor of 3.3e4.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is treated in the linearized regime; performance is currently limited by technical noise (laser-lock instability, frequency fluctuations) and in-loop optical losses.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the experimental schematic and phase-space evolution; Fig 3 shows numerical phonon occupation vs. delay/linewidth; Fig 6 shows predicted performance gains from upgrading coupling strength and reducing noise.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of room-temperature quantum optomechanics, describes the experimental setup and CFC protocol, presents the experimental results for phonon reduction, discusses the impact of technical noise, and concludes with a predictive model for future system upgrades.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Optomechanical systems provide a versatile platform for precision measurements and investigations of fundamental physics, where bringing macroscopic resonators into the quantum regime is a widely pursued goal. Achieving such quantum behavior of solid-state mechanical resonators at room temperature would greatly broaden their applications by removing the need for cryogenic environments. Reaching this goal requires efficient cooling of mechanical motion, among various laser cooling methods, dynamical backaction cooling (DBC) is widely utilized in experiments but fundamentally limited when operating in the sideband-unresolved regime. Coherent feedback cooling (CFC) can overcome this limitation, while avoiding state collapse and the electronic restrictions inherent to measurement-based feedback. Here, we experimentally demonstrate CFC using an ultracoherent density phononic crystal membrane. By combining CFC with strong DBC in a relatively narrow cavity, we achieve a phonon occupation reduction from $5.5\times10^{6}$ to $166\pm7$, corresponding to a cooling factor of $3.3\times10^{4}$ at room temperature, even with current experimental limitations. Our results show the potential of CFC for approaching the ground state of high-$Q$ membranes at room temperature.

</details>

### [WSi weak link element with a non-sinusoidal current-phase relation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20652v1)

**Summary.** This paper investigates the nonlinear properties of a tungsten silicide nanowire used as a weak link in a superconducting RF-SQUID. It demonstrates that the device follows a non-sinusoidal current-phase relation and shows that metastable flux states can be extremely long-lived. These findings suggest WSi is a promising material for developing new types of nonlinear superconducting quantum elements.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and quantum optics because it explores the control of decoherence and relaxation in highly nonlinear, metastable quantum states and demonstrates a platform for engineering non-standard energy-phase relationships.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to characterize the nonlinear behavior and current-phase relation (CPR) of a tungsten silicide (WSi) nanowire weak link to determine if it is better described by a non-sinusoidal Josephson junction model or a quantum phase slip (QPS) model.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrated that the WSi weak link exhibits a non-sinusoidal, sawtooth-like CPR and found that both the skewed Josephson junction and QPS models can describe low-energy spectra, though differences emerge at higher energy levels. They also measured long-lived (up to one hour) metastable flux states.

**Method.** The study uses microwave spectroscopy and single-shot readout of flux-dependent resonance frequencies and relaxation times. Theoretical modeling involves circuit quantization, symplectic geometry for the QPS model, and the Born-Oppenheimer approximation.

**Model / system.** The physical system is an RF-SQUID containing a WSi nanowire weak link embedded in a three-dimensional copper cavity. The models considered are a Josephson junction with a skewed energy-phase relation and a quantum phase slip element acting as the electromagnetic dual.

**Key observables.** Resonance frequency (f) as a function of external magnetic flux, transmission amplitude (|S21|), and relaxation times of metastable persistent-current states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Inductance ratio (beta), external magnetic flux (Phi_ext), skewness parameter (chi), kinetic inductance (Lk), and energy scales such as charging energy (Ec) and junction energy (Ej).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study employs a semiclassical approximation for resonance frequency calculations and uses the Born-Oppenheimer approximation to treat high-energy modes as decoupled.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the device schematic and potential landscapes; Figure 2 and 3 present the experimental setup, device images, and flux-dependent resonance frequency data showing hysteresis; Figure 4 and 8 compare JJ and QPS models and energy spectra; Figure 9 shows the circuit models and numerical energy spectrum comparisons.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the need for nonlinearity in superconducting circuits, describes the fabrication and setup of the WSi RF-SQUID, presents experimental measurements of resonance frequency and hysteresis, compares the data against JJ and QPS theoretical models, and concludes with the measurement of metastable state lifetimes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Nonlinearity is an essential ingredient for encoding quantum states with non-uniform energy spacing, implementing coherent quantum gates, reading out qubits, amplifying, and mixing electromagnetic signals. In this work, we demonstrate the nonlinear behavior of a constriction fabricated from an amorphous, high-kinetic inductance material, tungsten silicide, embedded in a three-dimensional RF-SQUID. We find that the results are consistent with the weak link behaving as a Josephson junction with a sawtooth-like current-phase relation or a quantum phase slip element. Finally, we measure relaxation times of the metastable, persistent-current states trapped in the local minima of the potential.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.21245"></a>
### [Boundary Geometry Turns Entanglement into Steering](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21245v1)

**Authors:** Yu-Xuan Zhang, Jing-Ling Chen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21245v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21245_figures/2605.21245_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Boundary contact and quadratic inward defect. (a) Product-null boundary contact at N, with shrinking caps Γt. (b) The normalized inward defect δ(u) is bounded by a quadratic curve Cu2 for a representative product-null fam- ily.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21245_figures/2605.21245_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Minimal boundary witness. (a) The boundary data consist of the product-null population p0, the auxiliary popu- lation p1, and the tangential coherence Ctan. (b) The witness Wbd = |Ctan|2 −p0p1 detects the negative partial-transpose minor. At the product-null boundary, the theorem upgrades this NPT witness to two-way projective steering.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper demonstrates that for certain classes of quantum states, entanglement and EPR steering are equivalent due to a geometric scaling obstruction at the state space boundary. It provides a practical experimental witness for steering by measuring tangential coherence in states where the conditional state touches the Bloch sphere boundary.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for quantum information and quantum optics as it provides a new geometric way to certify steering and provides a method to upgrade entanglement witnesses into steering certificates using only local boundary data.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the conditions under which the gap between entanglement and Einstein-Podolsky-Rosen (EPR) steering closes, specifically looking for a mechanism where entanglement implies steering.

**Main result.** The authors identify a boundary-geometric mechanism where, on the product-null boundary of the state space, entanglement is equivalent to two-way projective steering. This collapse of the hierarchy applies to all entangled two-qubit rank-two states and certain rank-three states.

**Method.** The study uses boundary-scaling analysis of conditional states near the Bloch-sphere boundary and a mathematical proof involving the Radon-Nikodym theorem to demonstrate a scaling obstruction to Local Hidden State (LHS) models.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on bipartite quantum systems (specifically two-qubit states and general cuts X|Y) involving an untrusted party (Alice) and a trusted party (Bob), modeled using projective assemblages and Local Hidden State (LHS) models.

**Key observables.** Tangential coherence (h02 or b_t), inward defect (delta), and the boundary witness (W_bd).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The product-null boundary regime, where the kernel of the density matrix contains a product vector, and the scaling regime defined by first-order tangential displacement and second-order inward defect.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary results rely on the specific geometric condition of conditional states touching the boundary of the Bloch ball (product-null contact) and assume Alice's side is restricted to local unitaries.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates boundary contact and the quadratic inward defect of conditional states; Figure 2 shows the boundary data (populations and coherence) and the boundary witness used to detect the NPT minor.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the gap between entanglement and steering, introduces the product-null boundary geometry, provides a mathematical proof of the scaling obstruction to LHS models, extends the results to higher-dimensional/multipartite cuts, and concludes with an experimental witness.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Entanglement does not in general imply Einstein-Podolsky-Rosen steering. We identify a boundary-geometric mechanism that closes this gap on product-null boundary strata of two-qubit state space, where Bob's conditional states touch the boundary of the Bloch ball. The key obstruction is local: if a projective assemblage approaches a Bloch-sphere boundary contact with a first-order tangential displacement but only a second-order inward defect, then no finite-measure local-hidden-state model can reproduce it. For two-qubit states with a product vector in the kernel, this boundary contact is exactly the tangency of Bob's steering ellipsoid to the Bloch sphere. At such a product-null tangency, a single tangential coherence controls both partial-transpose negativity and the boundary-contact scaling obstruction. The same boundary minor gives a compact experimental witness: once the product-null contact is verified or guaranteed, the tangential coherence supplies the steering signal. Consequently, every entangled two-qubit rank-two state, and every entangled rank-three state whose null space is spanned by a product vector, is two-way projectively steerable. The same boundary idea extends to arbitrary steering cuts: the Bloch-sphere contact is replaced by a rank-deficient trusted conditional state, and support-kernel first-order coherence implies both NPT entanglement and projective steering.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21206"></a>
### [Velocity-Controlled Directional Readout of Single Photons](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21206v1)

**Authors:** Mohamed Hatifi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21206v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `interference shaping light` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21206_figures/2605.21206_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Moving-detector geometry. A detector moving along +x with velocity v = βc probes two counterpropagating single-photon modes of the same laboratory frequency ω. The right-propagating blue mode is annihilated by ˆa+ and carries wave vector +k, while the left-propagating red mode is annihilated by ˆa−and carries wave vector −k. Although the detector is purely electric in its rest frame, its laboratory-frame response mixes electric and magnetic ampli- tudes and samples the two modes at the Doppler-shifted frequencies Ω± = γ(1 ∓β)ω. This is the origin of the velocity-dependent pho- todetection POVM derived below.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21206_figures/2605.21206_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Moving-detector photodetection and Doppler- enhanced directional readout. (a) Broadband limit. A detec- tor that is purely electric in its rest frame realizes, in the labora- tory frame, a velocity-fixed electric–magnetic readout. The visi- bility Vbroad decreases as the propagation bias |Bbroad| increases, while the ideal click effect satisfies V2 + B2 = 1. (b) Narrowband mechanism. A Lorentzian detector tuned near one Doppler-shifted component samples the two counterpropagating alternatives at dif- ferent detector-frame frequencies, Ω+ and Ω−. Propagation selec- tivity appears once the Doppler splitting resolves the linewidth. (c) Q-enhanced crossover. For a Lorentzian detector...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21206_figures/2605.21206_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Observed visibility in the presence of spectral selec- tivity and finite-time integration. The color map shows the visi- bility Vobs as a function of two dimensionless control parameters: the spectral-selectivity parameter βQ, proportional to the Doppler splitting in linewidth units, and the time-averaging parameter βωT, which measures the accumulated Doppler-beat phase during the de- tection window. The vertical dashed line marks the narrowband onset βQ ≃1/4 for a branch-tuned Lorentzian response, where the detec- tor begins to resolve the two propagation alternatives spectrally. The horizontal dashed line marks βωT ≃1, where finite-time integration begins to wash out the Doppler...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper shows that moving a detector relative to a photon can change the type of information extracted from a single-photon state. By using a narrowband detector, one can use the Doppler shift to switch from measuring interference to measuring propagation direction. This effect is operational and does not require decohering the photon itself.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel way to control measurement axes on a propagation qubit using kinematic motion and spectral selectivity, which is highly relevant for engineered quantum platforms like cQED and optomechanics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how the uniform motion of a detector relative to an optical apparatus changes the measurement-theoretic properties (POVM) of single-photon detection, specifically the transition from phase-sensitive to direction-sensitive readout.

**Main result.** The paper demonstrates that detector motion, combined with finite spectral bandwidth, can transform a phase-sensitive measurement into a direction-sensitive one without decohering the photon, with the transition being enhanced by the detector's quality factor.

**Method.** The author uses the Glauber formulation of photodetection, Lorentz transformations of electromagnetic fields, and the theory of Positive-Operator-Valued Measures (POVM) to derive the single-click effect density.

**Model / system.** A single-photon state in two counterpropagating modes (+ and -) interacting with an electric Glauber detector moving at a constant velocity v along the x-axis, modeled with a Lorentzian spectral response.

**Key observables.** Instantaneous Fringe Visibility (V_beta), Directional Bias (B_beta), and Doppler-beat frequency (Delta_Omega).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Normalized velocity (beta), detector Quality Factor (Q), relative phase (phi), and the integration time window (T).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The detector is treated as having a Lorentzian susceptibility; the motion is assumed to be uniform; the analysis considers both broadband and narrowband limits.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the geometry of a detector moving along the x-axis probing two counterpropagating modes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of detector motion, establishes the Lorentz-transformed field model, derives the POVM for the moving detector, analyzes the broadband and narrowband (Q-enhanced) regimes, and discusses the effects of finite-time integration.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Photodetection is usually treated in the frame in which the detector is at rest relative to the optical apparatus. We show that uniform motion of an electric Glauber detector changes the single-click POVM realized on two counterpropagating single-photon modes. Motion Doppler-shifts the alternatives in the detector frame; finite bandwidth then converts propagation direction into a detection bias without decohering the photon. For a Lorentzian response near one Doppler branch, the readout crosses from phase-sensitive to direction-sensitive with a quality-factor-enhanced onset. Finite-time integration adds Doppler-beat visibility loss, separating passive covariance from measurement change.

</details>
