**Summary.** This paper introduces a new theoretical framework to study dynamics on ultra small-world networks by accounting for the degree correlations caused by structural cut-offs. By mapping observed degrees to hidden degrees, the authors successfully predict species survival and stability in highly heterogeneous networks, showing much better agreement with simulations and real-world data than previous models.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Standard Heterogeneous Dynamical Mean-Field Theory (HDMFT) fails in 'ultra small-world' networks because it cannot account for the structural cut-offs and degree correlations (disassortativity) that arise in highly heterogeneous, finite-size networks.

**Main result.** The authors developed a framework using a Soft Configurational Model to map observed degrees to 'hidden degrees,' allowing for accurate prediction of species survival rates and stability in highly heterogeneous networks.

**Method.** The study employs Heterogeneous Dynamical Mean-Field Theory (HDMFT) combined with a maximum entropy ensemble (Soft Configurational Model) and generating functional analysis to derive effective single-species stochastic dynamics.

**Model / system.** The system is a Generalized Lotka-Volterra (GLV) model on highly heterogeneous, power-law distributed networks, where species interactions are stochastic and include competition or mutualism.

**Key observables.** Species survival rates, species abundance distributions, and linear stability of the equilibrium solutions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Power-law exponent (alpha), mean interaction strength (mu), disorder strength (sigma), reciprocity (gamma), and average connectivity (C).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theory assumes a sufficiently large average degree (C) to neglect higher-order terms in 1/C and uses a mapping between hidden and observed degrees to handle structural constraints.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows how structural constraints force negative assortativity; Figure 2 compares survival rate predictions across different joint distribution assumptions; Figure 3 validates the theory against real-world networks; Figure 4 identifies critical system size for stability; Figure 5 demonstrates numerical convergence of the theory.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the failure of existing HDMFT in ultra small-world regimes, proposes a new framework using hidden degrees via the Soft Configurational Model, derives the effective single-species dynamics and stability conditions, and validates the approach using both simulated power-law networks and empirical real-world networks.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Despite the knowledge that social, economical, and ecological networks are often of a small-world nature with inter-nodal distance growing even slower than logarithmically with system size, we often assume theoretical systems to be outside of this regime, to make them easier to treat analytically. Here we derive a framework to apply the powerful dynamical mean-field theory on highly heterogeneous networks that is able to account for more of the degree correlations naturally arising from network constraints, known as structural cut-offs. We apply this framework to the well-studied and understood disordered Lotka-Volterra model, and show typically reported observables such as survival rates and stability for these systems on ultra small-world networks. We find much better agreement for these variables for all ranges of exponents for simulated power-law networks as well as empirically sourced networks.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.20979"></a>
### [Equilibrium and dynamics of a three-state opinion model on a network of networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20979v1)

**Authors:** Irene Ferri, Albert Díaz-Guilera, Hiroki Sayama  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20979v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20979_figures/2605.20979_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Representation of four agents with a 4-node ring- shaped internal belief topology and socially connected form- ing a star. Modified from [8].</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.20979_figures/2605.20979_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Illustration of the three network topologies used to model belief systems with a number of internal beliefs c = 10.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.20979_figures/2605.20979_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Contribution of a pair of interacting agents to the energy of the system.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.20979_figures/2605.20979_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Magnetization curves for a particular belief as a function of temperature for each internal topology and different numbers of internal beliefs. Results obtained with MC simulations for a system of N = 104 agents, neutrality parameter α = 0, averaged over 100 repetitions with c · 104 MC steps. Comparisons with MFA results are shown for the clique and the ring.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.20979_figures/2605.20979_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Critical temperature versus number of beliefs when α = 0 for the clique (blue circles), the ring (red crosses), and the star-like (green dots) agent types. Results obtained averaging 100 repetitions with systems of N = 104 agents.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores a multi-scale opinion model where agents possess their own internal networks of beliefs. It demonstrates that the internal arrangement of these beliefs (e.g., star vs. ring) fundamentally changes how social consensus or polarization emerges at different temperatures and neutrality levels.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how the internal topology of an individual's belief system (the organization of interconnected beliefs) influences collective opinion formation, social consensus, and phase transitions in a network of networks.

**Main result.** The critical temperature for consensus destabilization increases with the number of beliefs in star-like agents but saturates in ring and clique topologies; additionally, mixing different agent topologies leads to regime-dependent dominance where rings act as catalysts for extremist convergence.

**Method.** The study employs Mean-Field Approximation (MFA) to analytically derive order parameters and free energy, complemented by Metropolis Monte Carlo simulations to study zero-temperature dynamics and finite-temperature phase transitions.

**Model / system.** A three-state (positive, negative, neutral) opinion model on a multilayer graph where each agent has an internal belief graph (clique, ring, or star) and interacts with others via an external social graph. The Hamiltonian includes terms for social homophily and internal belief coherence, incorporating temperature to represent social agitation and cognitive dissonance.

**Key observables.** Net polarization (magnetization m), neutral fraction (n0), internal magnetization (M_int), internal energy (E_int), and critical temperature (Tc).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Neutrality parameter (alpha) controlling the weight of the neutral state, temperature (T) representing social agitation/dissonance, and the number of internal beliefs (c).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The external social network is assumed to be a fixed, fully connected graph; for star-like agents, the identity of the central hub belief is assumed to be the same across all agents.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 visualizes agent belief topologies; Figure 2 shows the three internal network structures; Figure 3 illustrates energy contributions; Figure 4 shows order parameters vs. temperature; Figure 6 compares magnetization curves between MC and MFA; Figure 10 shows magnetization and neutrality distributions in mixed populations.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the network-of-networks model, defines the Hamiltonian and topologies, presents analytical Mean-Field results, validates them with Monte Carlo simulations, explores the effects of increasing belief count, analyzes zero-temperature dynamics/steady states, and concludes with the study of mixed-topology populations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Opinion formation models typically represent each individual as a single variable. However, in practice each individual holds interconnected beliefs whose internal organization may influence collective outcomes. To explore this dependence, we study a three-state opinion model on a network of networks in which each agent has an internal belief graph and interacts with other agents through an external social graph. Each belief can take two opposite polarized states or a neutral one and a neutrality parameter tunes the relative conviction of the neutral stance. We incorporate temperature into the model to account for external social agitation and for the tolerance of internal cognitive dissonance. We explore the stationary state and dynamics of the model using analytical approaches and Monte Carlo simulations on a fully connected external social graph, with internal belief topologies given by one-dimensional chains, cliques, and star-like structures, where there is a central core belief to which all other beliefs are connected. We find that the critical temperature at which the polarized consensus destabilizes increases with the addition of more beliefs to star-like agents but saturates in the case of ring- and clique-like internal topologies. We also consider binary mixtures of agents with different internal topologies in equal proportions, showing that the interplay between agents is regime-dependent, with the dominant topology depending on the value of the neutrality parameter.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21355"></a>
### [Essentially singular limits of Jacobi operators and applications to higher-order squeezing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21355v1)

**Summary.** The paper studies how Jacobi operators behave in a singular limit where the limiting operator is not essentially self-adjoint. It demonstrates that the specific way the limit is approached determines which self-adjoint extension is realized, providing a mathematical foundation for understanding higher-order squeezing in quantum optics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for quantum optics and open quantum systems because it addresses the fundamental ambiguity in defining the dynamics of non-self-adjoint squeezing operators when the regularization term vanishes, showing that symmetry plays a key role in selecting physical extensions.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the 'essentially singular' limit of a family of Jacobi operators as a coupling parameter approaches zero, specifically when the limiting operator admits multiple self-adjoint extensions.

**Main result.** The authors prove that the strong resolvent limit depends on the chosen sequence of the coupling parameter, meaning different extensions can be reached via different sequences; in the context of quantum optics, this implies the limit does not pick a unique extension but identifies a subclass compatible with symmetry.

**Method.** The study employs discrete WKB methods, Airy-function asymptotics, and the analysis of the Weyl m-function to derive uniform bounds for eigenvectors and establish strong resolvent convergence.

**Model / system.** The mathematical model is a family of Jacobi operators on l2(N) with a scaling parameter lambda. The physical application involves higher-order squeezing operators in quantum optics, specifically regularized Hamiltonians of the form Ak,h(K) = (a_dagger)^k + K(a_dagger a)^h + a^k.

**Key observables.** Wigner functions of the squeezed vacuum state and the spectral properties (eigenvalues and resolvents) of the operators.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The coupling parameter lambda (or K) representing the strength of the diagonal/free-field term, the order of squeezing k, and the power of the number operator h.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The results rely on specific growth conditions for the Jacobi coefficients (a_n and b_n) and assume smoothness and analyticity for the construction of approximate solutions via the Langer transform.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a numerical plot of the Weyl m-function at a complex point, illustrating how the values spiral around a limit circle as lambda approaches zero.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the mathematical framework for the essentially singular limit of Jacobi operators, provides technical proofs for eigenvector bounds using WKB and Airy asymptotics, and then applies these results to the selection of self-adjoint extensions in higher-order squeezing Hamiltonians.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study a family of Jacobi operators in which the diagonal entries are multiplied by a coupling parameter $λ\geq0$. Under suitable conditions, the operator is self-adjoint for every $λ>0$, while the formal limit at $λ=0$ is a symmetric Jacobi operator admitting a one-parameter family of self-adjoint extensions. A central ingredient of our analysis is the derivation of uniform bounds for square-summable generalized eigenvectors in the small-$λ$ regime, which combines discrete WKB methods with Airy-function asymptotics. Using these estimates, we analyze the limiting behavior $λ\to0$ in the strong resolvent sense, proving that for every sequence $λ_j\to0$ one can extract a subsequence along which the corresponding Jacobi operators converge to some self-adjoint extension of the limiting operator; conversely, every such extension can be obtained in this way. We call this behavior an essentially singular limit, by analogy with essential singularities in complex analysis.   As an application, we study higher-order squeezing operators arising in quantum optics. Using the connection with Jacobi operators, we show that when the relative strength of the free-field term tends to zero, different self-adjoint extensions of the squeezing operator are selected along different sequences. In particular, this limit does not single out a physically distinguished self-adjoint extension, but instead identifies a distinguished subclass of extensions compatible with the underlying symmetry.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper shows that quantum machine learning can outperform classical measurement-based strategies even with 30 to 40 noisy qubits. It demonstrates that the primary bottleneck for classical methods is the massive amount of data acquisition required, making quantum advantage accessible on near-term hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper provides a rigorous framework for predicting the onset of quantum advantage in noisy, near-term devices, which is critical for designing experiments in open quantum systems and evaluating the utility of NISQ-era hardware.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining whether the theoretical quantum advantage in Quantum Machine Learning (QML) persists at finite scales and in the presence of realistic hardware noise.

**Main result.** Demonstrated a clear performance separation between Fully Quantum and Measure-First protocols at a scale of 30 to 40 noisy qubits, where classical data acquisition would require months or years to match quantum performance.

**Method.** A scalable simulation pipeline for Fully Quantum protocols under hardware constraints, combined with a robust finite-size scaling extrapolation framework to predict large-scale performance.

**Model / system.** A learning task involving n-qubit binary phase-states subject to various noise channels including dephasing, depolarizing, and thermal relaxation, modeled across different hardware architectures (all-to-all and square-lattice connectivity).

**Key observables.** Sample complexity (number of state copies required), accuracy, and the visibility of the interference pattern.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Qubit number (n_q), Hamming weight of the target state (|alpha|), preparation noise rate (epsilon_p), and hardware-specific error rates.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study uses a specific learning problem known to exhibit asymptotic advantage and assumes a phenomenological 1/n_q scaling for the extrapolation of classical resource requirements.

**Figures summary.** Schematics of FQ vs MF frameworks, scaling plots of state copies vs qubit number, accuracy curves under different noise channels, and validation plots for the extrapolation model.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the QML advantage problem, description of the FQ and MF protocols, detailed noise and hardware modeling, simulation methodology, results for different noise channels, and a rigorous statistical validation of the extrapolation technique.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Learning problems involving quantum data are natural candidates for demonstrating an advantage in quantum machine learning. Recent results indicate that, for certain tasks and under noiseless conditions, coherent processing of quantum data outperforms fixed-measurement schemes followed by classical processing. It remained uncertain whether this performance gap persists at a finite scale, and in the presence of noise that is unavoidable with current quantum devices. In this work, we present simulations and analysis of the performance of existing hardware on a learning problem known to exhibit asymptotic advantage, now subjected to noisy quantum data. Comparing coherent quantum processing directly against fixed-measurement schemes, our results demonstrate a clear performance separation at a scale of just 30 to 40 noisy qubits. Already at this scale, the fundamental bottleneck is no longer classical computation but data acquisition; matching the noisy coherent protocol with measure-first strategies would still require months or even years of measurements. By systematically evaluating hardware constraints such as state preparation, gate errors, readout errors, connectivity, and coherence times, we provide evidence that a demonstration of such a strong learning advantage is accessible on near-term devices.

</details>

**Summary.** The study investigates how encoding multiple qubits into a single photon can maximize the secret key extracted per photon in satellite-based quantum communications. It finds that high-dimensional encoding significantly improves robustness against background noise and that optimal performance requires a finite, non-vanishing signal strength.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems researchers as it addresses the optimization of quantum communication protocols under realistic decoherence (dephasing and depolarization) and high-loss environments.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing the photon efficiency of entanglement-based Quantum Key Distribution (QKD) in photon-starved regimes, such as satellite-to-ground communications.

**Main result.** High-dimensional encoding (multiple qubits per photon) can increase the secret key rate by up to an order of magnitude, and the optimal efficiency is achieved at a finite photon pair production probability.

**Method.** Analytical derivation using the Lambert W function and numerical optimization of the secret key rate and Photon Key Efficiency (PKE) over source intensity and encoding order.

**Model / system.** Entanglement-based QKD protocols (BBM92 and SARG04) using temporal-slot encoding in a satellite-to-ground/free-space channel characterized by high loss and background radiation.

**Key observables.** Photon Key Efficiency (PKE), Quantum Bit Error Rate (QBER), and secret key rate.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of encoded qubits (m), pair production probability (p_pair), noise-to-transmission ratio (n/eta), and channel visibility (V).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Symmetric channels, low-noise regime (n/eta << 1), high-visibility measurements, and the use of the BBM92/SARG04 protocols.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the temporal-slot encoding mechanism; Figure 2 shows optimal PKE and pair production probability as functions of noise; Figure 3 compares PKE and QBER for dephasing and depolarizing channels; Figure 4 shows PKE scaling.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of photon efficiency in satellite QKD, describes the high-dimensional temporal encoding scheme, presents the mathematical framework for calculating key rates under noise, provides analytical approximations for the low-noise regime, and concludes with numerical results and scaling laws.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate entanglement-based quantum key distribution protocols, with particular emphasis on their efficiency under realistic conditions of satellite quantum communications, where performance is limited by the low power of a received signal and background radiation. We focus on scenarios where each photon pair is used to encode multiple qubits in order to optimally utilize the weak signal. By optimizing over the source intensity and the number of encoded qubits we study the theoretical information limit for the QKD efficiency. We show that the optimal efficiency is attained for finite entangled photons pair production probability which is in contrast to conventional communication efficiency maximized in the limit of vanishing signal strength. The multiqubit encoding can enhance the secret key rate by up to an order of magnitude compared to single-qubit schemes.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper uses a combination of Quantum Monte Carlo and mean-field methods to study how strain drives a phase transition in twisted bilayer graphene. It specifically explores the emergence of the incommensurate Kekulé spiral state and how many-body effects influence its stability. The work provides a way to study complex phase diagrams in moiré systems despite the presence of the fermion sign problem.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the strain-induced phase transition from the Kramers intervalley coherent (KIVC) state to the incommensurate Kekulé spiral (IKS) state in twisted bilayer graphene at filling factor nu = +/- 2.

**Main result.** The study identifies a strain-tuned transition from KIVC to IKS order and demonstrates that the IKS state is a topologically constrained, intermediate-coupling phase, though many-body fluctuations may prevent long-range IKS order in the QMC simulations.

**Method.** A combined computational protocol using continuous field momentum-space Quantum Monte Carlo (QMC), Exact Diagonalization (ED), and Hartree-Fock (HF) mean-field theory.

**Model / system.** Twisted bilayer graphene (TBG) at the magic angle (theta approx 1.05-1.15 degrees) subject to uniaxial heterostrain, modeled using the interacting Bistritzer-MacDonald (BM) continuum model.

**Key observables.** Electron occupation number distribution n(k), intervalley structure factors (S_KIVC and S_IKS), nesting diagnostics (O' and O''), and single-particle Green's functions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Twist angle theta = 1.15 degrees, filling factor nu = -2, strain strength epsilon_s (0% to 0.6%), and interlayer hopping parameters u0 = 80 meV, u1 = 110 meV.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The QMC method uses an approximation where the sampling weight is replaced by its absolute magnitude to mitigate the fermion sign problem; the model is projected onto the two central low-energy bands.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the effect of strain on Brillouin zones and Dirac cones; Figure 2 and 3 compare occupation numbers and nesting diagnostics between QMC and HF for different strain levels; Figure 4 shows the intervalley structure factor and the absence of Bragg peaks in QMC.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of the TBG phase diagram, describes the computational protocol (QMC, ED, HF), validates the approximate QMC method, presents the results of the strain-induced KIVC-IKS transition, and discusses the implications of many-body fluctuations on long-range order.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The understanding of quantum many-body states in twisted bilayer graphene at the magic angle has been greatly improved both in experiment and in theory. However, away from the exactly solvable chiral limit and the sign-problem-free charge neutrality point, the calculation of the ground state properties and the identification of the phase diagram are challenging due to the exponential increase in the complexity, which has rendered explanations of experimentally observed insulating and superconducting phases restricted largely to the perturbative level. Here we focus on the filling factors $ν= \pm2$ away from charge neutrality and address the question of the strain dependence of the interacting ground state. We adjust our continuous field momentum-space quantum Monte Carlo (QMC) method to treat the sign problem approximately, and perform a quantum many-body study together with exact diagonalization (ED) and Hartree-Fock (HF) mean field. Leveraging this combined protocol of QMC, ED, and HF, we investigate the strain-tuned transition from the Kramers intervalley coherent (KIVC) state to the incommensurate Kekulé spiral state (IKS). Our computational protocol sheds light on the KIVC-IKS transition in a projected correlated flat-band setting, and opens the door for further understanding of the rich phase diagram of twisted bilayer graphene and other strongly-correlated flat-band systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21423"></a>
### [Temperature-induced optical enhancement near a localization transition](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21423v1)

**Authors:** Raul Liquito, Miguel Gonçalves, Bruno Amorim, Eduardo V. Castro  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21423v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21423_figures/2605.21423_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

Notice the different energy (y-axis) scales of the main plot and inset. The color plot encodes the inverse participation ratio (IPR) of each eigenstate. The dashed black vertical line marks the metal-insulator transition of the AA model at W = 2.0.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21423_figures/2605.21423_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. The plots show the DOS of the central bands around E = 0. The dotted black curve is the Fermi Dirac function plotted for T ≈5 × 10−3. </details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21423_figures/2605.21423_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. Drude Weight (D) as a function of quasiperiodic potential strength (W) obtained with three different meth- ods. “Numerical Kubo” was obtained for a finite temperature of T = 5 × 10−4, a system size of L = 10946 via Krylov-Schur sparse methods. The “Real Space vF ” was obtained by numer- ically calculating the eigenstate at Fermi energy (E = 0) for a system size L = 514229 via via Krylov-Schur sparse methods (see Appendix. B). The “Perturbation Theory vF ” curve was obtained via perturbation theory for a system size L = 1597 truncated to order 100 (see Appendix. A). The dashed grey vertical line marks the metal insulator transition of the AA model at W = 2.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper investigates the optical response of the 1D Aubry-Andre model near its localization transition. It discovers that increasing temperature can significantly enhance low-frequency optical conductivity by activating transitions between van Hove singularities that are otherwise Pauli-blocked. This provides a new way to probe and control transport in quasiperiodic systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is interesting for many-body dynamics and quantum optics because it demonstrates how temperature can be used as a 'knob' to manipulate and enhance optical responses in non-trivial, fractal energy spectra.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study addresses the lack of systematic analysis regarding finite-frequency optical conductivity near the metal-insulator transition in quasiperiodic systems, specifically how the optical gap and conductivity evolve.

**Main result.** The authors uncover a mechanism where finite temperature induces a strong enhancement of low-frequency optical conductivity by thermally activating Pauli-blocked transitions between resonant van Hove singularities.

**Method.** The researchers use numerical simulations of the Kubo-Greenwood formula, Krylov-Schur subspace methods for eigenstates, and non-degenerate perturbation theory to analyze transport and spectral properties.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on the 1D Aubry-Andre model, a tight-binding chain with a deterministic quasiperiodic potential characterized by hopping amplitude t and potential strength W.

**Key observables.** Optical conductivity (regular and Drude weight), optical gap, density of states (DOS), and Inverse Participation Ratio (IPR).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Potential strength (W), temperature (T), frequency (omega), and the incommensurability parameter (golden mean).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The perturbative framework is strictly valid in the weak-potential regime (W < 2t); the study assumes a single-particle formulation.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the evolution of optical conductivity and the optical gap with increasing potential strength and temperature, highlighting the emergence of resonant peaks and the closing of the gap.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of quasiperiodic transport, describes the Aubry-Andre model and numerical methods, analyzes the zero-temperature optical gap evolution, details the temperature-induced enhancement mechanism via perturbation theory, and discusses scaling laws and localization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quasiperiodic systems are an intermediate class of systems between periodic crystals and disordered systems, famously exhibiting metal-insulator transitions (MITs) even in one dimension. While their transport properties have been studied extensively, a systematic analysis of the finite-frequency optical conductivity near the critical point has been lacking. In this work, we carry out a detailed study of the optical conductivity in the paradigmatic Aubry-André model. We find that the zero-temperature low-frequency optical signal is strongly restructured by the quasiperiodic potential, exhibiting an optical gap that closes discontinuously as the system approaches the MIT. Most strikingly, we uncover a mechanism for a strong enhancement of the low-frequency finite temperature optical conductivity at certain resonant frequencies. This enhancement stems from the thermal activation of Pauli-blocked transitions between strongly resonant van Hove singularities. This mechanism provides new insight into finite-frequency transport in quasiperiodic systems and a new pathway for manipulating optical properties near a localization transition. Furthermore, our findings establish the optical response as a powerful, experimentally accessible tool for probing non-trivial quasiperiodicity effects.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21327"></a>
### [Universal fusion category symmetries on tensor products of infinite-dimensional Hilbert spaces](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21327v1)

**Authors:** Ian Bunner, Corey Jones  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21327v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21327_figures/2605.21327_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21327_figures/2605.21327_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21327_figures/2605.21327_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21327_figures/2605.21327_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21327_figures/2605.21327_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a universal classification for the kinematic realization of fusion category symmetries on 1D lattices. By introducing a stabilization process using infinite-dimensional ancilla, the authors show that all anyon chains realizing the same symmetry category are equivalent, effectively reducing a complex 'zoo' of models to a single stable equivalence class.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to classify the different ways a fixed fusion category can be realized as a symmetry on a lattice (kinematical realization) and to resolve the 'zoo' of anyon chain variations using a physically motivated stable equivalence relation.

**Main result.** The authors prove that after stabilizing with infinite-dimensional ancilla, any anyon chain admits a local decomposition into a tensor product of infinite-dimensional Hilbert spaces, and for a fixed fusion category, there is a single stable equivalence class of symmetry realizations.

**Method.** The study utilizes the mathematical framework of quasi-local C*-algebras, W*-algebras, DHR theory, and the construction of W*-tensor categories via infinite-dimensional Hilbert space enrichment.

**Model / system.** The models studied are anyon chains and fusion spin chains (boundary algebras of Levin-Wen type string-net models) defined on 1D lattices, specifically utilizing infinite-dimensional Hilbert spaces and ancilla to achieve stabilization.

**Key observables.** Symmetry category, Drinfeld center, symmetry order algebra, and bounded spread isomorphism class.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The stabilization process via infinite-dimensional ancilla, the localization length R, and the strong tensor generator X.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The results assume the use of infinite-dimensional ancilla for stabilization and rely on the properties of unitary fusion/multi-fusion categories and local normality of the algebras.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the mathematical requirements for symmetry (Haag duality, local normality) to constructing the 'standard model' of anyon chains, then moves to proving the universality of these realizations via stabilization, and concludes with the classification of fusion spin chains based on their bulk topological order.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that anyon chains, after stabilizing with infinite-dimensional ancilla spaces, factorize locally as tensor products of infinite-dimensional Hilbert spaces. This implies that any unitary fusion category can be realized as symmetries on a tensor product of infinite-dimensional Hilbert spaces. We then show that any two anyon chains with the same symmetry category are related by a symmetry-compatible locality-preserving unitary after stabilizing with infinite-dimensional ancilla, showing that for a fixed fusion category, there is a single stable equivalence class of symmetry realizations on the lattice via anyon chains. As a corollary of our proof, we show that the physical boundary algebras of Levin-Wen type models are bounded spread isomorphic after stabilization if and only if they have the same bulk topological order.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.20343"></a>
### [Velocity collapse and non-conformal spiral phase in the sawtooth spin chain](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20343v1)

**Authors:** Nai Chao Hu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20343v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20343_figures/2605.20343_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Schematic stopping-event diagram based on the numerical integration of the one-loop RG equations, with g⊥ 1 (0) = g2(0). In the green and pink regions, λa/va is the first dimensionless coupling to reach order one. Green denotes the local-critical candidate regime, where the apical veloc- ity subsequently reaches the velocity cutoff before the basal diagnostic λb/vb reaches order one. Pink denotes the com- plementary case, where λb/vb reaches order one before the velocity cutoff. Blue denotes WA/KT current-channel domi- nation through g⊥ 1 /vb.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.20343_figures/2605.20343_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Representative trajectory at (a) α(0) = 0.05 and g⊥ 1 (0) = g2(0) = 0.1: The apical velocity is driven rapidly downward, while λa/va becomes the leading dimensionless coupling before the basal sector reaches its own strong-coupling scale; (b) α(0) = 0.7 and g⊥ 1 (0) = g2(0) = 0.15: the WA phase with g⊥ 1 /vb the leading dimensionless coupling; (c) α(0) = 0.7 and g⊥ 1 (0) = g2(0) = 0.01: a similarly gapped phase with both λs/vs the leading dimensionless couplings.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explains why the spiral phase in the sawtooth spin chain appears to violate standard conformal field theory predictions. By showing that the apical spin velocity collapses due to a specific twist interaction, the authors provide a theoretical basis for the observed anomalous scaling and flat excitation bands.

**Why it may be interesting.** The discovery of a mechanism where energy scales decouple from spatial correlation lengths via velocity collapse provides new insights into non-conformal critical points and the emergence of local quantum criticality in 1D systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Resolving the numerical puzzle of the spiral phase in the sawtooth spin chain, where recent calculations show signatures (large central charge, slow scaling) inconsistent with standard conformal field theory.

**Main result.** The authors identify a 'velocity collapse' mechanism where a marginal twist interaction selectively reduces the apical spin velocity, leading to a state of local quantum criticality where the energy scale collapses independently of the spatial correlation length.

**Method.** A bosonization theory developed by embedding the sawtooth limit into an asymmetric zigzag ladder, combined with a two-velocity Renormalable Group (RG) analysis and Operator Product Expansion (OPE) techniques.

**Model / system.** A frustrated spin-1/2 sawtooth (delta) spin chain consisting of basal and apical spins with exchange interactions J1 and J2.

**Key observables.** Central charge (c), dynamical exponent (z), apical spin velocity (va), and apical backscattering interaction.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Velocity ratio (alpha = va/vb), exchange couplings (J1, J2), and the regime of extreme velocity asymmetry (va << vb).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The sawtooth limit is regularized by an auxiliary asymmetric zigzag ladder; the analysis assumes a controlled regime of large velocity asymmetry to allow for perturbative RG.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 shows a phase diagram of different RG regimes (WZW, gap-opening, and local criticality); Figure 3 illustrates RG trajectories showing the rapid collapse of apical velocity and the dominance of specific couplings.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies numerical anomalies, constructs a continuum theory via a zigzag ladder regularization, applies RG analysis to track velocity and coupling evolution, and concludes by characterizing the resulting local quantum critical phase.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Recent matrix-product-state calculations show that the spiral phase in the sawtooth chain has numerical signatures that are difficult to reconcile with an ordinary conformal critical point: a large apparent central charge, slow dynamical scaling, nearly flat excitations, and no detectable dimerization. We develop a bosonization theory for this phenomenology by embedding the sawtooth limit in a zigzag ladder described by two coupled SU(2)$_1$ conformal field theories characterized by an extreme velocity ratio. We show that the sawtooth geometry cancels the leading staggered interaction, leaving a marginal twist interaction that selectively collapses the slow apical spin velocity. Crucially, as this velocity vanishes, the generated apical backscattering interaction diverges only in dimensionless units, causing the energy scale to collapse independently of the spatial correlation length. This mechanism naturally accounts for many of the numerical anomalies and we interpret the perturbative flow as an entrance to local quantum criticality in the strong-coupling regime.

</details>

### [What Lies Between Crystal and Randomly Packed Structures? A General Characterization of Non-Periodic Order](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20709v1)

**Authors:** Ian Douglass, Peter Harrowell  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20709v1>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

**Summary.** This paper provides a general framework for identifying order in non-periodic materials by using 'structural selectivity' as a metric. By studying thousands of groundstates in a 2D lattice model, the authors demonstrate that a significant portion of non-periodic structures are not merely random but follow identifiable ordering principles.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper seeks to characterize the structural landscape between periodic crystals and random/amorphous solids, specifically investigating if non-periodic structures can be classified by underlying ordering principles.

**Main result.** The study finds that 96% of groundstate structures are non-periodic, and approximately 35% of these exhibit 'structural selectivity,' a signature of order that persists even at high structural diversity.

**Method.** The authors performed extensive simulations using simulated annealing (cooling) to identify groundstate structures for thousands of different Hamiltonian configurations.

**Model / system.** A 2D Favored Local Structure (FLS) model on a triangular lattice where each site is occupied by an A or B particle, and a many-body Hamiltonian assigns energy -1 to a user-selected set of 'favored' local structures.

**Key observables.** Structural diversity (S1, using Hill's numbers), structural selectivity, and computable information density (CID).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Structural diversity (S1), unit cell size (Z), and the number of distinct local structures (14).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study focuses on groundstate structures to avoid thermal fluctuation complications; the results are based on a 2D lattice model which may not fully capture 3D off-lattice complexities.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the 14 possible local structures; Figures 7-8 show distributions of diversity for selective vs. non-selective structures; Figure 9 provides visual examples of different non-periodic selective categories; Figure 10 shows examples of disordered structures.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of characterizing non-periodic order, describes the FLS model and simulation protocol, presents the results regarding the prevalence of non-periodicity and selectivity, classifies types of non-periodic order, and discusses limitations and future directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this paper we address the characterization of the structure of condensed materials, periodic and non-periodic. Carrying out an extensive study of over 7000 different groundstate structures of a 2D lattice model of binary packing, we find a predominance of non-periodic structures (over 96%) that extend across the entire range of possible diversities. These non-periodic structures are resolved by establishing whether a structure will accommodate or reject additional local structures. This property, structural selectivity, is treated as a signature of an underlying ordering principle. The major result of the paper is the determination that roughly 35% of the non-periodic structures are selective and, hence, ordered in some way. This selectivity extends up to a diversity of ~ 9, well beyond the upper threshold for diversity in periodically ordered states.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.20801"></a>
### [Q-SpiRL: Quantum Spiking Reinforcement Learning for Adaptive Robot Navigation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20801v1)

**Authors:** Mohamed Khair Altrabulsi, Nouhaila Innan, Alberto Marchisio, Muhammad Kashif, Muhammad Shafique  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20801v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `QC/QI experiment` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20801_figures/2605.20801_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1: Overview of the proposed methodology pipeline. The framework begins with the definition of the navigation environment. It then considers multiple learning architectures, with the QSNN as the central quantum-enhanced model of this work. After independent hyperparameter selection through grid search, each trained policy is converted into an explicit Q-table for deterministic greedy inference. Final evaluation is carried out on held-out environments of different sizes using success rate, path length, success-weighted path length, and turn rate.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.20801_figures/2605.20801_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2: Architecture of the classical MLP.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.20801_figures/2605.20801_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4: Variational quantum circuit, also referred to as a parameterized quantum circuit, used in both the QMLP and QSNN architectures. The circuit is composed of repeated variational blocks acting on n qubits, where the highlighted region represents a single layer L. Each layer consists of Hadamard gates, trainable controlled rotation gates, and entangling operations, and the full circuit is formed by stacking multiple such layers. Final measurements on all qubits produce the quantum features used by the hybrid model.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.20801_figures/2605.20801_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3: Architecture of the classical SNN.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.20801_figures/2605.20801_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5: Example QSNN trajectories in the 20×20, 30×30, and 40×40 environments. Static obstacles are shown as dark cells, while dynamic obstacle motion is indicated by dashed traces.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces Q-SpiRL, a framework that combines quantum variational circuits with spiking neural networks for robot navigation. It demonstrates that the quantum-enhanced spiking architecture outperforms classical and quantum-MLP models in navigating complex, dynamic environments. The study also proves that such hybrid policies can be successfully executed on real IBM quantum processors.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing adaptive robot navigation policies that can reliably navigate dynamic environments with static and moving obstacles while addressing the resource limitations of classical RL on embedded platforms.

**Main result.** The Quantum Spiking Neural Network (QSNN) achieves the best trade-off between task completion, efficiency, and smoothness, reaching up to a 99% success rate in complex settings and demonstrating feasibility on IBM quantum hardware.

**Method.** The researchers implemented a hybrid framework (Q-SpiRL) comparing five agent families, using a unified training pipeline and converting trained policies into deterministic Q-tables for fair evaluation.

**Model / system.** The central model is a Quantum Spiking Neural Network (QSNN) that integrates a variational quantum circuit (8 qubits, 3 layers) with a Deep Spiking Q-Network using Leaky Integrate-and-Fire neurons and Poisson encoding.

**Key observables.** Success Rate (SR), Success-Weighted Path Length (SPL), Path Length (PL), and Turn Rate (TR).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Environment scales (20x20, 30x30, 40x40), 8 qubits, 3 variational layers, 800 training episodes, and Adam optimizer parameters.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The evaluation assumes that converting policies to deterministic Q-tables eliminates stochasticity for fair comparison; the hardware evaluation is a single-episode feasibility check subject to noise.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the methodology pipeline; Figure 2 and 3 illustrate the MLP and SNN architectures; Figure 4 details the Variational Quantum Circuit; Figure 5 shows QSNN trajectories; Figure 6 shows a trajectory on real IBM hardware.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the navigation problem, details the Q-SpiRL framework and its five agent architectures, describes the simulation environments and evaluation metrics, presents quantitative results across different scales, and concludes with a hardware feasibility check on IBM quantum hardware.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Adaptive robot navigation in dynamic environments requires policies that can reach the target reliably while producing efficient and stable trajectories. This paper presents Q-SpiRL, a quantum spiking reinforcement learning framework for obstacle-aware robot navigation. The framework develops and evaluates five agent families: tabular Q-learning, classical MLP, classical SNN, quantum-enhanced MLP (QMLP), and quantum-enhanced spiking neural network (QSNN). While all models are implemented under a unified training and evaluation pipeline, the QSNN is the central architecture of interest, as it combines spike-based temporal processing with variational quantum feature transformation. Experiments are conducted across three grid-world environments of increasing size, namely 20x20, 30x30, and 40x40, with both static and dynamic obstacles. Performance is assessed using success rate, success-weighted path length, path length, and turn rate under deterministic inference. Results show that QSNN achieves the strongest overall trade-off between task completion, trajectory efficiency, and motion smoothness, reaching up to 99% success rate while maintaining high path efficiency in the most challenging setting. Execution on IBM quantum hardware further demonstrates the feasibility of deploying the proposed hybrid policy under real-device conditions.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper uses machine learning force fields to perform large-scale simulations of the phase-change material Sb2S3. It reveals that the material's crystallization is governed by interface-controlled kinetics and highly anisotropic growth. These insights are crucial for improving the speed and efficiency of next-generation data storage and neuromorphic computing devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the atomistic-scale mechanisms, structural evolution, and anisotropic crystallization kinetics of the phase-change material Sb2S3 during the crystal-to-liquid phase transition.

**Main result.** The study finds that Sb2S3 crystallization is interface-controlled rather than diffusion-limited, with the [100] facet exhibiting the fastest growth due to strong covalent bonding along its 1D ribbon structure.

**Method.** Large-scale molecular dynamics simulations using a Machine Learning Force Field (MLFF) developed via the Moment Tensor Potential (MTP) approach and active learning.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the phase-change material antimony sulfide (Sb2S3), which transitions between a crystalline (Pnma space group) and an amorphous/liquid phase. The simulation uses a Dual Phase Coexistence (DPC) model with up to 7680 atoms.

**Key observables.** Lattice parameters, radial distribution functions (RDF), angular distribution functions (ADF), coordination numbers, crystal growth rates (Vg), and activation energies for growth and diffusion.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Activation energy for growth (0.55-0.57 eV), activation energy for diffusion (1.16-1.56 eV), melting point (975 K), and optimal undercooling (~100 K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The accuracy of the results depends on the quality of the MTP training set; the study assumes the MLFF-MD workflow effectively bridges microscopic dynamics to macroscopic growth.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows MTP training correlation, the Sb2S3 unit cell, and liquid phase snapshots/RDFs; Figure 2 displays angle and coordination number distributions for crystal and liquid phases.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the material and problem, describes the development and validation of the MTP machine learning force field, presents structural and kinetic results of the phase transition, and discusses the implications for device optimization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The phase-change material antimony sulfide (Sb$_2$S$_3$) relies on rapid and reversible phase transitions between crystalline and amorphous states, which are critical for their performance in data storage and photonics applications. In this work, a machine learning force field is developed based on the moment tensor potential approach, allowing us to understand the atomistic origin of the structural evolution and crystallization kinetics in Sb$_2$S$_3$ for the first time, by enabling large-scale molecular dynamics simulations (up to 7680 atoms for 40 ns). Sb$_2$S$_3$ shows anisotropic growth rates with the [100] facet exhibiting the fastest growth due to the strong Sb-S covalent bonding along its quasi-1D ribbon-like structure of its crystalline phase. The activation energy for crystal growth is found to be 0.55-0.57 eV, whereas that for diffusion is around 1.16-1.56 eV. The lower activation energy for crystal growth indicates that its heterogeneous crystallization is interface controlled rather than diffusion limited, unlike GST and GeTe with atomic attachment at the solid-liquid interface being energetically favoured over long range atomic transport. These findings provide key insights into the structural, thermodynamic, and kinetic properties of Sb$_2$S$_3$, paving the way for optimizing its functionality including switching speed, reliability, and energy efficiency.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21451"></a>
### [Approximation Theory for Neural Networks: Old and New](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21451v1)

**Authors:** Soumendu Sundar Mukherjee, Himasish Talukdar  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21451v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

The outputs of these are then passed though another neuron that imple- ments the AND gate.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21451_figures/2605.21451_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Schematic of a KAN with L layers: each edge here represents a learnable (typically nonlinear) univariate function φ(l) j,i, and each blue node aggregates by summation. Thus, unlike FNNs, nonlinearities are associated with edges rather than nodes.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This survey provides a comprehensive overview of the mathematical foundations of neural network approximation theory. It details how depth and width influence the ability to represent complex functions and evaluates the emerging Kolmogorov-Arnold Network architecture. It is a significant resource for understanding the fundamental limits of machine learning architectures.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper surveys the mathematical theory of neural network approximation, specifically comparing the expressive power, parameter efficiency, and error rates of classical feedforward networks versus Kolmogorov-Arnold Networks (KANs).

**Main result.** It establishes that deeper architectures can achieve superior parameter efficiency for complex functions and provides quantitative error bounds for spline-based KANs, while noting that a definitive comparison of approximation rates between KANs and FNNs remains an open question.

**Method.** The survey reviews functional analytic proofs (e.g., Hahn-Banach, Riesz representation), Fourier analysis, and constructive methods using B-splines and tent functions to analyze approximation rates in Sobolev and Besov spaces.

**Model / system.** The paper discusses various neural network architectures, including Feedforward Neural Networks (FNNs) with ReLU/Sigmoidal activations and Kolmogorov-Arnold Networks (KANs) which utilize learnable univariate functions on edges.

**Key observables.** Approximation error rates, network width, network depth, and topological complexity of represented functions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Network width (m), depth (L), grid size (G) for splines, and the smoothness (Holder/Sobolev) of target functions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on functions defined on compact subsets of R^d and assumes familiarity with functional analysis; it does not address optimization or statistical generalization.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the XOR problem as a case of linear non-separability; Figure 2 illustrates how a hidden layer enables linear separation; Figure 3 depicts a standard multi-layer perceptron architecture; Figure 4 shows the edge-based nonlinearity structure of KANs.

**Paper structure.** The paper provides a historical overview of universal approximation, moves into quantitative refinements regarding depth-width trade-offs, analyzes specific activation functions, explores the new KAN paradigm, and concludes with open theoretical challenges.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Universal approximation theorems provide a mathematical explanation for the expressive power of neural networks. They assert that, under mild conditions on the activation function, feedforward neural networks are dense in broad function classes, such as continuous functions on compact subsets of $\mathbb{R}^d$, $L^p$ spaces, or Sobolev spaces. Over the past four decades, these qualitative universality results have evolved into a rich quantitative theory addressing approximation rates, parameter efficiency, and the role of architectural features such as depth and width. This survey presents several glimpses into this theory. We review classical density results for single-hidden-layer networks, as well as quantitative bounds that relate approximation error to network size and smoothness assumptions on target functions. Particular emphasis is placed on depth--width trade-offs and on results demonstrating that deeper architectures can achieve superior parameter efficiency for structured function classes. In addition to standard feedforward neural networks, we also review recent developments on Kolmogorov--Arnold Networks (KANs), which offer an alternative architectural paradigm and whose approximation-theoretic properties have begun to attract significant theoretical attention.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.20789"></a>
### [Circuits of Quantum Hashing and Quantum Fourier Transform for a Cactus as a Qubit Connectivity Graph](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20789v1)

**Authors:** Kamil Khadiev, Ilnur Valeev  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20789v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20789_figures/2605.20789_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2. Representation of CRy, SWAP gates, and a pair CRy and SWAP gates using only basic gates.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides an efficient, polynomial-time method for building optimized quantum circuits for Quantum Hashing and QFT on cactus-structured quantum hardware. By solving a specific graph-theoretic path problem, the authors significantly reduce the number of CNOT gates required compared to previous methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing quantum circuit construction, specifically minimizing CNOT gate counts for Quantum Hashing and Quantum Fourier Transform (QFT), under restricted qubit connectivity constraints.

**Main result.** The authors present an O(n^3) algorithm to construct shallow circuits for cactus graph architectures, significantly reducing CNOT complexity compared to exponential-time algorithms for arbitrary graphs.

**Method.** The approach uses a solution to the 'Shortest non-simple 1-covering path' (1-SNSCPP) problem via dynamic programming on a tree-transformed cactus graph to guide qubit movement via SWAP gates.

**Model / system.** A quantum device where two-qubit gates are restricted by a 'cactus' connectivity graph, where any two simple cycles share at most one vertex.

**Key observables.** CNOT gate count (cost) and circuit depth.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of qubits (n), number of edges (m), path length (k), and the length of the input word (l).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The optimization results are specifically tailored to the cactus graph topology and assume the ability to implement SWAP and controlled rotation gates.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate shallow circuit architectures, gate decompositions (CRy, SWAP, CRd), comparisons between standard and optimized paths on cactus graphs, and the construction of auxiliary trees for the DP algorithm.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of connectivity-constrained circuit optimization, defines the cactus graph model, presents a polynomial-time solution for the 1-SNSCPP problem using dynamic programming, applies this to Quantum Hashing and QFT, and provides complexity analysis and CNOT cost bounds.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a quantum circuit implementation of the quantum hashing algorithm (quantum fingerprinting) for a quantum device with restrictions on the application of two-qubit gates by a qubit connectivity graph. We present an optimization technique for the shallow circuit for quantum hashing in the case of a cactus as a qubit connectivity graph. The algorithm has $O(n^3)$ complexity to build the circuit, where $n$ is the number of qubits and $m$ is the number of connections (edges) in the graph. It is improvement compared to the existing exponential-time algorithm in the case of arbitrary graphs. The algorithm uses solution for the shortest non-simple 1-covering path problem as a subroutine. We present an $O(n^3)$-time solution for this graph-theory problem in the case of a cactus. This result can be interesting independently. The algorithm also used for improving of the quantum circuit for Quantum Fourier Transform.

</details>

### [Competing anisotropies and phase transitions in the $q$-state clock model with a $p$-fold crystalline field](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20990v1)

**Authors:** Milan Žukovič  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20990v1>

<sub>Figure 3: Temperature dependencies of (a,d,g,j) the speciﬁc heat, (b,e,h,k) the magnetizations 푚1 and 푚3, and (c,f,i,l) the magnetic susceptibilities for 푝= 2, (a-c) ℎ2 = −0.5, (d-f) ℎ2 = −0.1, (g-i) ℎ2 = 0.1, (j-l) ℎ2 = 0.5, and various lattice sizes. 푇푐1 and 푇푐2 are the critical temperatures at the respective 퐹0 −퐹1 and 퐹1 −푃transitions for ℎ2 &lt; 0 and 푇푐denotes the critical temperature at the 퐹0 −푃transition for ℎ2 &gt; 0.</sub>

<sub>Figure 4: Phase diagram for 푝= 2 and 4. 퐹0 and 퐹1 denote diﬀerent FM LRO phases and BKT denotes the line of BKT transition points between 푇푐1 and 푇푐2 (marked by pentagrams) for ℎ푖= 0, 푖= 2, 4. </details>

<img src="2605.20990_figures/2605.20990_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: FSS analysis for 푝= 2 of the quantities (a-c) 휒1, 푑푙푚푙 1, 푙= 1, 2, and 푑푚1 at the transition temperature 푇푐2, (d-f) 휒3, 푑푙푚푙 3, 푙= 1, 2, and 푑푚3 at the transition temperature 푇푐1, for ℎ2 = −0.5 and (g-i) 휒1, 푑푙푚푙 1, 푙= 1, 2, and 푑푚1 at the transition temperature 푇푐, for ℎ2 = 0.5.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores how adding a p-fold symmetry-breaking field to the 2D q-state clock model reshapes its phase diagram. It demonstrates that these fields can destroy the topological BKT phase and replace it with conventional long-range order, with the specific transition type depending on the competition between the intrinsic and imposed symmetries.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how competing discrete anisotropies (intrinsic Zq symmetry vs. external Zp crystalline fields) affect the phase transitions and the stability of the BKT phase in the 2D q-state clock model.

**Main result.** Even weak crystalline fields suppress the BKT phase and induce transitions to long-range order, with the phase diagram being governed by the competition between locking mechanisms rather than symmetry alone. Specifically, for q=6, p=2, the sign of the field determines whether there is a single transition (h2 > 0) or a two-step ordering process (h2 < 0).

**Method.** Monte Carlo simulations using the Metropolis algorithm combined with finite-size scaling (FSS) analysis to determine critical exponents and universality classes.

**Model / system.** A two-dimensional q-state clock model on a square lattice with an additional p-fold crystalline field term, described by the Hamiltonian H = -J sum cos(theta_i - theta_j) - hp sum cos(p*theta_i).

**Key observables.** Specific heat, generalized magnetizations (m1, m3), magnetic susceptibilities, and logarithmic derivatives of magnetization moments.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (T/J), crystalline field strength (hp), symmetry indices (p, q), and lattice size (L).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the system is in the strong-anisotropy limit of generalized XY models and focuses on the interplay between discrete symmetries.

**Figures summary.** Figures show temperature dependence of specific heat, magnetization, and susceptibility for the isotropic model; phase diagrams and specific heat peaks for p=2 (showing single vs. double transitions); and FSS analysis of critical exponent ratios for p=3.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the model and the problem of competing anisotropies, details the Monte Carlo methodology, presents results for the p=2 case (distinguishing between positive and negative field strengths), analyzes the p=3 case (identifying Potts criticality), and concludes with a discussion on the instability of the BKT phase and the role of finite-size effects.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the two-dimensional $q$-state clock model in the presence of an additional $p$-fold symmetry-breaking crystalline field using Monte Carlo simulations. While the pure clock model exhibits Berezinskii--Kosterlitz--Thouless (BKT) transitions for sufficiently large $q$, the effect of competing discrete anisotropies on this topological phase remains nontrivial. We show that even weak crystalline fields qualitatively modify the phase diagram by suppressing the BKT phase and inducing transitions to states with true long-range order. The resulting behavior depends sensitively on the interplay between the intrinsic $\mathbb{Z}_q$ symmetry and the imposed $\mathbb{Z}_p$ anisotropy. In particular, in the six-state clock model for $p=2$ we observe qualitatively different scenarios depending on the sign of the field: a single transition for $h_2>0$ and a two-step ordering process for $h_2<0$ with an intermediate ordered phase. For $p=3$, the system exhibits a direct transition consistent with three-state Potts criticality. These results demonstrate that the phase structure cannot be inferred from symmetry considerations alone, but is governed by the competition between distinct locking mechanisms. Our findings provide a discrete counterpart to the multi-frequency sine-Gordon description of generalized $XY$ models and illustrate how additional anisotropies reshape topological phase transitions in two dimensions.

</details>

### [Discernible signatures of fractionally charged anyons in a Pfaffian-Laughlin state](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21462v1)

**Authors:** Vadym Apalkov, Tapash Chakraborty  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21462v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a theoretical method to detect e/4 anyons in the Pfaffian state by coupling the state to a quantum dot. By analyzing the energy dispersion and charge density, the authors show that the interaction between the dot and the fluid provides clear signatures of these elusive quasiparticles. This approach could potentially be verified using photoluminescence spectroscopy.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to identify discernible signatures of e/4 fractionally charged anyons in a Pfaffian-Laughlin state by investigating their energy dispersion and charge density distribution.

**Main result.** The authors observe clearly gapped energy branches in the energy spectra, where branch splitting is driven by inter-quasihole interactions, and estimate the e/4 quasihole creation energy to be approximately 0.014 epsilon_C.

**Method.** The researchers use exact diagonalization of the interaction Hamiltonian within a spherical geometry to calculate many-electron energy spectra and electron density distributions.

**Model / system.** A bilayer system consisting of a 2D electron layer in an incompressible Pfaffian state at filling factor nu = 1/2, coupled via Coulomb interactions to a nearby 2D quantum dot with a parabolic confinement potential.

**Key observables.** Energy dispersion, charge density distribution, and quasihole creation energy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Filling factor nu = 1/2, magnetic field B = 15 T, interlayer distance d (ranging from 1.5l0 to 4.5l0), and Haldane pseudopotentials.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The electrons are assumed to be spinless due to large Zeeman energy, and the system is treated as a finite electron system.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows energy spectra for a QD with one electron at various distances; Figure 2 shows electron density distribution for the one-electron case; Figure 3 shows energy spectra for a QD containing an exciton.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of anyon identification, describes the theoretical model of a QD-coupled Pfaffian state, presents numerical results for energy spectra and density distributions for both electron and exciton-filled dots, and concludes with implications for PL spectroscopy.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Understanding the nature of quasihole excitations, i.e., anyons that have fractional charge and statistics, has been a challenging problem in condensed matter physics. Our theoretical approach to this problem has been to consider a quantum dot, containing a few charged particles, coupled to the incompressible fluid. It has provided important insights into the energetics of Laughlin quasiholes. Photoluminescence (PL) spectroscopy studies of this system have been able to probe these quasiholes that have confirmed our expectations. Turning to the Pfaffian state, we now observe that such a system is also able to provide valuable information about the Pfaffian quasiholes, viz., the energy dispersion, the charge density distribution and the quasihole creation energy. The energy dispersion of e/4 quasiholes derived here, clearly reflect the interaction between the quantum dot and the incompressible Pfaffian state. PL spectroscopy experiments on the 5/2 Pfaffian-Laughlin state could perhaps shed light on the energetics of these elusive quasiparticles.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.20573"></a>
### [Distinct lattice and charge excitations in AV3Sb5 kagome superconductors](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20573v1)

**Authors:** Dongjin Oh, Stefan Enzner, Lennart Klebl, Harley D. Scammell, Julian Ingham, Tim Wehling, Giorgio Sangiovanni, Ronny Thomale, Ahmet Kemal Demir, Connor Occhialini, Dirk Wulferding, Ziqiang Wang, Andrea C. Salinas, Stephen D. Wilson, Riccardo Comin  
**Type:** both · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20573v1>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper investigates the difference between lattice and charge excitations in AV3Sb5 kagome superconductors using polarized Raman spectroscopy. It finds that while the splitting of certain phonon modes is a universal feature across Cs, Rb, and K variants, the charge density wave excitations are highly material-dependent. This distinction provides new clues into how symmetry breaking emerges in these correlated electronic systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The microscopic origin of the distinct electronic symmetry-breaking phenomena and the material-dependent charge density wave (CDW) orders in AV3Sb5 (A=Cs, Rb, K) kagome superconductors remains elusive.

**Main result.** The study reveals that while the lifting of the E2g phonon degeneracy is a ubiquitous lattice anomaly across all three compounds, the collective CDW amplitude modes exhibit markedly different polarization dependences depending on the alkali-metal species.

**Method.** Angle-resolved polarized Raman spectroscopy (ARPRS) combined with first-principles calculations (DFPT) and an effective Hamiltonian model.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the family of AV3Sb5 (A=Cs, Rb, K) kagome superconductors, which feature both superconductivity and charge density wave phases with complex symmetry breaking.

**Key observables.** Phonon modes (E2g doublet splitting and A1g singlet), CDW amplitude modes, and polarization-resolved Raman spectra (XX and XY geometries).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Polarization angle (theta), temperature (T), Raman shift (cm-1), and phonon frequency modulation.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the observed phonon shifts are due to spectral overlap of independent modes rather than experimental misalignment.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the experimental geometry and basic Raman spectra; Fig 2 demonstrates the fourfold modulation of the P1 phonon frequency across Cs, Rb, and K compounds; Fig 3 provides detailed Lorentzian fitting of the P1 phonon splitting.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of symmetry breaking in AV3Sb5, presents experimental Raman spectroscopy results showing universal lattice anomalies and distinct charge dynamics, and concludes with theoretical modeling and implications for charge order structures.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The kagome superconductor family AV3Sb5 (A=Cs, Rb, K) provides a rich platform for exploring diverse electronic symmetry breaking phenomena, including superconductivity and various forms of density wave orders. Although these compounds share the identical lattice structure in the normal state, they exhibit distinct forms of symmetry breaking upon entering the charge density wave (CDW) phase, and the microscopic origin of which remain elusive. Here, we investigate the lattice and charge degrees of freedom in AV3Sb5 using angle-resolved polarized Raman spectroscopy. Our comprehensive polarization-resolved measurements reveal that the lifting of the twofold-degeneracy of the E2g phonon mode in the CDW phase-previously reported only in CsV3Sb5 with a 3 GHz splitting-also appears ubiquitously in the other two compounds. In contrast, the collective CDW excitations exhibit markedly different polarization dependences depending on the alkali-metal species. These distinct behaviors in the lattice and charge channels provide crucial insight into the enigmatic material-dependent symmetry breaking phenomena that appear in the CDW phase. Furthermore, our experiments, together with first-principles calculations and an effective Hamiltonian model, shed light on the nature of the charge order structure in AV3Sb5 kagome superconductors.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.21213"></a>
### [Enhanced Reinforcement Learning-based Process Synthesis via Quantum Computing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21213v1)

**Authors:** Austin Braniff, Fengqi You, Yuhe Tian  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21213v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21213_figures/2605.21213_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: An overview of the classical and quantum RL approaches for process design.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21213_figures/2605.21213_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Value-based deep reinforcement learning nomenclature for the present work.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21213_figures/2605.21213_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>

<img src="2605.21213_figures/2605.21213_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Circuit diagram for a PQC layer.</sub>
