**Summary.** This paper proposes a quantum reinforcement learning framework for optimizing chemical process flowsheets. By introducing new encoding strategies, it overcomes previous qubit scaling limitations, showing that quantum circuits can match classical performance with significantly fewer parameters.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the scalability bottleneck in using quantum reinforcement learning for combinatorial process synthesis, where previous qubit requirements scaled poorly with problem complexity.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that new state encoding algorithms decouple qubit requirements from problem size, allowing quantum-enhanced RL to achieve competitive performance and improved parameter efficiency compared to classical Deep Q-Networks.

**Method.** The work introduces a hybrid quantum-classical framework using Parameterized Quantum Circuits (PQCs) as value-function approximators, employing data re-uploading and multi-axis encoding strategies.

**Model / system.** The problem is modeled as a Markov Decision Process (MDP) for chemical process synthesis, specifically applied to a hydrodealkylation (HDA) reaction involving toluene, hydrogen, and methane.

**Key observables.** Performance metrics include the number of optimal, unique, and feasible solutions found, as well as the episode number of first discovery and total runtime.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Key parameters include qubit count, circuit depth, number of input features, rotation gate angles, discount factor, and learning rate.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the HDA reaction occurs in the vapor phase with ideal vapor-liquid equilibrium and that the process synthesis can be discretized into a sequential decision-making task.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares classical and quantum RL; Figure 2 shows RL nomenclature; Figures 3-6 illustrate the Bloch sphere, PQC architectures, and data re-uploading; Figures 8-10 show the RL workflow and specific encoding variants; Figure 11 shows an optimal flowsheet.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the process synthesis problem, defines the MDP framework, details the classical DQN baseline, presents the proposed quantum-enhanced RL variants and encoding strategies, evaluates performance across increasing design complexities, and concludes with a discussion on scalability and hardware limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this work, we present quantum reinforcement learning (RL) as a solution strategy for process synthesis problems. Building on our prior work, we develop a generalized framework that formally poses process synthesis as a Markov decision process and introduces quantum-enhanced RL algorithms to solve it with improved scalability. Earlier implementations of quantum-based RL for process synthesis were limited by qubit requirements, which scaled poorly with problem complexity. This work overcomes this challenge by introducing state encoding algorithms to decouple qubit requirements from problem size. A classical RL-based solution strategy is used as a baseline to benchmark the quantum algorithms under identical training conditions. All algorithms are evaluated across a flowsheet synthesis problem of increasing unit counts to analyze their performance and scalability. Results show that all approaches are capable of identifying the optimal flowsheet designs in small design spaces. For moderate-scale unit counts, quantum approaches demonstrate competitive performance on a per-episode basis and improved efficiency on a per-parameter basis versus the classical RL benchmark. This work provides a foundation for future quantum computing applications within process systems engineering, establishes a controlled benchmark for comparing classical and quantum algorithms, and shows that the proposed quantum variants remain competitive for the process synthesis problem examined in this work.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.21277"></a>
### [Fermion condensation in a generalized Hatsugai-Kohmoto model with momentum-mixing Landau interactions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21277v1)

**Authors:** Jan Heinrich, Andreas Rückriegel, Peter Kopietz  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21277v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21277_figures/2605.21277_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. (a) Left figure: ground state occupation of a one- dimensional energy band in the phase HSD where all three types of occupations coexist in the band. Here and in all subsequent figures empty states (holes) are shaded red, singly occupied states are shaded green, and doubly occupied states are shaded blue. The horizontal dashed lines mark the Fermi energies ϵD and ϵS where the occupation changes discontin- uously. Right figure: momentum distribution in the HSD phase. The vertical dashed lines mark the positions of the Fermi momenta kD and kS. All wavevectors are measured in units of the inverse lattice spacing. (b)-(d) are analogous illustrations of the phases HS, SD, and S.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21277_figures/2605.21277_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Phase diagram of the HK model as a function of the density ρ and the interaction U. The color coding indicates the occupancies of momentum states: holes (H, red), singly occupied states (S, green), and doubly occupied states (D, blue). The insets represent the corresponding band fillings.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21277_figures/2605.21277_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Phase diagram of the HK model as a function of the chemical potential µ/U and band width W/U in units of the interaction U. The thick black lines represent the chemical potentials µ+, µ−defined in Eq. (38). The thick red lines represents µS = −W/2, while the thick blue line represents µD = U + W/2.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21277_figures/2605.21277_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21277_figures/2605.21277_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. (a) Local magnetic moment mk = ⟨Sz k⟩of the pseu- dospin HK model (48) as function of the pseudomagnetic field hk. (b) Phase diagram of the HK model in the plane spanned by hk and U.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores how momentum-dependent interactions modify the electronic structure of the Hatsugai-Kohmoto model. It demonstrates that these interactions can induce a partially flat energy band, providing a theoretical mechanism for fermion condensation. The study also provides a mapping between the electronic model and a generalized Ising model.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether momentum-mixing Landau interactions in a generalized Hatsugai-Kohmoto model lead to the emergence of a partially flat energy band (fermion condensation).

**Main result.** The study finds that momentum-mixing interactions smooth out magnetic domain walls in a pseudospin representation, resulting in a partially flat electronic band consistent with the fermion condensation scenario.

**Method.** The authors employ a self-consistent mean-field analysis and a pseudospin formulation that maps the model onto a generalized Ising model in reciprocal space.

**Model / system.** A generalized Hatsugai-Kohmoto (HKL) model describing electrons on a d-dimensional hypercubic lattice with kinetic energy, Hubbard-like interaction (U), and momentum-mixing Landau-type interactions.

**Key observables.** Momentum distribution (n_k), Fermi surfaces, energy dispersion, pseudomagnetization (m_k), and the excitation gap (Delta).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength (U), bandwidth (W), particle density (rho), and momentum-mixing interaction parameters (f_kk').

**Assumptions / limitations.** The HKL model is not exactly solvable, necessitating a mean-field approximation; the interaction range is assumed to be finite in reciprocal space.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows ground state configurations and momentum distributions for different phases (HSD, HS, SD, S); Figure 6 displays the local magnetic moment, effective magnetic field, and the phase diagram in the U vs. h_k plane.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the generalized HK model, develops a pseudospin mapping to an Ising model, performs a mean-field analysis to identify the fermion condensation phase, and concludes by proposing an exactly solvable variant of the model.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Hatsugai-Kohmoto (HK) model is an exactly solvable electronic lattice model where the interaction between electrons with opposite spin is diagonal in momentum space. We generalize the HK model by introducing momentum-mixing Landau interactions. Within a self-consistent mean-field analysis we find that the ground state of this model exhibits a partially flat energy band, in agreement with the fermion condensation scenario proposed by Khodel and Shaginyan [JETP Lett. 51, 553 (1990)]. Inspired by Andersons pseudospin formulation of BCS theory, we show that the HK model with Landau interactions can be mapped onto a generalized Ising model where each site of the reciprocal lattice hosts two Ising spins. In the pseudospin picture the emergence of a partially flat electronic band corresponds to the smoothing of a magnetic domain wall. Moreover, guided by the pseudospin picture, we propose an exactly solvable variant of the HK model which has a unique ground state for all densities.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.20346"></a>
### [Forced Gap Post-Selection for Quantum LDPC Codes and their Operations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20346v1)

**Authors:** Adam Wills, Theodore J. Yoder, Isaac Chuang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20346v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20346_figures/2605.20346_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Overview of the Forced Gap post-selection strategy. Phase 1 (blue): the decoder is run on syndrome σ to produce a baseline correction e(0) with logical class L(0) = A·e(0). If the decoder declares erasure the instance is immediately rejected. Phase 2 (amber): for each of the K logical observables (i = 1, . . . , K), a forced run solves the modified problem (H(i), σ(i)), where an extra row appended to the parity-check matrix constrains the i-th observable to the complementary outcome 1 ⊕L(0) i . Phase 3 (pink): the corrections from all runs are pooled; the two most-likely distinct logical classes λ(0), λ(1) are identified and their log-likelihood difference, the Gap, is computed....</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.20346_figures/2605.20346_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Simulated error rates per round for the idling [[72, 12, 6]] code, and the [[144, 12, 12]] code under post-selection, at physical noise p = 10−3 and 2.5 × 10−3, respectively. The x-axis shows “Post- selection rate”, that is, the fraction of shots that are rejected, where a curve is generated by varying the threshold T. Both codes are simulated for 6 and 12 rounds of syndrome extraction, respectively, and the logical error rate is then normalised per round (we do not normalise by the number of logical qubits). The shaded regions are 95% confidence intervals. The flat horizontal sections on the right of both graphs indicate the regions where all shots have been post-selected that...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.20346_figures/2605.20346_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Logical error rates of various surgery bicycle instructions [Yod+25] subject to post-selection under the forced gap strategy, all at physical noise p = 2.5 × 10−3. We present the logical error rate for the entire circuit, without normalising by the number of rounds. The number of syndrome extraction rounds that we used in our simulations of each gadget are shown above. These were chosen by optimality of the logical error rate for regular error correction without post-selection. For the in-module Y1 and inter-module X1X1 measurements, a higher number of rounds than 10, the circuit distance, was seen to give an improvement, although the differences observed were minimal.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a new post-selection strategy for quantum LDPC codes that significantly reduces logical error rates with minimal computational overhead. By forcing the decoder to explore complementary logical outcomes, the method identifies and rejects unreliable decoding shots. This approach is particularly effective for high-rate codes and is compatible with efficient, FPGA-friendly decoders.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing an efficient and lightweight post-selection strategy for high-rate quantum LDPC codes to reduce logical error rates without the high computational overhead of existing methods.

**Main result.** The proposed 'forced gap' strategy improves logical error rates by over a factor of 4 compared to previous strategies in the low post-selection regime (up to 1%) and scales linearly with the number of logical observables.

**Method.** A decoder-agnostic strategy involving a baseline decoding run followed by K additional runs where the decoder is forced to find corrections with complementary outcomes for each logical observable, using the Relay-BP decoder.

**Model / system.** Quantum Low-Density Parity-Check (LDPC) codes, specifically the 72-qubit and 144-qubit bivariate bicycle codes, under a circuit depolarizing noise model.

**Key observables.** Logical error rate, post-selection rate, and the 'Gap' (difference in log-likelihoods between the most-likely and second-most-likely logical classes).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Physical noise rates (p = 10^-3 and 2.5 x 10^-3), number of logical observables (K), and user-defined gap threshold (T).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The decoder is assumed to be able to operate on modified decoding instances; faults are assumed to occur independently; the 'Gap' is an approximation of the exact difference between logical class probabilities.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the flowchart of the forced gap strategy; Figure 2 plots logical error rates vs. post-selection rate for idling codes; Figure 3 shows logical error rates for various surgery instructions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of post-selection in high-rate LDPC codes, details the proposed forced gap algorithm, describes the Relay-BP decoder implementation and parameter optimization, presents benchmarking results on bivariate bicycle codes for both idling and surgery operations, and discusses future work regarding error floors and scaling.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop a simple and general post-selection strategy for high-rate quantum codes that is transferrable across decoders. After an initial baseline run, the decoder is re-run once per logical observable, and forced in these latter runs to provide a solution where the given observable has the complementary outcome. Shots are rejected that find logically complementary solutions with similar likelihoods compared to the baseline. Using the Relay-BP decoder, we benchmark the strategy on the $72$-qubit and $144$-qubit bivariate bicycle codes, as well as surgery gadgets for the latter. In comparison to previous post-selection strategies, our results offer an improved logical error rate by over a factor of $4$ on the same circuit and physical error rate, and at the same rate of post-selection. Our strategies are also lightweight, relying only on FPGA-friendly belief propagation, whereas the previous best used repeated rounds of a high-latency BP-OSD decoder.

</details>

### [Giant nonlinear conductivity in 2D electron gas from substrate-induced dipolar scattering](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20699v1)

**Authors:** Dmitry V. Chichinadze, Alexander Seidel, Zohar Nussinov  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20699v1>

<img src="2605.20699_figures/2605.20699_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<sub>Figure 2. (Left) Linear conductivity as a function of dipole moment. </details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes that the unexpectedly large nonlinear conductivity in 2D materials arises from electrons scattering off periodic electric dipoles in the substrate. The mechanism relies on a mathematical singularity in the scattering gradients, which naturally predicts the observed scale of 1 micro-ohm/volt.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing theoretical frameworks cannot quantitatively explain the massive second-order nonlinear conductivities observed in 2D van der Waals heterostructures.

**Main result.** The authors identify a mechanism where electron scattering from a substrate-induced periodic dipole array leads to a singular enhancement of the nonlinear response due to logarithmic divergences in scattering probability gradients.

**Method.** The study uses a Boltzmann equation approach, T-matrix calculations to leading order in the dipole moment, and symmetry analysis to determine the conductivity tensor structure.

**Model / system.** A 2D isotropic Fermi gas in the xy-plane subject to both conventional short-range impurity scattering and a static periodic array of electric dipoles originating from a substrate.

**Key observables.** Linear conductivity (sigma) and nonlinear conductivity tensor components (specifically longitudinal response B and transverse Hall response C).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dipole moment (d), impurity concentration (n_imp), Fermi momentum (kF), and the screening parameter (xi).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The 2D electron gas is assumed to be isotropic with a circular Fermi surface, and the dipole array is assumed to be periodic with uniform dipole moments and orientations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a schematic of electrons in a 2D gas scattering from substrate dipoles. Figure 2 displays linear conductivity scaling with d^2, nonlinear tensor components as a function of d, and the logarithmic enhancement of nonlinear conductivity with the screening parameter.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the puzzle of giant nonlinearity, defines the physical model of dipole scattering, performs symmetry analysis, derives the nonlinear conductivity using the Boltzmann equation and T-matrix, and concludes with the identification of the singular enhancement mechanism.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Despite a surge of interest in the nonlinear transport in 2D materials, a fundamental puzzle remains: existing theoretical frameworks are unable to quantitatively account for the giant nonlinear conductivities ($\gtrsim 1 \frac{μ\text{m}}{Ω\text{V}}$) recently reported in 2D van der Waals heterostructures. Here, we introduce a mechanism based on electron scattering from a substrate-induced periodic dipole array. We show that the strict kinematic constraints, inherent to 2D scattering, lead to a singular enhancement of the nonlinear response, fundamentally dictating a natural scale of $1 \frac{μ\text{m}}{Ω\text{V}}$.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.20334"></a>
### [Halving the cost of QROM](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20334v1)

**Authors:** Danial Motlagh, Matthew Pocrnic  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20334v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20334_figures/2605.20334_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Cost comparison of our improved QROM against the previous state-of-the-art. We plot the improvement factor of our algorithm as a function of the number of elements N being loaded for different bitstring length b and number of available dirty qubits. We plot the improvement factor for the 2 ends of the spectrum α = 1 (orange) and α = b (green) in the parametric family of methods provided in this work as well as for the optimal choice of α (red). The jagged lines at the beginning correspond to the qubit unconstrained regime where the optimal λ does not yet make use of the entire dirty qubit budget.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.20334_figures/2605.20334_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Comparison of the (a) “SelectSwap” QROM construction taken from Appendix A of Ref. [1] vs (b) our “SelectCopy” construction. Sel1 directly loads f(q, 0) into the clean register and f(q, r)⊕f(q, 0) into the rth dirty register for r = 1, ..., λ−1, Sel2 is similar but only goes over r = 1, ..., λ −1. This construction corresponds to α = 1 in the parametric family of methods provided in Section II D of this work.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.20334_figures/2605.20334_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Our QROM construction with sequential bit packets for the case of α = b. Note that the dirty qubit registers, as well as the qubits in the output registers, are now depicted as single qubit registers. Sel and Copy operations are now loading and copying single qubit states into the output register per iteration. Recall that following Sel1 operation, each subsequent Selj loads the XOR between the new data and the prior bit in each dirty register. Finally, the “Restore” operation refers to the final dirty-qubit clean-up, which is then copied into the output register to return the desired output. This operation is detailed in Appendix B. This new construction is now available in...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.20334_figures/2605.20334_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. A circuit construction of the Copy operation required to perform the Restore operation highlighted outlined in Figure 3. The Sel† operation simply unloads the previous data loaded into the dirty qubits, and the final copy uses the dirty qubit registers to clean up the data register as shown. The control on the |r⟩register is taken to be a single qubit control on the corresponding sawtooth wire from the unary iteration circuit in Figure 10 of Ref. Of course, in the full operation, µ temp-ANDs must be initialized for each outcome from unary iteration [8].</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a way to significantly reduce the gate complexity of the QROM subroutine, which is a major bottleneck in many quantum algorithms. By replacing the standard SelectSwap architecture with a SelectCopy approach, the authors effectively halve the Toffoli cost in certain regimes. This allows for much more efficient data loading using available dirty ancilla qubits.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The goal is to reduce the computational overhead, specifically the Toffoli gate cost, of the QROM (Quantum Read-Only Memory) subroutine in fault-tolerant quantum algorithms.

**Main result.** The authors achieve approximately a 50% reduction in the dominant Toffoli cost in the qubit-constrained regime, effectively matching the performance of clean-qubit QROM using dirty qubits.

**Method.** The paper introduces a 'SelectCopy' architecture to replace 'SelectSwap' and presents a parametric family of methods that treats a b-bit QROM as sequential smaller QROMs to optimize the cost based on available ancilla qubits.

**Model / system.** The work focuses on the algorithmic implementation of coherent table lookup (QROM) for loading N bitstrings of length b in superposition, utilizing both clean and dirty ancilla qubits.

**Key observables.** Toffoli gate count (non-Clifford cost) and the number of required ancilla qubits.

**Important parameters / regimes.** N (number of elements), b (bitstring length), lambda (sub-register parameter), and the availability of dirty vs. clean qubits.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Initial derivations assume N, b, and alpha are powers of 2; the efficiency is tied to the availability of dirty ancilla qubits.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares the cost improvement of the new QROM against the previous state-of-the-art; Figure 2 shows circuit diagrams for the SelectCopy and SelectSwap constructions; Figure 4 illustrates the circuit for the Copy operation in the Restore process.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the QROM bottleneck, introduces the SelectCopy architecture, presents an optimization for the qubit-constrained regime, provides a parametric family of methods for cost interpolation, and concludes with a generalized theorem for non-power-of-2 parameters.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Table lookup, often referred to as quantum read only memory (QROM), is one of the most widely used subroutines in quantum algorithms, and constitutes the majority share of algorithmic overheads in most practical applications of quantum computers. It involves the coherent loading of $N$ bitstrings of length $b$ in superposition, and naively has a non-Clifford cost of $N$ Toffolis. It is known that given access to $b\, λ$ dirty qubits, one can reduce the Toffoli cost of QROM to $2\frac{N}λ + 4b(λ- 1)$. In this work, we first present an optimization to reduce this cost to $2\frac{N}λ + 2b(λ- 1) + 2λ-6$ by replacing the ``SelectSwap" architecture with ``SelectCopy". We then provide a further optimization for the qubit-constrained regime where the Toffoli cost is typically $\sim 2\frac{N}λ$, and reduce it to $\sim (1+\frac{1}{b})\frac{N}λ$, cutting the cost by approximately $50\%$ and effectively matching the performance of clean-qubit QROM using dirty qubits for practical values of $b$. Lastly, we provide a parametric family of methods that allow the interpolation of the prefactor of the $\frac{N}λ $ term from $2$ to ($\, 1+\frac{1}{b}\,$) to obtain the best cost for different qubit availability regimes.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.20985"></a>
### [Hubbard-$U$-corrected electron-phonon interactions in strongly correlated materials via the finite-displacement method](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20985v1)

**Authors:** Jiale Chen, Youyou Tu, Chengliang Xia, Jin Zhao, Hanghui Chen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20985v1>

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.20985_figures/2605.20985_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Electron-phonon properties of 20% hole-doped LaNiO2. (a) Electron-phonon spec-</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.20985_figures/2605.20985_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. Electronic self-energy of 20% hole-doped LaNiO2 that arises from the electron-</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.20985_figures/2605.20985_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. Electronic properties of strained RuO2: (a-c) Band structure calculated at URu = 1,</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new algorithm that consistently applies Hubbard-U corrections to both electronic and phonon properties in strongly correlated materials. By applying this to nickelates and ruthenates, the authors show that such corrections are vital for accurately predicting structural stability and electron-phonon coupling strengths. This work helps resolve discrepancies between previous theoretical predictions and experimental observations of superconductivity.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study addresses the need for a consistent method to include Hubbard-U corrections in both electronic and lattice degrees of freedom to accurately model electron-phonon interactions in correlated materials.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that applying Hubbard-U corrections to the entire calculation (phonon spectrum and e-ph matrix elements) is essential to stabilize the structure of strained RuO2 and to reconcile theoretical e-ph coupling with experimental superconductivity temperatures in both nickelates and ruthenates.

**Method.** The researchers implemented an algorithm integrating the DFT+U method with the finite-displacement method using Numerical Atomic Orbital (NAO) basis sets to calculate phonons and electron-phonon g matrices.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on two prototypical correlated materials: 20% hole-doped infinite-layer nickelates (LaNiO2) and strained ruthenium dioxide (RuO2) thin films on TiO2 substrates.

**Key observables.** Electron-phonon coupling constant (lambda), phonon dispersion/spectrum, electron-phonon g matrices, superconducting transition temperature (Tc), and electronic self-energy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hubbard U parameters (U_Ni and U_Ru), hole-doping concentration, and substrate-induced strain.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The calculation of Tc assumes the Coulomb pseudopotential (mu*) is zero due to the explicit inclusion of the Hubbard U; the study also focuses on bands close to the Fermi level.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the evolution of electronic properties and Fermi surface for LaNiO2 with varying U; other figures illustrate the evolution of e-ph coupling, phonon DOS, and electronic self-energy.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the computational challenge, describes the implementation of the integrated DFT+U and finite-displacement algorithm, applies the method to LaNiO2 and RuO2 to analyze electronic and phonon-channel effects, and concludes with the necessity of self-consistent U-corrections.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Although the density functional theory plus Hubbard $U$ correction method (DFT+U) is broadly used to study electronic structure of strongly correlated materials, the extension of this method to electron-phonon $g$ matrices has received limited attention. Here, we implement an algorithm that integrates DFT+U method with the finite-displacement method for the calculations of phonons and electron-phonon $g$ matrices. The Hubbard $U$ corrections are applied not only to electronic and phonon structures, but, more importantly, also to electron-phonon $g$ matrices. We demonstrate our algorithm in two prototypical correlated materials: infinite-layer nickelates LaNiO$_2$ and ruthenium dioxide RuO$_2$. We find that: i) While the Hubbard $U$ corrections weakly increase the electron-phonon interaction of 20% hole-doped LaNiO$_2$, its total electron-phonon coupling remains small and is insufficient to account for the observed superconducting transition temperature of about 10-30 K. Our results contrast with the recent work showing that the full GW corrections yield an elevated electron-phonon coupling of 20% hole-doped LaNiO$_2$ five times larger than its DFT value. We attribute this discrepancy to the differences in the Fermi surface topology between DFT+$U$ and GW methods. ii) The inclusion of Hubbard $U$ corrections eliminates the imaginary phonon modes of RuO$_2$ under strain on the TiO$_2$ substrate and substantially reduces the electron-phonon coupling. Our results alleviate the discrepancy between the reported large theoretical electron-phonon coupling and the low superconducting transition temperature observed experimentally. Our work provides an algorithm that fully includes the Hubbard $U$ corrections on electron-phonon properties of correlated materials, and highlights the importance of Fermi surface shape and correlation effects on phonon spectrum and electron-phonon $g$ matrices.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Interpreting Bohm quantum potentials in Computing quantum waves exactly from classical action](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20443v1)

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a mathematical clarification for a method used to compute exact quantum waves from classical action. It proves that while the Bohm quantum potential is present in standard Madelung equations, it vanishes in this specific computational framework without affecting the final quantum wave. This resolves a technical dispute regarding the completeness of the underlying mathematical derivation.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses a technical critique claiming that a previous proof (Lemma 3.1) omitted the Bohm quantum potential term in the Madelung hydrodynamic equations.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that the Bohm quantum potential vanishes in their specific wave construction due to a specific initialization of density and action, and that the resulting overall wave remains independent of this computational initialization.

**Method.** The authors extend the previous proof by explicitly introducing the Bohm potential and using time rescaling to show the potential becomes zero when the Laplace-Beltrami operator is made space-independent.

**Model / system.** A mathematical framework for computing exact quantum waves from classical action using the Feynman kernel and Madelung partial differential equations.

**Key observables.** Action field (phi_j), classical density field (rho_j), and the resulting overall wave (psi).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The ratio of phi/hbar (noted as approximately 5 in a double-slit example); the regime is explicitly noted as being far from the quasi-classical limit.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The equivalence of operator and product forms of the Schrodinger equation relies on specific action and density initializations; the time transport solution is guaranteed only outside branch points.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by addressing a specific technical critique regarding a missing Bohm potential, provides an extended mathematical proof to incorporate the term, explains the mechanism for its vanishing via time rescaling, and concludes by verifying the correctness of the approach using a double-slit example.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The recent arXiv posting [11], commenting on the paper [7], argues that the proof of Lemma 3.1 in [7] is missing the Bohm quantum potential [1, 2] of the Madelung p.d.e. [9]. This short technical note extends the proof of Lemma 3.1 to introduce a Bohm quantum potential explicitly, and then shows why this term can be assumed to be zero in the wave construction, without loss of generality. The continuity p.d.e. and the Hamilton-Jacobi p.d.e., extended by the Bohm potential, are undisputed. However, the actual action and density solutions depend on their initialization at t = 0. In [7], this initialization is motivated by the Feynman kernel [4], which is fundamentally different from the standard initialization of the Madelung solution [9]. This in turn leads to different action and density solutions, and explains why in one case the Bohm quantum potential disappears and in the other does not. The resulting overall wave, however, is independent of this computational initialization.

</details>

### [Ligand-mediated Origin of Altermagnetic Spin-Splitting](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21284v1)

**Authors:** Luigi Camerano, Federico Bisti, Gianni Profeta  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21284v1>

**Summary.** This paper identifies that the spin-splitting in the altermagnet Co1/4NbSe2 arises from indirect hybridization via ligands rather than direct interaction between magnetic ions. By using Wannier-based Hamiltonian engineering, the authors demonstrate how anisotropy is transferred from cobalt to the itinerant niobium bands. This provides a microscopic blueprint for designing new altermagnetic materials for spintronics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Identifying the microscopic, real-space origin of altermagnetic spin-splitting in the metallic state of the $g$-wave altermagnet Co1/4NbSe2.

**Main result.** The study reveals that altermagnetic spin-splitting is driven by ligand-mediated hybridization (specifically the Co-Se-Nb pathway) rather than direct magnetic-ion hopping.

**Method.** First-principles DFT calculations combined with Wannier Hamiltonian engineering and real-space decomposition of hopping channels.

**Model / system.** The $g$-wave altermagnet Co1/4NbSe2, an intercalated transition metal dichallycogenide, modeled using a Wannier-based tight-binding Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Spin-resolved band structures, spin polarization, magnetization density, and momentum-dependent spin-splitting.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cutoff radii (rc1, rc2) for Hamiltonian truncation and a scaling parameter (lambda) for Co-Se hybridization strength.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The electronic structure can be accurately captured by a short-range tight-binding model derived from DFT.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows atom-resolved band structure and magnetization density; Fig 2 displays hopping amplitudes and unit cell; Fig 3 illustrates the evolution of the spectrum as different hopping terms (Nb-Nb, Se-Se, Nb-Co, Co-Se) are included.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the altermagnetic system, presents DFT and Wannier-based electronic structure results, performs a real-space decomposition of the Hamiltonian to isolate hopping contributions, and concludes with the identification of the ligand-mediated mechanism.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Altermagnets host spin-split electronic bands despite zero net magnetization, opening new routes for spintronics beyond conventional ferromagnets. Going beyond symmetry-based classifications, which specify allowed terms but not their hierarchy, here we use first-principles calculations and Wannier Hamiltonian engineering to uncover the microscopic bonding contributions of altermagnetic spin splitting in the $g$-wave altermagnet Co$_{1/4}$NbSe$_2$. We show that the splitting is captured by a short-range tight-binding model, establishing its local origin. By selectively controlling hopping channels, we demonstrate that the dominant contribution arises not from direct magnetic-ion hopping, but from ligand-mediated hybridization that transfers anisotropy to itinerant states. This identifies ligand-assisted coupling as the key mechanism of altermagnetic spin splitting and provides a microscopic bridge between minimal models and symmetry guided first-principles material searches, enabling real-space design of altermagnetic functionality.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper characterizes the magnetic properties of the breathing pyrochlore spinel AgInCr4S8, revealing an incommensurate helical spin structure. It demonstrates how controlling sulfur stoichiometry during growth can transition the material from a spin-freezing regime to long-range antiferromagnetic order. The study provides key insights into how cation ordering and geometric frustration interact in these complex lattices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the magnetism and structural ordering of the breathing pyrochlore spinel AgInCr4S8, specifically focusing on the impact of Ag/In cation ordering and growth conditions on its magnetic ground state.

**Main result.** The researchers confirmed an incommensurate helical magnetic ground state with a propagation vector k = (0, 0, 0.343) and identified that excess sulfur during crystal growth promotes long-range antiferromagnetic order.

**Method.** The study uses chemical vapor transport for crystal growth, followed by single-crystal X-ray and neutron diffraction, AC/DC magnetization, and specific heat capacity measurements.

**Model / system.** The system is a single-crystalline breathing pyrochlore spinel AgInCr4S8, characterized by a non-centrosymmetric lattice with alternating large and small Cr3+ tetrahedra driven by Ag/In ordering.

**Key observables.** Néel temperature (TN), propagation vector (k), magnetic entropy, magnetic moment (mu_eff), and breathing ratio (Br).

**Important parameters / regimes.** TN approx 9.6 K, Weiss temperature approx 56.8 K, propagation vector delta = 0.343, and breathing ratio Br = 1.065.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The magnetic model assumes a circular helix with equal sine and cosine amplitudes and assumes equal domain populations for the three symmetry-related domains.

**Figures summary.** Fig 2 shows structural/growth details; Fig 3 shows specific heat and entropy; Fig 4 presents a magnetic phase diagram; Fig 5 displays neutron diffraction intensity maps and a helical structure schematic; Fig 6 shows the temperature dependence of the incommensurability parameter.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with crystal growth and structural characterization, moves to magnetic property measurements (magnetization and specific heat), presents neutron diffraction results for the magnetic structure, and concludes with a discussion on growth-dependent stability and phase diagrams.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Single crystals of \ce{AgInCr4S8} were grown by chemical vapor transport and crystallographic ordering of Ag/In that results in a breathing pyrochlore motif of Cr$^{3+}$ was verified by x-ray and neutron diffraction. Long-range antiferromagnetic order is observed below a Néel temperature of $T_{\mathrm N}$ $\approx$ 9.6 K. The magnetic properties are characterized using ac and dc magnetization, specific heat capacity, and single crystal neutron diffraction measurements. The specific heat data are characterized by a small lambda anomaly near 9.5 K and the estimated magnetic entropy reaches $\approx$ $\frac{1}{3}$ of the expected value by 3$T_{\mathrm N}$, suggesting significant short-range order in the paramagnetic phase. Single crystal neutron diffraction evidences an incommensurate spin structure with propagation vector $\textbf{\textit{k}}$ = (0,0,$δ$) and $δ$ = 0.343 at 5 K. The minimal model that accounts for the data consists of ferromagnetic layers of Cr atoms, with magnetic moments lying in the plane of the layers and modulating in the perpendicular direction to form a helical structure propagating along $\textbf{\textit{k}}$. This study represents a rare investigation of single crystals within the family of breathing pyrochlore materials.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21326"></a>
### [Matching $A$ with $F$ in long-range QFTs](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21326v1)

**Authors:** Lorenzo Benfatto, Omar Zanusso  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21326v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21326_figures/2605.21326_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. The three different phases of the critical point in the long range Ising model. The dashed line is the curve s∗(d) = 2−ηSR(d), interpolated by with the known behaviors in the integer dimensions d = 1, 2, 3 and the perturbative expansion below d = 4.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proves that the Renormalization Group flow for long-range, non-unitary scalar field theories follows a gradient structure up to the third loop order. By matching the RG-derived A-function with the sphere free-energy, the authors provide a perturbative proof of the F-theorem for these models. This establishes a fundamental connection between the flow of couplings and the thermodynamic properties of the theory at fixed points.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether the Renormalization Group (RG) flow in non-unitary, long-range quantum field theories possesses a gradient structure and satisfies monotonicity theorems like the A-theorem and F-theorem.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that the RG flow of long-range multiscalar phi^4 theory satisfies a gradient structure up to the third loop order and show that the A-function can be matched to the sphere free-energy, providing a perturbative proof of the F-theorem at leading order.

**Method.** The study employs perturbative RG analysis up to the third loop order, conformal perturbation theory on a d-dimensional sphere, and Mellin-Barnes integral representations for evaluating Feynman diagrams.

**Model / system.** The model is a long-range multiscalar phi^4 theory characterized by power-law interactions, including specific cases like the long-range vector O(N) and hypercubic H_N models.

**Key observables.** Beta functions, A-function (conformal anomaly), F-function (sphere free-energy), Zamolodchikov's metric (C_IJ), and the RG metric (G_IJ).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spacetime dimension d, interaction parameter s (where s = (d+epsilon)/2), coupling constants lambda, and the epsilon expansion parameter.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to the third loop order in perturbation theory and assumes the validity of the epsilon expansion near the critical dimension.

**Figures summary.** Not specified in the provided notes.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the gradient flow problem, proceeds to analyze the RG flow and beta functions of the long-range phi^4 theory up to three loops, computes the sphere free-energy and the metric on theory space, and concludes by matching these quantities to establish the F-theorem.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Unitarity is often a crucial ingredient in establishing theorems -- such as the $A$-theorem -- on the irreversibility of the renormalization group flow. The strongest thesis of this type of theorems would be that there exists a scalar function $A$ and a positive definite metric $G_{IJ}$ in the space of couplings such that the renormalization group flow satisfies a gradient equation, $\partial_I A= G_{IJ}β^J$, in which case $A$ is locally monotonic along the flow. In this paper we consider the long-range multiscalar $φ^4$ theory, which is a nonunitary model that is believed to be conformally invariant at fixed points, and show that its renormalization group flow satisfies the gradient structure up to the third loop order in the coupling. We also show that $A$ and $G_{IJ}$ can be matched to the leading nontrivial order with the sphere free-energy $\tilde{F}$ and Zamolodchikov's metric $C_{IJ}$ of the corresponding conformal theory concentrating on the examples of the long-range vector $O(N)$ and hypercubic $H_N$ models. Our results imply a perturbative proof of the $\tilde{F}$-theorem at the leading nontrivial order. We conclude the paper discussing briefly whether this result should hold to the next orders in perturbation theory.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21402"></a>
### [Memorisation, convergence and generalisation in generative models](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21402v1)

**Authors:** Antoine Maillard, Sebastian Goldt  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21402v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21402_figures/2605.21402_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Memorisation, convergence, and latent recovery in generative models. Left: We illus- trate three regimes in generative models by sketching the principal components (top) and the covariance spectra (bottom) of the true data distribution (blue) and a distribution learnt from n samples (red) in d dimensions. For very small data sets, n = Θ(1), the model does not learn a smooth approximation of the data distribution, but memorises the training set. As the number of training samples becomes comparable to the input dimension, n ≍d, the spectra continuously converge and independently trained models produce similar outputs given the same latents, as described by Kadkhodaie et al. [1]....</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21402_figures/2605.21402_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Convergence and latent recovery govern different statistical distances between true and learnt distributions P⋆and ˆP. Left: Rescaled Kullback-Leibler divergence between P⋆and ˆP for two regularisation strengths σ, as a function of sample complexity γ = n/d, where n is the number of samples and d is the input dimension. The dashed lines show the upper and lower bounds of eq. (9), which only depend on the convergence overlap q. Right: Maximum-sliced distance between P⋆and ˆP (solid line), which decays asymptotically with the subspace overlap as √1 −Q, see eq. (10) (dashed line). Parameters: β = 4.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21402_figures/2605.21402_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: A rotated subspace overlap identifies a phase transition on data with power-law spectrum. </details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21402_figures/2605.21402_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Convergence and subspace recovery in diffusion models. We plot the convergence overlap q, the naïve subspace overlap Q and the rotated subspace overlap Q⋆in the Fourier basis, eqs. (5), (7) and (14) respectively, for images sampled from diffusion models trained on CelebA. (a) Images sampled from a vanilla UNet denoiser that we trained independently on increasing large disjoint subsets of CelebA. </details>

**Summary.** The paper provides a mathematical framework to show that generative models can learn to match the general statistics of a dataset (convergence) without necessarily recovering its key structural features (latent factors). It identifies a clear decoupling between these two stages of learning, which is crucial for understanding the true capabilities of diffusion models.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the transition from memorization to generalization in generative models and determining if model convergence is a reliable proxy for recovering underlying data structures.

**Main result.** Generalization decomposes into two decoupled objectives: matching the bulk of the data distribution (convergence) and recovering principal latent factors (subspace recovery), with the former occurring at lower sample complexity.

**Method.** Analytical characterization using Random Matrix Theory, specifically the Marchenko-Pastur law and the BBP transition, applied to spiked Wishart models and extended to power-law spectra.

**Model / system.** Linear generative models using a Gaussian process where the covariance is a spiked Wishart model (Sigma = I + beta * u * u^T), later extended to power-law spectral models and real-world datasets like CelebA and ImageNet.

**Key observables.** Memorization (m), Convergence overlap (q), Subspace recovery (Q), and Rotated subspace overlap (Q*).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Sample complexity ratio (gamma = n/d), signal-to-noise ratio (beta), regularization strength (sigma), and power-law exponent (alpha).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary analytical results rely on Gaussian approximations for both the data distribution and the generative models, and focus on the high-dimensional limit.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the three regimes (memorization, convergence, latent recovery) and the evolution of order parameters; Figure 3 shows the effectiveness of the rotated metric Q* in identifying phase transitions in power-law data.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of memorization vs. generalization, develops an exact theory for linear Gaussian models using RMT, extends the framework to power-law spectra to account for real-world image statistics, and validates the findings using diffusion models and empirical datasets.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Generative neural networks learn how to produce highly realistic images from a large, but finite number of examples - or do they simply memorise their training set? To settle this question, Kadkhodaie, Guth, Simoncelli and Mallat (ICLR '24) trained diffusion models independently on disjoint subsets of a dataset and showed that they converge to nearly the same density when the number of training images is large enough. This result raises two basic questions: how much data do you need for convergence, and what does convergence capture about learning the data distribution? Here, we address these questions by providing an exact analytical characterisation of the transition from memorisation to generalisation in linear generative models. We find that these models memorise at small load, while convergence emerges continuously when the number of samples is linear in the input dimension. Strikingly, we find that convergence is insensitive to recovery of the principal latent factors of the data, which are recovered in a sharp transition. After extending our approach to data with power-law spectra, we find the same distinction between convergence and latent recovery in our experiments with convolutional denoisers and in the data of Kadkhodaie et al. We thus show that generalisation in generative models decomposes into at least two distinct objectives: matching the bulk of the data distribution and recovering the principal latent factors. These objectives correspond to two different distances between true and learnt data distribution, and only the first one is captured by convergence.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21021"></a>
### [Microscopic Nonaffine Deformation Theory of LAOS in Polymers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21021v1)

**Authors:** Dario Nichetti, Alessio Zaccone  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21021v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21021_figures/2605.21021_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Experimental master curve of the normalized nonlinear elastic contribution, NLI = −G′ NL/G′ 1, plotted as a function of the normalized strain amplitude γ0/γLR 0 . Symbols are experimental data from the literature [11], while the solid line is the theoretical crossover fit given by Eq. (83). The data include polymers with markedly different molecular architectures, including predominantly linear linear-low-density polyethylene (LLDPE), highly branched low-density polyethylene (LDPE), randomly branched emulsion-polymerized styrene–butadiene rubber (e- SBR), and solution-polymerized partially coupled styrene–butadiene rubber (s-SBR). Despite these architectural differences, the...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a microscopic molecular basis for the nonlinear viscoelasticity observed in polymers during large-amplitude oscillations. It reinterprets the Nonlinearity Index as a measure of how structural constraints break down, linking polymer physics to the broader theory of nonaffine deformations in glasses.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Bridging the gap between tube-based polymer dynamics and the microscopic nonaffine theory of disordered elasticity to explain the origin of nonlinearities in Large-Amplitude Oscillatory Shear (LAOS).

**Main result.** The authors establish that the Nonlinearity Index (NLI) is a dynamic nonaffinity parameter measuring the transfer of elastic response from coherent tube elasticity to nonlinear relaxation channels, predicting a theoretical upper bound of |NLI|max = 3.

**Method.** The study uses a multiscale framework combining Fourier-transform rheology, nonaffine lattice dynamics, and a constraint-counting argument based on the eight-chain network model.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of entangled polymer melts and concentrated solutions, modeled using a combination of Doi-Edwards tube theory and nonaffine deformation theory for amorphous solids.

**Key observables.** Nonlinearity Index (NLI), first-harmonic storage modulus (G'1), nonlinear modulus (G'NL), and the tube survival fraction (q).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Characteristic strain amplitude (gamma_c), maximum nonlinearity (Nmax), crossover exponent (m), and the nonaffine isostatic threshold.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework assumes a mapping between tube constraint loss and cage-breaking, and uses an eight-chain representation to estimate mechanical coordination.

**Figures summary.** The paper includes experimental master curves for the NLI, scaling plots of the modulus components, and a summary table of effective scaling regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of connecting tube theory to nonaffine elasticity, develops a mathematical framework for LAOS harmonics, proposes a crossover scaling law, performs experimental fitting, and concludes with a theoretical derivation of the NLI upper bound.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop a molecularly motivated framework connecting large-amplitude oscillatory shear (LAOS) nonlinearities in entangled polymers to frequency-dependent nonaffine relaxation in disordered solids. The central idea is that the first harmonic in LAOS measures the residual phase-locked elastic response, whereas the higher harmonics encode the Fourier signature of strain-dependent nonaffine relaxation. The finite-amplitude modulus is interpreted as a local tangent stiffness of the evolving microstructure, in the spirit of elastoplastic and incremental nonaffine models. For entangled polymers, the analogue of the decreasing coordination number in cage-breaking theories of glass mechanics is identified not with the tube-orientation tensor itself, but with the fraction of surviving tube constraints. This distinction leads naturally to a crossover description controlled by a characteristic strain amplitude $γ_c$, rather than by universal fixed power-law exponents. The fitted value $N_{\max}\simeq1.72$ indicates that the present experimental data approach a strong but not fully saturated nonlinear state, remaining below the ideal limiting value predicted for complete constraint collapse. Finally, a constraint-counting argument combining an eight-chain affine network representation with the central-force nonaffine isostatic threshold gives a limiting estimate $|\mathrm{NLI}|_{\max}=3$. The results support the interpretation of the NLI as a Fourier-resolved dynamic nonaffinity parameter and establish a bridge between tube-based polymer dynamics, LAOS harmonic analysis, elastoplastic rheology, and microscopic nonaffine lattice dynamics.

</details>

### [Modeling and Resource Optimization for Quantum Oracles](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21380v1)

**Authors:** Zhihang Li, Bo Zhao, Chuanbing Han, Jie Zhao, Jinchen Xu, Guoqiang Shu, Yimin Gao, Woji He, Zheng Shan

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>Fig. 1. Framework for constructing quantum oracles using the qubit mul- tiplexing technique. (a) Conversion between XOR and phase oracles using multi-controlled X (MCX) and Z (MCZ) gates. (b) Evaluation of three constraint functions via qubit multiplexing. Each function is first encoded into an auxiliary qubit. The results are then merged onto a target qubit using an MCX gate, after which the auxiliary qubits are reset for reuse. (c) Hierarchical composition of single-function and multi-function oracles, where smaller modules are recursively combined into higher-level structures, thereby reducing the required number of auxiliary qubits.</sub>

The experimental results demonstrate how the circuit depth decreases as more auxiliary qubits (i.e., greater space cost) are allocated, illustrating the trade-off between circuit depth and qubit count.</sub>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new framework and algorithm for designing more efficient quantum oracles. By using a hierarchical recursive model, the authors can systematically balance the use of auxiliary qubits against circuit depth, significantly reducing the computational cost of essential quantum algorithms.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The optimization of quantum oracle construction to reduce resource overhead, specifically minimizing quantum gate complexity and circuit depth while managing the trade-off between qubit count and depth.

**Main result.** The proposed ASDT algorithm is theoretically proven to achieve optimal gate counts for a given number of qubits and experimentally reduces average quantum circuit depth by 53.99% compared to the W-cycle approach.

**Method.** The authors introduce the Hierarchical Recursive Synthesis-Evaluation (HRSE) model to formally describe oracle architectures and the Adaptive Space-depth Trade-off (ASDT) algorithm to optimize their structure.

**Model / system.** The work focuses on Boolean functions f: {0, 1}^n -> {0, 1} represented as quantum oracles, using a hierarchical tree-based model (HRSE) where nodes represent computation units and edges represent recursive containment.

**Key observables.** Quantum gate complexity (gate count), quantum circuit depth, and the optimization ratio compared to baseline methods.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of variables (n), number of auxiliary qubits (k/m), recursion depth (level), and the trade-off between space (qubits) and depth (time).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes the monotonicity of node size and that nodes with size 1 or 2 must be leaf nodes; it also focuses on the organization of functions rather than specific function encoding.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows oracle conversion and qubit multiplexing; Figure 2 illustrates the HRSE model; Figure 3 plots node depth vs. function count; Figure 4 shows the ASDT algorithm execution; Figure 5 displays the space-depth trade-off bar graph.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of oracle overhead, presents the HRSE mathematical model, details the ASDT algorithm and its optimality proof, provides experimental comparisons against the W-cycle approach, and discusses future optimization directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum computing has demonstrated its significant advantage over supercomputing for specific applications and shown promising prospect, such as machine learning, cryptography, finance, etc.. Quantum oracles are very common in many quantum algorithms and oracle resource consumption directly affects algorithm performance. However, existing oracle designs often exhibit high resource overhead and limited compatibility. Moreover, structured description tools and complexity analysis methods are lacked. In this work, we introduces a Hierarchical Recursive Synthesis-Evaluation (HRSE) model, enabling formal description and precise quantum gate complexity analysis of oracles. Based on this model, we propose an Adaptive Space-depth Trade-off (ASDT) algorithm for generating oracle structures under a fixed qubit constraint. We provide a theoretical proof showing that the ASDT algorithm achieves the optimal gate count for a given number of qubits. Experimental results show that the ASDT algorithm reduces the average quantum circuit depth by 53.99% compared with the W-cycle approach, with the number of variables being 10, 15, and 20, respectively.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new machine learning interatomic potential that enables large-scale simulations of the thermal evolution of polymer-derived ceramics. It successfully captures the phase separation of carbon into graphene-like sheets within an amorphous SiCN matrix, providing a microscopic view of how structural defects mediate the formation of stable aromatic networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of modeling the atomic-scale evolution and phase separation mechanisms of carbon domains within amorphous silicon carbonitride (SiCNH) ceramics during thermal processing.

**Main result.** The development of a highly accurate machine learning interatomic potential (MACE) that reveals how carbon domains nucleate and grow into graphene-like sheets via defect-mediated processes involving 5- and 7-member rings.

**Method.** Development of an equivariant machine learning interatomic potential (MACE) trained on a diverse database of 9,000+ configurations, followed by large-scale molecular dynamics simulations (8,000 atoms) and thermal annealing cycles.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a polymer-derived silicon carbonitride hydride (Si-C-N-H) ceramic, characterized by an amorphous SiCN matrix and a separate free-carbon phase.

**Key observables.** Atomic pair distribution functions (PDFs), structural motifs (carbon ring sizes, dimers, chains, and sheets), and local coordination numbers (Si, C, N).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Simulation scales of 8,000 atoms and nanosecond timescales; thermal annealing temperatures up to 2200 K; MLIP energy RMSE of 12.4 meV/atom.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of a DFT-based training set (PBE functional) assumes that the accuracy of the MLIP is sufficient to capture the complex chemical reactivity and long-range structural organization of the amorphous network.

**Figures summary.** The figures illustrate the structural diversity of the training database, the thermal annealing protocols, the evolution of carbon structural motifs (rings and chains), and the validation of the model against experimental pair distribution functions.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the limitations of previous computational methods, details the construction of a diverse training database and the MACE potential, describes the large-scale MD simulation protocols, presents the results of phase separation and carbon growth, and validates the findings against experimental scattering data.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Polymer-derived ceramics combine the thermal stability of ceramics with the versatile properties of carbon domains, but modeling their atomic-scale evolution during processing remains elusive due to the limitations of traditional computational methods. To address this issue, here we develop and apply a machine learning interatomic potential for silicon carbonitride-based (Si-C-N-H) systems, trained on a diversified database of over 9000 configurations -including amorphous models, high-temperature states, surfaces, and crystal structure predictions - to capture the full complexity of these materials. This potential enables large-scale molecular dynamics simulations of 8000-atom systems revealing the atomic-scale evolution of the polymer-derived ceramic during thermal treatment. A key result of this work is the occurrence of a phase separation where carbon domains progressively nucleate from the amorphous SiCN matrix during thermal processing, forming distinct graphene-like sheets while preserving the integrity of the ceramic network. The resulting models reproduce the experimental atomic pair distribution functions with exceptional fidelity, validating our approach and providing microscopic explanations for the material unique combination of ceramic and graphitic properties. In this process, defective 5- and/or 7-member carbon rings, mediate the transformation to stable 6-member aromatic structures. These findings offer new atomic-scale insights into the thermal stability and structural transformation pathways of polymer-derived ceramics, while our methodology opens avenues for studying complex amorphous systems with first-principles accuracy at experimentally relevant scales.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.20321"></a>
### [Poles-zeros duality in semi-holographic Mott insulators](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20321v1)

**Authors:** Thomas Kögel, Alessio Caddeo, Amelie Pitters, Francesca Paoletti, Lorenzo Crippa, Giorgio Sangiovanni, René Meyer, Johanna Erdmenger  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20321v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20321_figures/2605.20321_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Comparison of log | det GR(ω, k, η)| for opposite-sign values of the bulk coupling, η = 5 (metallic, in panel (a)) and η = −5 (Mott insulating, in panel (b)). Bright yellow regions indicate peaks in the quantity, while dark purple regions correspond to zeros. The horizontal dashed lines represent the Fermi level which is ωF ≈−1.2 for the metallic phase (left) while it is fixed in the middle of the gap, i.e. at ωF ≈−1.3 in the Mott insulator (right). The poles and zeros of | det GR(ω, k, η)| exhibit an approximate one-to-one correspondence over the displayed range of momenta and frequencies.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.20321_figures/2605.20321_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: For negative values of η, the system is a Mott insulator and here we plot its gap size as a function of η &lt; 0. The gap opens at η = −0.17 for the cutoff 10−2 and at η = −0.51 for the 10−5 cutoff. The datapoints are determined from the average of the result for the gap for the two different cutoffs, while the error bars are given by the deviation from the average. For larger negative values of η the two criteria converge towards the same value due to the spectral features becoming sharper.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper uses a semi-holographic framework to explain the poles-zeros duality observed in the transition to Mott-insulating phases. It shows that the zeros in the Green's function (Luttinger surfaces) arise from the poles of the self-energy, which are linked to collective excitations in a strongly coupled sector. The study provides a clear mapping between the insulating and metallic phases through the choice of holographic quantization.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the physical mechanism behind the poles-zeros duality in the Green's functions of Mott insulators and the emergence of Luttinger surfaces.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that a poles-zeros duality in the self-energy, driven by a bulk scalar coupling, induces an approximate duality in the single-particle Green's function, effectively mapping the Mott-insulating phase to a metallic phase.

**Method.** A semi-holographic approach using the AdS/CFT correspondence, involving the calculation of retarded Green's functions in an AdS Reissner-Nordstrom black hole background and analyzing the transition between standard and alternative quantizations.

**Model / system.** A semi-holographic model consisting of a fundamental Dirac fermion coupled to a strongly interacting large-N sector, where the latter is described by a 3+1D gravitational theory with a non-minimal scalar coupling.

**Key observables.** Retarded Green's function (G_R), self-energy (Sigma_R), spectral function (rho), and the determinant of the Green's function.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Non-minimal scalar coupling parameter (eta), large-N limit, chemical potential (mu), and temperature (T).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The large-N limit is used to treat the strongly coupled sector as a bath and neglect backreaction on the gravitational background; the probe approximation is applied to the bulk fermion.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the self-energy magnitude; Figure 2 compares the determinant of the Green's function; Figure 3 shows the determinant at k=0 identifying poles and zeros; Figure 4 relates the determinant to the self-energy; Figure 5 shows the Mott gap size vs eta; Figures 6 and 7 show the real and imaginary parts of the Green's function in the insulating and metallic regimes, respectively.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of Green's function zeros, defines the semi-holographic model and its coupling, derives the analytical poles-zeros duality for the self-energy via quantization schemes, performs numerical analysis of the spectral functions and phase transitions, and discusses the physical implications and limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Inspired by the poles-zeros duality of Green's functions that appears in transitions into Mott-insulating phases in strongly correlated condensed matter systems, we propose a semi-holographic approach to Mott insulators. In this model, a fundamental fermion is coupled to a large-$N$, strongly interacting sector that generates a self-energy for the fundamental fermion's Green's function. This coupling amounts to a hybridization of the fundamental fermion with a strongly correlated fermionic composite. Within the holographic framework, at large $N$, the Green's function of the composite fermion naturally exhibits a poles-zeros duality. Zeros of the Green's function are caused by the poles of the self-energy that correspond to collective many-body excitations of the holographic strongly interacting sector. We calculate the spectral function of the fundamental fermion, from which we characterize the semi-holographic metallic and the Mott-insulating phases. In addition to the new physical interpretation of the zeros, our analysis yields a well-defined picture of the poles-zeros duality in terms of the freedom to choose between standard and alternative quantization in the strongly coupled sector.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.20637"></a>
### [PUBO Formulation for MST and Application to Optimum-Path Forest](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20637v1)

**Authors:** Guilherme E. L. Pexe, Lucas A. M. Rattighieri, Leandro A. Passos, Danilo S. Jodas, Douglas Rodrigues, Felipe F. Fanchini, João P. Papa, Kelton A. P. Costa

<sub>Figure 3 presents the results of FALQON execution on the graph from Figure 2,</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.20637_figures/2605.20637_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Results of FALQON execution on the MST problem. (a) Evolution of the expected value of the</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.20637_figures/2605.20637_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4 shows the results of applying FALQON to the MST task formulated as</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a quantum-inspired method to optimize the selection of prototypes in the Optimum-Path Forest classifier. By reformulating the Minimum Spanning Tree problem as a PUBO task and using the FALQON algorithm, the authors achieve performance parity with classical algorithms while improving scalability for quantum hardware. This demonstrates a potential path for using quantum optimization to enhance machine learning tasks.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The scalability of the Optimum-Path Forest (OPF) classifier is limited by the quadratic complexity of computing the Minimum Spanning Tree (MST) for large datasets. The authors seek to overcome this computational burden using a quantum-inspired optimization approach.

**Main result.** The proposed PUBO formulation using the FALQON algorithm achieves classification accuracies comparable to the classical Prim's algorithm while reducing the required number of qubits and auxiliary variables.

**Method.** The authors reformulate the MST problem as a Polynomial Unconstrained Binary Optimization (PUBO) task and apply the Feedback-Based Quantum Optimization (FALQON) algorithm for Hamiltonian minimization.

**Model / system.** The model is a supervised OPF classifier where training samples are represented as nodes in a fully connected graph with edge weights based on Euclidean distances. The problem is mapped to a PUBO Hamiltonian (H_MST) consisting of a penalty term to ensure a valid tree structure and a cost term to minimize total edge weight.

**Key observables.** Success rate of finding the ground state, frequency of the true solution in the top 10 most probable states, energy evolution (expectation value of the Hamiltonian), and probability distribution of the computational basis states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Penalty parameters A and B, time step delta_t = 0.01, and 10,000 layers for FALQON convergence.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes that the MST can be effectively captured by a PUBO formulation and relies on small-scale dataset subsets for simulation due to current hardware/computational constraints.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the end-to-end process flowchart; Figure 2 displays a sample graph and its MST; Figure 3 shows energy decrease and probability distributions; Figure 4 compares QUBO results with different driver strategies; Figure 5 compares PUBO results.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the OPF framework, proposes a PUBO formulation for MST, describes the FALQON algorithm and its implementation, presents experimental results on various datasets comparing accuracy and execution time, and discusses limitations regarding scalability and local minima.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Optimum-Path Forest is a graph-based framework for designing classifiers that exploit inter-sample connectivity. A particular variant constructs decision boundaries based on prototypes computed by a Minimum Spanning Tree (MST) over the training data, which might become prohibitive for large-scale datasets. In this context, Quantum Machine Learning has emerged as a promising approach to overcome the high computational burden of combinatorial problems. We propose a quantum-inspired approach for prototype selection in OPF classifiers by reformulating the MST problem as a Polynomial Unconstrained Binary Optimization (PUBO) task and further employing the Feedback-Based Quantum Optimization (FALQON) algorithm for Hamiltonian minimization. The PUBO formulation reduces the need for qubits and eliminates the need for auxiliary variables, thereby addressing scalability constraints in current quantum hardware. Experiments on real-world datasets demonstrate that the FALQON-optimized MST achieves accuracies comparable to those of the classical Prim's algorithm while maintaining prototype quality. While FALQON occasionally reached local minima, it did not significantly impact the accuracy of the prototype selection process.

</details>

### [Q-SYNTH: Hybrid Quantum-Classical Adversarial Augmentation for Imbalanced Fraud Detection](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21164v1)
