<sub>Fig. 3: Quantum generator circuit. It starts with angle embedding, followed by trainable single-qubit rotations (RX, RY, RZ) and a ring of CNOT entangling gates. The circuit ends with Pauli-Z measurements on all qubits.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21164_figures/2605.21164_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4: Generator loss over training epochs.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21164_figures/2605.21164_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5: Discriminator loss over training epochs.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces Q-SYNTH, a hybrid quantum-classical GAN designed to augment imbalanced datasets for fraud detection. It demonstrates that a quantum generator can produce synthetic samples that better match the real data distribution than classical GANs while remaining effective for downstream classification tasks.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Extreme class imbalance in credit card fraud detection causes supervised learners to favor the majority class, leading to poor recall for rare fraudulent transactions.

**Main result.** Q-SYNTH provides a favorable compromise between distributional fidelity and downstream performance, reducing marginal distribution mismatch compared to classical GANs while maintaining competitive fraud detection utility.

**Method.** A hybrid quantum-classical Generative Adversarial Network (GAN) where a parameterized quantum circuit (PQC) acts as the generator and a classical neural network acts as the discriminator.

**Model / system.** A hybrid architecture using a 6-qubit variational quantum circuit with a StronglyEntanglingLayers ansatz, implemented via PennyLane, to synthesize tabular fraud data.

**Key observables.** Kolmogorov-Smirnov (KS) statistics, Wasserstein distances, AUC-ROC for detectability, and downstream metrics including Precision, Recall, and F1-score.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Synthetic-to-real injection ratios (10%, 25%, 50%, 100%), dimensionality reduction to d=4, and adaptive regularization parameters (noise scale, label smoothing, and dropout).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes data can be effectively represented in a bounded, low-dimensional continuous space; assumes the quantum generator's parameter efficiency is comparable to classical counterparts.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the trade-off between similarity and utility across different augmentation methods; Figure 2 outlines the end-to-end Q-SYNTH pipeline; Figure 3 details the quantum circuit architecture; Figures 4 and 5 show training loss trajectories; Figure 6 presents the ROC curve for synthetic sample detectability.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of class imbalance, proposes the Q-SYNTH hybrid framework, details the architecture and preprocessing pipeline, describes the adversarial training and regularization methods, presents quantitative results on distributional fidelity and downstream detection, and concludes with scaling analysis and limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Credit card fraud detection is fundamentally challenged by extreme class imbalance, where fraudulent transactions are rare yet operationally critical. This imbalance often biases supervised learners toward the legitimate class, leading to high overall accuracy but weaker fraud-class recall and F1-score. This paper introduces Q-SYNTH, a hybrid classical--quantum generative adversarial framework in which a parameterized quantum circuit serves as the generator and a classical neural network serves as the discriminator. Q-SYNTH is designed for minority-class fraud synthesis in tabular data and is evaluated along two dimensions: statistical fidelity to real fraud samples and downstream performance for fraud detection. To this end, generated samples are assessed using distributional similarity measures based on Kolmogorov-Smirnov statistics and Wasserstein distances, real-vs-synthetic detectability measured by AUC-ROC, and downstream classification performance across both quantum and classical classifiers. Under the reported protocol, Q-SYNTH reduces marginal distribution mismatch relative to a classical GAN baseline while maintaining competitive downstream fraud-detection performance. Although SMOTE achieves the strongest feature-wise similarity and the classical GAN attains the highest downstream performance in several settings, Q-SYNTH offers a favorable compromise between distributional fidelity and downstream performance, supporting the feasibility of hybrid quantum augmentation for imbalanced fraud detection.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21486"></a>
### [Quantifying Hyperparameter Transfer and the Importance of Embedding Layer Learning Rate](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21486v1)

**Authors:** Dayal Singh Kalra, Maissam Barkeshli  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21486v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21486_figures/2605.21486_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Computing the three transfer metrics for µP. (a) Loss vs. log learning rate ν, with star marking the optimum ν∗(n), (b) Joint fit of the loss model (Equation (6), dashed lines), with a low predictability error E = 0.0034 , (c) Loss curves in the normalized coordinates (Equation (8)), with κ = −2.640 indicating robust transfer. (d-f) Scaling laws for optimal loss L∗(n), optimal log-learning-rate ν∗(n), and curvature H(n). In (d), the orange curve shows the best loss across parameterizations at each width, used for estimating the asymptotic loss gap R(∞).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21486_figures/2605.21486_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Embedding layer learning rate is the critical difference between SP and µP. Loss vs. log learning rate ν across widths for: (a) SP with Θ(1/n) embedding learning rate; (b) SP modified to use Θ(1) embedding learning rate (SP+Embd); (c) µP modified to use Θ(1/n) embedding learning rate (µP-Embd). Speeding up the embedding in SP eliminates training instabilities and yields smooth, µP-like curves, while slowing it down in µP reintroduces SP-like instabilities. These interventions isolate the embedding learning rate as the primary driver of µP’s advantage, and show that training it fast enough is critical for stable training.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21486_figures/2605.21486_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Transfer metrics for parameterizations interpolating between SP and µP. Parameterizations with ‘+’ denote incremental changes from SP towards µP, while ‘−’ denotes changes from µP towards SP. Green and red regions indicate desirable and undesirable regimes, respectively. The orange arrow highlights SP+Embd (SP with Θ(1) embedding learning rate), which matches µP across all three metrics, suggesting the embedding layer learning rate is the primary driver of µP’s advantage.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21486_figures/2605.21486_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: (a) Reducing the embedding learning rate from Θ(1) to Θ(1/n) in µP at step tswitch causes a persistent loss gap that grows larger for earlier switches (inset). (b) Increasing it from Θ(1/n) to Θ(1) in SP at step tswitch eliminates training instabilities and improves performance over SP (tswitch = 104).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21486_figures/2605.21486_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Effect of weight decay on the three transfer metrics in the fixed-step (top) and fixed TPP (bottom) settings. In the fixed-step setting, weight decay improves predictability error E at the cost of asymptotic performance R(∞). In the TPP setting, stable parameterizations achieve near-zero R(∞), and landscape predictability trends are similar, but robustness κ degrades as weight decay increases.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a formal framework to quantify how well hyperparameters can be transferred from small to large neural networks. It discovers that the success of the popular muP parameterization is largely due to its ability to stabilize the training of the embedding layer. The work also explores how weight decay affects the predictability and robustness of scaling laws.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the mechanisms behind hyperparameter transferability in neural networks, specifically comparing the effectiveness of Maximal Update Parameterization (muP) versus Standard Parameterization (SP) when scaling model width.

**Main result.** The authors identify that the primary driver of muP's superior learning rate transfer is the maximization of the embedding layer learning rate, which prevents training instabilities found in SP.

**Method.** The study uses a quantitative framework with three new metrics (scaling law fit quality, transfer robustness, and asymptotic loss penalty) and a series of 16 ablation studies on Transformer and CNN architectures.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on decoder-only Transformers and CNNs, analyzing scaling behavior as model width (n) increases, using the AdamW optimizer and the FineWeb-Edu dataset.

**Key observables.** Scaling law fit error (E), transfer robustness exponent (kappa), and asymptotic loss penalty (R(infinity)).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Model width (n), learning rate (eta), weight decay (lambda), and the abcd parameterization exponents controlling forward pass, initialization, and learning rate scaling.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumptions include a width-independent training horizon, the 'Full Alignment Assumption' for simplifying scaling exponents, and specific scaling for input/loss derivatives.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the calculation of transfer metrics; Figure 2 demonstrates how adjusting the embedding layer learning rate in SP mimics muP; Figure 3 shows transfer metrics across parameterizations; Figure 4 illustrates loss gaps and stability; Figure 5 shows the impact of weight decay in fixed-step and compute-optimal regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces a quantification framework for hyperparameter transfer, presents an ablation study isolating the embedding layer's role, provides a mathematical derivation of stability conditions for SGD and Adam, and concludes with an analysis of weight decay and compute-optimal scaling.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Hyperparameter transfer allows extrapolating optimal optimization hyperparameters from small to large scales, making it critical for training large language models (LLMs). This is done either by fitting a scaling law to the hyperparameters or by a judicious choice of parameterization, such as Maximal Update ($μ$P), that renders optimal hyperparameters approximately scale invariant. In this paper, we first develop a framework to quantify hyperparameter transfer through three metrics: (1) the quality of the scaling law fit, (2) the robustness to extrapolation errors, and (3) the asymptotic loss penalty due to choice of parameterization. Next, we investigate through a comprehensive series of ablations why $μ$P appears to offer high-quality learning rate transfer relative to standard parameterization (SP), as existing theory is inadequate. We find that the overwhelming benefit of $μ$P relative to SP when training with AdamW arises simply from maximizing the learning rate of the embedding layer. In SP, the embedding layer learning rate acts as a bottleneck that induces training instabilities; increasing it by a factor of width to match $μ$P dramatically smooths out training while improving hyperparameter transfer. We also find that weight decay improves the scaling law fits, while, in the fixed token-per-parameter setting, it hurts the robustness of the extrapolation.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21254"></a>
### [Random Matrix Spectra from Boltzmann-Weighted Lattice Ensembles](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21254v1)

**Authors:** Yaprak Önder, Abbas Ali Saberi, Roderich Moessner  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21254v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21254_figures/2605.21254_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Behavior of V(k) plotted for d = 2, 3, 4, 5 for ξ = 0.1 (left), ξ = 1 (middle), and ξ = 100 (right). The propagator is normalized as shown in Eq. (III.25). In the ξ →0 limit, V(k) follows the Ornstein-Zernike form for all dimensions. As ξ →∞, the function has an IR divergence of the form 1/|k|d−4 for d &lt; 4, and −ln(|k|) for d = 4.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21254_figures/2605.21254_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Distinct pairings (up to a symmetry factor) for the 4th and 6th moments of Wigner matrices. We have identified the matrix indices with the vertices and the matrix elements with the edges; pairings are indicated via lines connecting the edges inside the n-gon. Non-crossing pairings (4-ii,6-iii,6-iv in the figure) contribute at O(1) in the large-L limit, while crossing ones are suppressed.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21254_figures/2605.21254_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Fourth and sixth moments of the eigenvalue spectrum for T &gt; Tc as a function of ξ for d = 2, explicitly computed from the numerical integration of relevant terms as shown in Eqs. (IV.6),(IV.7). Insets indicate the limit ξ →0. Note that initial deviations are O(ξ4), which indicates that the moments are stable to small perturbations to the semicircle law.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21254_figures/2605.21254_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Fourth and sixth moments of the eigenvalue spectrum of Ising Model (top) and Gaussian EA spin glass (bottom) as a function of ξ, computed from Monte Carlo sampling. ξ is calculated by fitting the matrix of variances to the relevant propagator form. The black dashed line indicates the analytical solution shown in Fig. 3.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21254_figures/2605.21254_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5: Scaling of the fourth and sixth moments of the eigenvalue spectrum at T = Tc for the Ising model as a function of system size, computed from Monte Carlo sampling. The results show good agreement with the scaling predictions from a moment method analysis, and the results for the fourth moment agree with [12].</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a method to bridge statistical mechanics and random matrix theory by mapping lattice configurations to correlated matrix ensembles. It demonstrates that the eigenvalue spectrum of these matrices can quantitatively capture phase transitions and the evolution of spatial correlations.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a random matrix framework to study statistical-mechanical lattice systems through spectral observables and determining if eigenvalue spectra can serve as probes for collective behavior and criticality.

**Main result.** The authors establish that the eigenvalue spectrum evolves from a Wigner semicircle law at high temperatures to model-dependent critical forms that reflect the underlying spatial correlation structure.

**Method.** The framework maps Boltzmann-weighted configurations to matrix ensembles using Wick-contraction expansions for moments and the Vector Dyson Equation (VDE) for the bulk density.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on d-dimensional hypercubic lattices, specifically the 2D ferromagnetic Ising model and the 3D Edwards-Anderson spin glass.

**Key observables.** Eigenvalue spectral distribution (bulk density), spectral moments, spectral edge, and the variance profile in momentum space.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (beta), correlation length (xi), system size (L), and the anomalous dimension (eta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes translational invariance, periodic boundary conditions, and uses a Gaussian/Wick approximation for off-critical regimes.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the variance profile V(k) across dimensions, the scaling of the fourth and sixth moments with correlation length, and the accuracy of the analytic spectral edge compared to Monte Carlo data.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the matrix construction, derives the momentum-space variance profile, applies analytical techniques (Wick expansion and VDE) to specific models (Ising and EA spin glass), and benchmarks results against Monte Carlo simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce a random matrix framework for studying statistical-mechanical lattice systems through spectral observables. Equilibrium configurations sampled from a Boltzmann measure are mapped to matrix ensembles whose covariance structure is inherited from the spatial correlations of the underlying model. This construction maps real-space correlation functions to a momentum-space variance profile, providing a direct bridge between statistical-mechanical correlations and correlated random matrix ensembles. We derive this variance profile in finite-correlation-length and critical regimes, and compute spectral moments within a Wick-contraction expansion. A complementary self-consistent description of the bulk density is developed using the resolvent formalism. These analytical methods are benchmarked against Monte Carlo data for the two-dimensional Ising model and three-dimensional Edwards--Anderson spin glasses. In both cases, the spectra evolve from the semicircle law at high temperature to model-dependent critical forms reflecting the structure of correlations. The framework, therefore, provides a quantitative spectral route to probing collective behavior in ordered and disordered statistical systems, while also defining a class of physically motivated correlated random matrix ensembles.

</details>

### [Representability-Aware Neural Networks for Reduced Density Matrices: Application to Fractional Chern Insulators](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20326v1)

**Authors:** Justin B. Hart, Awwab A. Azam, Thomas Li, Yunxuan Li, Ye Bi, Haining Pan, Jiabin Yu

<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic of Representability-Aware NN (a) The input of the NN involves the Bloch momenta on cer- tain momentum meshes. This pipeline supports prediction on any L1 × L2 conventional or tilted mesh in any convention as the NN can be evaluated at arbitrarily dense or arbitrarily shaped momentum meshes in the first Brillouin zone (1BZ). (b) Loss function for the interpolation training. The NNs are trained on 2-RDMs on small meshes, where we provide true ˜D, ˜Q, and ˜G through the ED calculation. (c) Loss...</sub>

</details>

<sub>FIG. 2. NN Parameter Efficiency for Interpolation of the 2-RDM of the one-band projected tMoTe2. We plot the NN architectures’ maximum achievable (a) Rvalue and (b) Roverlap at the indicated parameter count. The prediction is tested against ED on the conventional 6 × 6 mesh.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.20326_figures/2605.20326_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Comparison of BPSDP and NN Varia- tional Optimization for 2-RDM on several conven- tional meshes. We provide BPSDP incorporating (2, 2) (blue), BPSDP incorporating (2, 2) and T1 (brown), and BPSDP incorporating (2, 2), T1, and T2 (green). We also pro- vide the prediction of the NN variational optimization with all the listed meshes as target meshes (purple). We plot (a) E −EED with E the converged/predicted energy and EED the ED ground-state energy, (b) Rvalue against the ED results, and (c) Roverlap against the ED results over various conven- tional L1 × L2 meshes. In (d), we plot the required number of real parameters for the each calculation, in log-scale.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.20326_figures/2605.20326_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. The plot of S(q) for the one-band projected tMoTe2. **Summary.** The authors present a neural network framework designed to predict reduced density matrices by embedding physical N-representability constraints directly into the network. This approach allows for efficient interpolation of many-body properties across different momentum meshes and provides a highly parameter-efficient alternative to traditional semidefinite programming for studying fractional Chern insulators.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing an efficient way to predict two-particle reduced density matrices (2-RDMs) and many-body energies for large-scale strongly correlated systems without the exponential cost of Exact Diagonalization.

**Main result.** The proposed representability-aware neural network, specifically a residual MLP, achieves high accuracy in predicting 2-RDMs and ground-state energies with significantly fewer parameters than traditional semidefinite programming methods.

**Method.** A neural network framework that incorporates N-representability conditions (Hermiticity, antisymmetry, and PSD constraints) into its architecture and loss function, utilizing both interpolation-based training and variational optimization.

**Model / system.** A one-band projected many-body model of twisted bilayer MoTe2 (tMoTe2) at a 3.89 degree twist angle and 2/3 hole filling, featuring double-gate screened Coulomb interactions.

**Key observables.** Two-particle reduced density matrices (2-RDMs), many-body ground-state energy, many-body quantum metric, and static structure factor S(q).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Twist angle of 3.89 degrees, hole filling of 2/3, gate distance of 20 nm, and various momentum mesh sizes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The implementation only enforces a subset of N-representability conditions because the full set is NP-complete.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the NN pipeline and loss function schematics; Figure 9 compares architecture performance via R-value and overlap; Figure 14 illustrates the scaling of accuracy against parameter count.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the representability-aware NN framework, evaluates various architectures (MLP, KAN, SIREN, etc.) using the Richardson model, applies the method to the tMoTe2 fractional Chern insulator, compares performance against BPSDP, and concludes with scaling and generalization results.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop a representability-aware and interpolable neural network (NN) framework for predicting two-particle reduced density matrices (2-RDMs). The NN incorporates a subset of representability conditions through its architecture and loss function, and can operate on different momentum meshes, enabling evaluating the representability conditions across multiple meshes, which we call interpolated representability condition. The framework can be used either to predict 2-RDMs on large momentum meshes by interpolating exact results from small meshes, or as a variational 2-RDM ansatz optimized by energy minimization on arbitrary meshes. We apply this approach to the fractional Chern insulator in the one-band projected model of twisted bilayer MoTe$_2$ at twist angle $3.89^\circ$ and hole filling $2/3$. Trained on exact-diagonalization (ED) 2-RDMs from meshes with $12$ or $18$ momentum points using six different NN architectures, the best NN is the residual multilayer perceptron, which predicts the $6\times6$ 2-RDM with $97.07\%-98.18\%$ accuracy relative to the ED 2-RDM but predicts an energy $77.353$ meV above ED ground-state energy. We then variationally optimize the NN on several meshes including $6\times6$, predicting a $6\times 6$ energy of just $0.104$ meV below ED while maintaining $98.94\%-98.96\%$ accuracy. Compared with the conventional boundary-point semidefinite programming, which gives an energy $5.560$ meV below ED with $96.40\%-98.94\%$ accuracy, the NN achieves a more accurate energy and similar accuracy while using only less than 1/20 as many parameters. Eventually, we add a symmetric mesh of $48$ momentum points to the variational optimization of the NN, and provide a prediction of the many-body ground-state energy and the many-body quantum metric on that mesh.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.21386"></a>
### [Strange metallicity in the Kagome metal Ni$_3$In: a DMFT investigation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21386v1)

**Authors:** Ruslan Mushkaev, Francesco Petocchi, Philipp Werner  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21386v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21386_figures/2605.21386_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Panel (a): one of the Ni3In Kagome layers. Panel (b): shifted Ni3In unit cell. Panel (c): local orbital axes.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21386_figures/2605.21386_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Left panel: DFT bandstructure (black) and 6 Wannier bands (orange), derived from a Ni-dxz Wannier Hamiltonian. The band derived from the CMO states is the blue dashed line. Middle and right panels: Full DOS for the 6-band and 1-band (CMO) models, and DOS calculated in the kz = 0 plane.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21386_figures/2605.21386_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Drude fit of the current-current correlation func- tion for (a) β =60 and (b) 240 eV−1 (193 K and 48 K respectively).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21386_figures/2605.21386_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: DMFT local density of states per spin for the one-band model of Ni3In for U = 1 eV and 2 eV, at β = 100 eV−1 (116 K). The black curve is the local DOS of the noninteracting model.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21386_figures/2605.21386_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5: Local DMFT self-energies for Ni3In for different interactions and temperatures in the undoped case (top row) and for different doping values δ = 1.85 −n at U = 1 eV (second and third rows).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper uses DMFT to show that the strange metallicity in the Kagome metal Ni3In arises from a partially filled quasi-flat band. The researchers demonstrate that a single-band Hubbard model can capture the non-Fermi-liquid behavior and the crossover to a heavy Fermi-liquid regime upon hole doping. This provides a microscopic link between the Kagome lattice geometry and anomalous transport properties.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the microscopic origins of strange metallicity and the coexistence of localized and itinerant electronic degrees of freedom in the Kagome metal Ni3In.

**Main result.** The study finds that a single-band Hubbard model can reproduce the linear-in-T resistivity and non-Fermi-liquid behavior, which is driven by the presence of a partially filled quasi-flat band.

**Method.** Dynamical Mean Field Theory (DMFT) using a hybridization-expansion continuous-time Monte Carlo (CT-HYB) solver applied to a Hubbard model derived from DFT-based Wannier functions.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the Kagome metal Ni3In. The theoretical model is a minimal single-band Hubbard model on a stacked triangular lattice, constructed from Compact Molecular Orbitals (CMOs) to capture the electronic structure near the Fermi level.

**Key observables.** Temperature-dependent resistivity, imaginary part of the self-energy, local density of states (DOS), and static local spin susceptibility.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hubbard repulsion U (1-2 eV), band filling (n approx 1.85), and temperature (32-193 K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The resistivity calculation neglects vertex corrections within the DMFT approximation; the model is reduced to a minimal single-band Hubbard model.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the Ni Kagome layer and orbital axes; Figure 2 displays DFT bandstructure and DOS; Figure 10-11 illustrate the stacked triangular lattice model and orbital character of the CMO band; Figure 12 provides a numerical benchmark for resistivity.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of strange metallicity in Ni3In, describes the construction of the Hubbard model from DFT/Wannier functions, presents DMFT results regarding self-energy and resistivity, explores the effects of hole doping, and concludes with a discussion on the role of the quasi-flat band.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Strange metallicity, characterized by a linear temperature dependence of the resistivity, is observed in a broad range of correlated materials, including heavy-fermion compounds and cuprate superconductors. It has also recently been reported for the Kagome metal Ni$_3$In, where almost localized and itinerant electronic degrees of freedom coexist as a result of a partially flat band. We investigate the correlated electronic structure and transport properties of Ni$_3$In with dynamical mean field theory (DMFT) calculations performed on a minimal single-band Hubbard model, constructed from compact molecular orbitals. Despite the large band filling, even for moderate Hubbard repulsion, we observe a non-Fermi-liquid like frequency dependence of the self-energy, as well as the formation of local magnetic moments. With increased hole doping, a crossover to a heavy Fermi-liquid regime is found. We interpret these results in terms of an effective model for the partially filled narrow band near $k_z=0$.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.20328"></a>
### [Targeting Clause Type Distributions: a Picklock for Random Satisfiability Problems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20328v1)

**Authors:** J. Schwardt, J. C. Budich  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20328v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20328_figures/2605.20328_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Illustration of Tsat and Walksat trajectories through clause-type distribution space (CTDS), starting from random initial states. The color corresponds to the log10 of the density of states (DoS), and for the Tsat trajectories we also show the accumulated DoS on the left (cf. Fig. 4). The grayscale projections are along the E and r2 axes and serve as a visual guide. Walksat does not reach the solution at E = 0 because it gets stuck in a vast local minimum of low-energy states (trap). The Tsat algorithm introduced in this article takes a completely different path and encounters a barrier be- fore entering a final search for the solution located near the bottom left.</sub>

</details>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<sub>FIG. 5. Setup as in Fig. 4, but at B = (0.65, 0.1). Away from the critical line the initial barrier appears to increase only linearly (note that the number of bins along the x-axis is ∝N, hence the coalescence of all lines). This explains why Tsat retains an average ⟨p⟩≈1 in Fig. 3(b) up to very large N; beyond N ≈5000 the final search phase becomes the bottleneck (see crossing in the inset), and thus eventually inhibits solubility.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<sub>FIG. 4. For varying size N, we run Tsat on 102 instances at A = (0.7, 0.1) for 102 trials each, with unlimited flips per trial (until a solution is found). **Summary.** The paper presents a new algorithm, Target-SAT, that significantly improves the ability to solve the hardest instances of random 3-SAT problems. By navigating through a specific parameter space of clause distributions, the algorithm overcomes a major complexity barrier that previously limited stochastic local search methods. This provides new insights into the landscape of computational hardness in frustrated systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Overcoming the computational hardness of random 3-SAT instances in the most difficult regime, characterized by a critical line of hard instances and a vast low-energy trap.

**Main result.** The introduction of the Target-SAT (TSAT) algorithm, which roughly triples the tractable problem size in the hardest regime and identifies a specific complexity barrier in the clause-type distribution space.

**Method.** A new Stochastic Local Search (SLS) heuristic called TSAT that uses statistical information from the Clause-Type Distribution Space (CTDS) to actively guide the search toward a target distribution.

**Model / system.** Random 3-SAT problems mapped to Ising spin Hamiltonians, representing strongly correlated many-body systems with rugged energy landscapes and frustrated magnetic interactions.

**Key observables.** Clause-Type Distribution (CTD), average success probability, energy (number of unsatisfied clauses), and scaling of runtime.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Clause density (alpha), clause-type distribution ratios (r1, r2), system size (N), and the critical parameter line.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study focuses on random 3-SAT instances; the effectiveness of the algorithm on highly structured or practical non-random problems is not yet established.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows search trajectories in the CTDS; Figure 2 compares success probabilities of Walksat and Tsat; Figure 3 shows scaling of success probability with N; Figure 4 shows the emergence of complexity barriers in state histograms; Figure 7 compares solved problem ratios across different regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of 3-SAT hardness, presents the TSAT algorithm, compares its performance against Walksat and complete solvers, analyzes the complexity barriers in the CTDS, and discusses scaling and limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Optimization problems such as the NP-complete 3-SAT provide an important benchmark for the difficult task of finding ground-states in strongly correlated many-body systems with rugged energy landscapes. The study of random 3-SAT problems as Ising spin Hamiltonians in statistical physics has yielded major insights including the existence of a satisfiability phase transition, and the prediction of a critical parameter line of particularly hard instances. Yet, progress on solving those instances has been scarce for several decades. Here, introducing the Target-SAT (TSAT) algorithm, we roughly triple the tractable problem sizes in the hardest regime, with an even greater improvement in a vast range of neighboring regions. By leveraging statistical information hidden in the combinatorial constraints of the problem, TSAT is actively guided in its stochastic local search toward a target within the relevant parameter space. Our analysis also explains why established local search algorithms are limited to relatively small system sizes due to a vast low-energy trap. Furthermore, we characterize the aforementioned critical line in terms of a dominant additional complexity barrier, whose exponential scaling is quickly overcome by TSAT only in the surrounding parameter space. With TSAT, the lead in solving the hardest known random satisfiability problems returns to the realm of stochastic local search algorithms.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Textured phase diagrams of featureless insulators](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20339v1)

**Authors:** Sashank Singam, Nick G. Jones, Abhishodh Prakash  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20339v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20339_figures/2605.20339_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.20339_figures/2605.20339_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Single-particle energy dispersion of the Hamiltonian (1) (a) away from and (b) at the origin of the J-δ plane.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>FIG. 5. Phase diagram of Eq. (1) the different limits encap- sulated; as one takes a trajectory around the origin, we see a transport of charge by one unit cell which is central to the behaviour of the Thouless pump.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.20339_figures/2605.20339_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. The textured phase diagrams of the Rice-Mele model (1). The insulating phase with a single filled band has a topo- logical texture similar to that of a vortex, exposed by plot- ting the Berry phase γ (numerically evaluated). The insulator with both bands filled or empty has no texture. The broken line indicates the presence of edge modes. For µ ̸= 0, the left and right edge modes are estranged and occur on different parts of the phase diagram as can be seen in (b), (c).</sub>

</details>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that even 'featureless' insulators, which lack topological order, can possess highly structured phase diagrams filled with topological textures like vortices. By using a recursive construction method, the authors show how these textures and their associated edge modes generalize to higher dimensions and remain stable against interactions.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the existence of topological textures (vortices and higher-dimensional geometric invariants) within the phase diagrams of featureless, topologically trivial insulators.

**Main result.** The authors show that trivial insulating phases can host non-trivial topological textures in parameter space, where gap-closing diabolical points act as singularities and lead to robust, sometimes 'estranged' edge modes.

**Method.** The study uses a 'suspension construction' to generalize 1D models to higher dimensions, employing Berry-Bloch connections, Chern-Simons forms, and effective field theory (Dirac fermion Lagrangians) to compute topological invariants.

**Model / system.** Non-interacting, charge-conserving 'class A' fermions, specifically utilizing the 1D Rice-Mele model and its higher-dimensional 'ascendants' constructed via iterative stacking.

**Key observables.** Berry phases, higher-dimensional Chern-Simons forms, Chern numbers (C1, C2), and edge mode localization/robustness.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Staggered hopping (delta), staggered potential (J), chemical potential (mu), and interaction strength (U).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary focus is restricted to non-interacting fermion systems and the Altland-Zirnbauer symmetry class A.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the Rice-Mele model schematic; Figure 2 displays single-particle energy dispersion; Figure 22/23 show phase diagrams for the QWZ model and its ascendants.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of textures in trivial phases, develops the 1D Rice-Mele model analysis, introduces the suspension construction for higher dimensions, applies effective field theory to study stability and edge modes, and concludes with a generalization to arbitrary dimensions and interacting spin systems.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study phase diagrams of charge-conserving `class A' non-interacting fermions, focusing on the trivial phase in various dimensions. Such phases are usually termed `featureless' to distinguish them from those others with either symmetry-broken or topological order. We show that the presence of non-trivial topological families of states, including charge pumps and their generalizations, results in phase diagrams being endowed with non-trivial topological textures that can be visualized through Berry phases and their higher-dimensional generalizations. We show that for non-interacting fermion systems with translation invariance, these `higher' Berry phases can be computed using integrals of non-abelian Chern-Simons forms of the Berry-Bloch connection over momentum and parameter spaces. Singularities in these textures correspond to gap-closing loci of `diabolical points', which represent the obstruction to contracting topologically non-trivial families of states, and bulk-boundary correspondence results in a locus of robust boundary modes that terminate at the bulk diabolical points. In the presence of finite chemical potential, we argue that the edge modes are generically robust without any need for fine-tuning for two and higher dimensions, whereas in one dimension they are `estranged' in the phase diagram, i.e. appearing at different parameter values for different edges. We demonstrate our results by constructing several microscopic models of non-interacting fermions. We argue stability to interactions and explore proximate phase diagrams by mapping to continuum field theories.

</details>

### [Thermodynamic and structural behavior of one-dimensional divalent patchy hard rods: Wertheim's first-order thermodynamic perturbation theory versus exact results](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21003v1)

**Authors:** Ana M. Montero, Andrés Santos, Péter Gurin, Szabolcs Varga

<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic representation of divalent patchy hard rods of length σ aligned along the x-axis. Particles cannot overlap (i.e., x &gt; σ), but neighboring rods can form bonds when σ &lt; x &lt; σ + δ through the interaction between the right (R) site of the left particle and the left (L) site of the right particle. A monomer and a dimer are shown for illustration.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21003_figures/2605.21003_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Structural diagram in the τ–ρ∗plane for divalent patchy hard rods with δ∗= 0.5. The FW line (solid curve) separates the regions of monotonic and oscillatory asymptotic decay of the pair correlation function. Below the FW line, the Widom line (dash-dotted curve) is deﬁned as the locus of ab- solute maxima of the correlation length ξ in the monotonic regime. In the oscillatory regime, ξ may exhibit a weak lo- cal maximum followed by a local minimum; the locus of these extrema deﬁnes the ECO line (dashed curve). **Summary.** This paper provides an exact analytical treatment of 1D patchy hard rods to demonstrate where standard association theories fail. It reveals that finite-range interactions introduce complex structural transitions, such as the ECO line, which are absent in the simpler sticky-limit model.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the accuracy of Wertheim's first-order thermodynamic perturbation theory (TPT1) for 1D divalent patchy hard rods and explores the structural complexity arising from finite-range interactions.

**Main result.** The authors show that while TPT1 is exact in the zero-range sticky limit, it fails for finite-range interactions; they identify new structural features like the ECO line and demonstrate that the high-pressure scaling of the correlation length changes depending on the interaction range.

**Method.** The authors use analytical derivations via the isothermal-isobaric ensemble and Laplace-pole analysis of the structure factor to compare exact solutions against TPT1 approximations.

**Model / system.** A one-dimensional system of divalent patchy hard rods with a hard-core diameter sigma and attractive square-well patches of width delta and depth epsilon located at the rod tips.

**Key observables.** Pressure, fraction of unbonded sites, pair correlation function, correlation length, and structural lines (Fisher-Widom, Widom, and ECO lines).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rod length (sigma), interaction range (delta), well depth (epsilon), inverse stickiness (tau), and number density (rho).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is confined to a strictly 1D channel (single-file regime) preventing rotation or particle overtaking; the study focuses on the nearest-neighbor bonding limit.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a schematic of the 1D rods; Figure 2 compares exact vs TPT1 pressure; Figure 3 shows the density dependence of the pressure ratio; Figure 5 illustrates structural lines and correlation length behavior.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the 1D patchy model, derives exact thermodynamic and structural properties using Laplace transforms, compares these results to Wertheim's TPT1, and analyzes various structural regimes and asymptotic limits.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the thermodynamic and structural properties of divalent patchy hard rods confined to a one-dimensional channel by modeling the bonding sites as attractive square-well (SW) patches located at the rod tips. The zero-range sticky limit is recovered by letting the well width vanish while keeping the stickiness parameter finite. While Wertheim's first-order thermodynamic perturbation theory (TPT1) becomes exact in this sticky limit, it fails for finite-range site-site interactions. We show that the theory can be made exact in one dimension by replacing the standard law of mass action with an exact relation between the density and the fraction of unbonded sites, together with an exact bonding free-energy contribution. Finite-range SW sites produce a richer structural behavior than sticky sites, including monotonic and oscillatory asymptotic decay of the pair correlation function, separated by the Fisher--Widom line. In the monotonic regime, the correlation length exhibits an absolute maximum defining the Widom line, while in the oscillatory regime it may display a local maximum and minimum, whose locus defines the ``Extrema of the Correlation length under Oscillatory decay'' (ECO) line. These features disappear in the sticky limit, where the system remains entirely in the oscillatory regime. We also show that the high-pressure behavior of the correlation length changes from $ξ\sim p^2$ for finite-range SW sites to $ξ\sim p^3$ in the sticky limit.

</details>

### [Thermodynamics of homogeneous Universes: de Sitter, Bonnor-Melvin and static Einstein](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21047v1)

**Authors:** G. E. Volovik  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21047v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21047_figures/2605.21047_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21047_figures/2605.21047_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21047_figures/2605.21047_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21047_figures/2605.21047_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21047_figures/2605.21047_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a unified thermodynamic description for different types of homogeneous universes by treating the gravitational field as a matter field. It shows that the cosmological constant problem can be resolved via thermodynamic compensation in the Minkowski vacuum. Additionally, it identifies the de Sitter graviton as the second sound of a two-fluid system.

**Why it may be interesting.** The use of Landau's two-fluid hydrodynamics and the identification of gravitons as 'second sound' provides a deep connection between relativistic cosmology and the many-body physics of superfluids.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the thermodynamics of homogeneous universes (de Sitter, Bonnor-Melvin, and Einstein static) and addressing the cosmological constant problem through an emergent gravity framework.

**Main result.** The author demonstrates that these three distinct universes share identical thermodynamic properties and that the cosmological constant naturally vanishes in the Minkowski vacuum due to thermodynamic compensation.

**Method.** The paper employs a two-fluid hydrodynamic analogy (similar to Landau's superfluid model) and a generalized energy-momentum tensor construction within a pre-geometric/emergent gravity framework.

**Model / system.** The study considers three homogeneous cosmological models: the de Sitter Universe (vacuum energy + thermal gravity), the Bonnor-Melvin-Lambda Universe (magnetic field + vacuum energy), and the static Einstein Universe (ordinary matter + vacuum energy).

**Key observables.** Generalized energy density and pressure, temperature (Hubble-related), entropy density, and the speed of second sound.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Ricci scalar curvature (R), gravitational coupling (K), 4-form curvature (q), and the equation of state parameter (w).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The universes are treated as equilibrium thermodynamic states, and boundary effects in non-compact de Sitter space are ignored.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by establishing the emergent gravity framework, proceeds to construct the thermodynamic formalism for the three specific universes, applies a two-fluid hydrodynamic analogy to identify gravitons as second sound, and concludes with the derivation of the zero cosmological constant in the Minkowski limit.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In the theories, in which dynamic gravitational field emerges from the underlying matter fields, the gravitational field can be considered as a part of matter. Using this approach, we construct the thermodynamics of the homogeneous Universes -- the de Sitter Universe, the Bonnor-Melvin-$Λ$ Universe and the static Einstein Universe. It is demonstrated that although these three Universes have different types of matter fields (ordinary matter, magnetic field, gravitational field and vacuum energy), they have the same thermodynamic properties. Their energy densities obey the same equation, which contains the corresponding matter densities and the pairs of the thermodynamically conjugate variables. In Minkowski vacuum, where the ordinary matter and magnetic and gravitational fields are absent, this thermodynamic approach automatically leads to zero cosmological constant.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21336"></a>
### [Unconventional Magnetism: Symmetry Classification, Hybrid-parity and Unconstrained-parity Classes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21336v1)

**Authors:** Xun-Jiang Luo, Dan Li, Rui-Chun Xiao, Ding-Fu Shao, Lei Li, Mingliang Tian, Yugui Yao  
**Type:** both · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21336v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21336_figures/2605.21336_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. (a) Schematic illustration of the even-parity, odd-parity, hybrid-parity, and unconstrained-parity classes of UM. ˜P denotes the SSG symmetry that flips momentum. S(k) = (sx(k), sy(k), sz(k)) is the spin expectation value. E(k) is the energy at momentum k. (b) Illustration of a hybrid-parity spin texture and its decomposition into d-wave and p-wave channels along different spin directions, thereby leading to the spin current (js) and Edelstein effect under an applied electric field E.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21336_figures/2605.21336_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Flowchart illustrating the symmetry classification of UM. Starting from the SSG G, the derived point group Ps and G1 dictate the conditions for SCM and NSS, respectively. The spin textures form a representation of the point group ˜G. If ˜G is a Laue group, the spin texture acquires a definite parity, leading to even-, odd-, or hybrid-parity classes; if not, the unconstrained-parity class arises. Here λi = det(XiUi).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21336_figures/2605.21336_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. (a) Crystal structure and magnetic order of bulk FePO4. (b) Fermi surface of FePO4 at EF = −35 meV. The spin textures on the kx = ±0.5 Å−1 planes are plotted; arrows indicate the orientation of vector S(k), and the color encodes the out-of-plane spin component sx(k). (c) Calculated T-odd spin Hall conductivity σz(o) xy and σz(o) yx as a function of the Fermi energy EF . (d) T-even Edelstein susceptibility χ(e) xx as a function of EF .</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21336_figures/2605.21336_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Proposed spintronic device architectures enabled by HPMs. (a) Hybrid spin-splitter torque: a charge current in- jected into an HPM/FM bilayer generates spin currents with multi spin polarizations components. (b) Magnetic tunnel junction: the top layer utilizes the Edelstein effect for electri- cal write operations, while the bottom HPM layer provides a spin-polarized readout current. (c) Spin transistor: an HPM channel sandwiched between ferromagnetic (FM) source (S) and drain (D) contacts; gate voltage Vtg modulates spin pre- cession in the HPM, enabling non-volatile logic functionality.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper establishes a new symmetry-based classification for unconventional magnetism, predicting the existence of hybrid-parity and unconstrained-parity magnets. It demonstrates that hybrid-parity magnets allow for the simultaneous occurrence of spin current and Edelstein effects, offering a new platform for multifunctional spintronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper seeks to complete the symmetry taxonomy of unconventional magnetism beyond the established even-parity (altermagnets) and odd-parity paradigms.

**Main result.** The authors identify two new magnetic classes, hybrid-parity magnets (HPMs) and unconstrained-parity magnets (UPMs), and demonstrate that HPMs enable the coexistence of spin current and Edelstein effects.

**Method.** The study uses Spin Space Group (SSG) theory for symmetry classification, combined with first-principles DFT calculations and tight-binding modeling to validate transport properties.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on symmetry-compensated magnets, using FePO4 as a prototypical example of a Type-I hybrid-parity magnet, and explores candidate materials identified via the MAGNDATA database.

**Key observables.** Non-relativistic spin splitting (NSS), spin current conductivity, Edelstein susceptibility, and spin texture parity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spin texture dimensionality (collinear, coplanar, noncoplanar), spin-splitting group (SSG) symmetries, and effective U parameter for Fe-3d orbitals.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The classification assumes the validity of the Spin Space Group framework and uses the constant relaxation-time approximation for transport calculations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the four classes of unconventional magnetism and the decomposition of spin textures; Figure 2 provides a symmetry classification flowchart; Figure 3 shows the crystal structure and Fermi surface of FePO4; Figure 4 depicts proposed spintronic device architectures.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with a symmetry-based classification of unconventional magnetism, introduces the new HPM and UPM classes, derives symmetry criteria for HPM types, validates these findings with DFT calculations on FePO4, and concludes with proposed spintronic applications.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Unconventional magnetism has emerged as a transformative frontier in condensed matter physics. Such phases are characterized by substantial non-relativistic spin splitting (NSS) in symmetry-compensated magnets. They have been classified by the parity of their spin textures under momentum inversion, leading to the paradigms of altermagnets (even-parity) and odd-parity magnets. However, the full symmetry landscape remains largely unexplored. In this Letter, we present a systematic classification framework for unconventional magnetism based on the representation theory of the spin textures and the associated parity properties. Within this framework, we predict two previously unidentified classes beyond the established pure-parity categories: hybrid-parity magnets (HPMs) and unconstrained-parity magnets (UPMs), where the spin textures exhibit contrasting parities among their Cartesian components and the parity of the spin textures is ill-defined, respectively. We derive universal symmetry criteria that categorize HPMs into three distinct types. Importantly, by combining the spin splitting characteristics of altermagnets and odd-parity magnets, HPMs can enable the coexistence of the spin current and Edelstein effects. Taking FePO4 as an example, we perform first-principles calculations to demonstrate this coexistence. Finally, we discuss the potential applications of HPMs in spintronic devices. Our work provides a comprehensive symmetry classification of unconventional magnetism and establishes HPMs as a promising platform for multi-functional spintronics.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.20847"></a>
### [Unifying Plasticity in Ordered and Disordered Matter using Topological and Geometrical Descriptors](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20847v1)

**Authors:** Xin Wang, Yang Xu, Jin Shang, Yi Xing, Jie Zhang, Yujie Wang, Walter Kob, Matteo Baggioli  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20847v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20847_figures/2605.20847_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a unified framework for understanding plasticity by applying continuous topological and geometric descriptors to both crystalline and amorphous materials. By treating defects as continuous fields rather than discrete singularities, the authors successfully bridge the gap between ordered and disordered systems. The findings show that these fields accurately predict plastic events and highlight the increasing importance of rotational defects in 3D systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of identifying the specific regions responsible for plastic flow in amorphous solids due to the lack of long-range order and the absence of discrete topological defects like dislocations found in crystals.

**Main result.** The introduction of continuous dislocation, disclination, and incompatibility density fields provides a unified description of plasticity that correlates strongly with standard measures like D2min and reveals that rotational defects become dominant in 3D.

**Method.** The authors apply differential geometry to map particle displacement fields onto a grid, calculating continuous defect densities using the Nye tensor, Frank tensor, and Saint-Venant incompatibility tensor.

**Model / system.** The study utilizes 2D simulated Lennard-Jones glasses, 2D experimental photoelastic granular disks, and 3D experimental 3D-printed plastic sphere packings under shear.

**Key observables.** Dislocation density, disclination density, incompatibility density, D2min (non-affine squared displacement), and Structural Similarity Index Measure (SSIM).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Shear strain (pre-yield vs. post-yield regimes), particle diameter (d), and noise amplitude (R).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The approach treats discrete defects as continuous fields and assumes that the displacement field can be meaningfully interpolated onto a regular lattice.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows displacement fields overlaid with dislocation density; Figure 2 compares defect densities to D2min in 2D; Figure 3 quantifies SSIM correlation as a function of strain in 2D and 3D; Figure 4 shows stress-strain response across different platforms.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the problem of plasticity in disordered matter, introduces the mathematical framework for continuous defect densities, presents results from 2D simulations and experiments, extends the findings to 3D systems, and concludes with a robustness analysis against noise and discretization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Identifying the regions responsible for plastic flow in amorphous solids remains an open problem, since structural disorder seems to prevent the direct application of concepts such as dislocations, topological defects that successfully describe irreversible deformations in crystalline systems. Here, we introduce fields of dislocation, disclination, and incompatibility densities, that reduce to the standard sources of plasticity in crystals and assess their predictive power in amorphous materials. We find that, in a simulated two-dimensional glass as well in two- and three-dimensional experimental granular systems, these fields exhibit strong spatial correlations with $D^2_{\text{min}}$, the standard measure used to locate plastic events under shear in disordered solids. Unlike $D^2_{\text{min}}$, these fields also allow to disentangle rotational and translational contributions to the plastic events, revealing that rotational defects becoming dominant in three dimensions. Our approach paves the way for a unified description of plasticity in crystalline and amorphous solids.

</details>

</details>

### [Spin-polarized lasing in a photonic lattice](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20927v1)

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum optics experiment` **4/5** · `interference shaping light` **3/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates how a 2D photonic lattice can be used to achieve spin-polarized lasing with extended spatial coherence. By controlling the pump polarization, the researchers can tune the handedness of the emitted light, providing a platform for spin-controlled coherent emission in large-scale optical systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems as it demonstrates the manipulation of spin-polarized coherent states and the transition between different light-matter coupling regimes in an engineered lattice.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to develop a scalable, coherent light source with controllable polarization by engineering the photonic band structure of coupled microlaser arrays, addressing the loss of spatial coherence and multi-mode instabilities in large-area VCSELs.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrated a transition from the strong-coupling (polariton) regime to the photon lasing regime in a 2D photonic lattice, achieving spin-controlled coherent emission where the light's circular polarization follows the pump's handedness.

**Method.** The work combines electron-beam lithography and wet etching to fabricate a GaAs/InGaAs microcavity lattice, followed by micro-photoluminescence, polarization-resolved spectroscopy, and Michelson interferometry, complemented by a two-mode spin-laser rate equation model.

**Model / system.** A 2D staggered square photonic lattice of rounded rectangular GaAs/InGaAs semiconductor microcavity mesas, which supports both exciton-polariton modes and photon lasing regimes.

**Key observables.** Energy- and momentum-resolved emission, full Stokes parameters (S0, S3), spatial coherence (g1), and Rabi splitting.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Lasing threshold (Pth ~ 0.3 mW/um2), Rabi splitting (3.35 meV), and TE-TM splitting (0.05 meV/um2).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theoretical model assumes a minimal two-mode system and neglects gain saturation effects, which could potentially hide bistability or chaos.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: Schematic of the lattice design; Fig 2: Nonlinear input-output response and transition to the weak-coupling regime; Fig 3: Spatial coherence maps; Fig 4: Polarization maps (S0S3) and the dependence of S3 on pump power and QWP angle.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the challenge of large-area VCSEL stability, describes the fabrication and design of the 2D photonic lattice, presents experimental evidence of the polariton-to-lasing transition, analyzes the spin-control of polarization, and concludes with a theoretical model validating the observed spin-injection dynamics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We characterize spin-polarized lasing in a two-dimensional photonic lattice fabricated from a GaAs/InGaAs semiconductor microcavity sample. The lattice is defined by a staggered arrangement of rounded rectangular micrometric mesas that laterally confine and couple the optical modes. Polarization-, angle-, and energy-resolved micro-photoluminescence measurements reveal the transition from the strong-coupling regime to photon lasing, accompanied by extended spatial coherence across several lattice unit cells. Under circularly polarized nonresonant excitation, the emitted light acquires a controllable circular polarization whose handedness follows that of the pump. These results establish photonic-lattice VCSELs as a platform for spin-controlled coherent emission in extended optical systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Coexisting Ballistic and Diffusive Heat Transport in Micrometer-Scale Molecular Junctions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20517v1)

**Authors:** P. M. Martinez, O. Mateos-Lopez, J. C. Cuevas, J. G. Vilhena  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20517v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<sub>FIG. 2. Enhancement of ballistic transport through mechanical pulling. a) Thermal conductance versus chain length under various applied tensions (averaged across 3 inde- pendent NEMD runs). Dashed lines indicate theoretical scal- ings for ballistic, superdiffusive, and diffusive regimes. <sub>FIG. 3. Chain thermalization and persistent non- interacting modes in Au-alkane-Au junctions. a) Tem- perature profiles versus normalized coordinate (z/L) for vary- ing chain lengths. ∆T is the boundary temperature jump. b) Normalized spectral temperature ¯T(ω) for a 30 nm chain at 0.1L (blue), 0.5L (yellow), and 0.9L (red). The spatial splitting of ¯T(ω)...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that heat transport in micrometer-scale molecular junctions does not follow Fourier's law, instead exhibiting superdiffusive scaling. The divergence in thermal conductivity is driven by low-frequency acoustic modes that remain ballistic even at large scales. This finding highlights a fundamental coexistence of ballistic and diffusive transport in 1D momentum-conserving systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper explores non-equilibrium transport and the breakdown of standard kinetic theory in 1D systems, which is highly relevant to understanding anomalous diffusion and universality classes in many-body dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the breakdown of Fourier's law and the validity of the Boltzmann transport equation in quasi-1D molecular systems, specifically looking for the emergence of superdiffusive heat transport.

**Main result.** The study finds that thermal conductivity diverges as L^{1/3}, following the Kardar-Parisi-Zhang universality class, due to the coexistence of a persistent ballistic channel (low-frequency modes) and a diffusive sector.

**Method.** Atomistic non-equilibrium molecular dynamics (NEMD) simulations combined with frequency-resolved spectral heat current decomposition and a 3-resistor model to isolate intrinsic conductance.

**Model / system.** Au-alkane-Au single-molecule junctions spanning lengths from 0.5 nm to 4 micrometers, modeled using the OPLS-AA force field for the molecular chain and EAM for the gold electrodes.

**Key observables.** Thermal conductance (G_th), thermal conductivity (kappa_th), spectral heat current q(omega), vibrational density of states (VDoS), and spectral temperature T(R; omega).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Chain length (0.5 nm to 4 um), temperature (310 K), mechanical tension (0.35 nN to 1.05 nN), and frequency-dependent transport regimes (ballistic, superdiffusive, diffusive).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Neglected Coulomb terms due to highly stretched conformations; reliance on classical molecular dynamics which may not capture all quantum effects at very low temperatures.

**Figures summary.** Schematic of the simulation setup; plots of thermal conductance and conductivity vs. length showing scaling regimes; spectral heat current and VDoS; and temperature profiles/spectral temperature analysis.

**Paper structure.** Introduction to the breakdown of Fourier's law; description of the NEMD simulation setup and molecular models; presentation of conductance scaling results; frequency-resolved analysis to identify the ballistic/diffusive coexistence; and discussion of universality and chemical insensitivity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Boltzmann transport theory, the standard framework for predicting thermal conductivity, assumes that every vibrational mode eventually scatters, acquiring a finite lifetime that yields a convergent, length-independent thermal conductivity: Fourier's law. Here we show that this assumption fails in a real molecular system. Through atomistic simulations of Au-alkane-Au single-molecule junctions spanning five orders of magnitude in length (0.5~nm to 4~$μ$m), we find that thermal conductivity never converges. Transport is ballistic for up to one hundred nanometers at room temperature, extending nearly two orders of magnitude beyond existing single-molecule measurements. Past this window, conductivity diverges as $L^{1/3}$, the scaling predicted by the Kardar-Parisi-Zhang universality class for momentum-conserving systems. Frequency-resolved decomposition of the heat current reveals the mechanism behind the divergence. Low-frequency acoustic modes never thermalize: protected by momentum conservation, they remain ballistic at every chain length, still carrying 50\% of the total heat current at $L = 2~μ$m. All other modes thermalize collectively as discrete vibrational states merge into scattering-active phonon bands with increasing length. Hence, the diverging conductivity emerges from the boundary between these coexisting transport regimes: as $L$ grows, the onset of scattering shifts progressively toward lower frequencies, suppressing the ballistic channel at a rate that sustains the $L^{1/3}$ divergence, leaving a finite contribution at every length. This coexistence of permanent ballistic and well-behaved diffusive transport, anticipated in abstract one-dimensional lattice models, survives the structural and chemical complexity of real micrometer-sized junctions.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Occupation Dynamics of Floquet-Volkov States and Spectral Sum Rule](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21339v1)

**Authors:** Xuanxi Cai, Changhua Bao, Benshu Fan, Haoyuan Zhong, Fei Wang, Shaohua Zhou, Tianyun Lin, Hongyun Zhang, Pu Yu, Peizhe Tang, Wenhui Duan, Shuyun Zhou  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21339v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21339_figures/2605.21339_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Conservation of spectral sum rule upon below-gap pumping, and violation of spec- tral sum rule upon above-gap pumping in black phosphorus. </details>

**Summary.** This paper uses TrARPES to show that in semiconductors like black phosphorus, the total electron spectral weight is conserved when driving with below-gap mid-infrared light, despite the creation of multiple Floquet sidebands. This spectral sum rule is broken when the driving energy exceeds the bandgap, leading to direct interband transitions. These findings are crucial for controlling transient material properties via light-field engineering.

**Why it may be interesting.** The discovery of a spectral sum rule and the observation of population inversion in light-dressed states provide fundamental insights into the non-equilibrium dynamics of driven quantum systems and the conservation laws governing energy-periodic state redistribution.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the transient occupation dynamics of Floquet-Volkov states and determining the validity of a spectral sum rule in semiconductors under intense mid-infrared driving.

**Main result.** The study discovers that under below-gap driving, the total spectral weight of Floquet sidebands is conserved (a spectral sum rule), whereas this rule is violated during above-gap pumping due to interband transitions.

**Method.** Time- and angle-resolved photoemission spectroscopy (TrARPES) is used to measure spectral weight redistribution, complemented by Floquet theory and Bessel function approximations to model sideband intensities.

**Model / system.** The experimental system consists of two semiconductors, black phosphorus and 2H-MoSe2, subjected to mid-infrared (160 meV) pulse driving.

**Key observables.** Floquet-Volkov sidebands, spectral weight S(k), isosbestic points in Energy Distribution Curves (EDCs), and pump fluence.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Below-gap vs. above-gap pumping regimes, pump photon energy (160 meV), and pump fluence (up to 1.1 mJ/cm2).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of a Bessel function approximation neglects potential coupling with external baths, and the study does not cover the sub-cycle regime.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 shows spectral weight conservation in black phosphorus; Figure 3 shows conservation in MoSe2 at the K valley; Figure 4 compares the conservation in below-gap driving to the violation in above-gap driving.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of Floquet-Volkov dynamics, presents TrARPES experimental results for black phosphorus and MoSe2, demonstrates the spectral sum rule and its violation, and provides a theoretical framework using Floquet theory and Bessel functions to explain the observed weight redistribution.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Time-periodic light fields can dress electronic states in quantum materials, forming Floquet states whose dynamic occupation determines transient material properties. Here by using time- and angle-resolved photoemission spectroscopy (TrARPES), we reveal the transient occupation of Floquet-Volkov states in two semiconductors, black phosphorus and MoSe$_2$. While the occupation of the light-induced sidebands, directly reflected by TrARPES spectral weight, strongly depends on the driving field, we find that the total spectral weight obtained by summing up all sidebands is conserved upon below-gap driving. Our work provides critical insights into the Floquet population dynamics, which are essential for light-field tailoring of transient material properties.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21389"></a>
### [Huge ultrafast spin Seebeck effect mediated by laser-excited superdiffusive magnon currents](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21389v1)

**Authors:** Luca Mikadze, Peter M. Oppeneer, Markus Weißenhofer  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21389v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21389_figures/2605.21389_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Illustration of the method. (a) In the local (N+2)TM, the laser pulse P impinges on a metal film and heats the thermal electronic system Te homogeneously, which, in turn, is coupled to a thermal phonon bath Tp and nonther- mal magnon occupations nq. (b) The nonlocal (N+2)TM allows for a heterogeneous laser absorption profile resulting in temperature gradients. Transport occurs within each sub- system across the isolated film domain z ∈[0, L], and the subsystems exchange heat locally.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21389_figures/2605.21389_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21389_figures/2605.21389_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Laser-excited magnonic spin currents in a 1 µm film. (a) Magnonic spin current density following irradiation with a 1 mJ/cm2 laser pulse.
