**Summary.** The paper develops a new theoretical framework to describe how ultrafast laser pulses drive non-equilibrium magnon currents in iron films. It identifies a superdiffusive transport regime that produces a spin Seebeck effect much larger than previously predicted. This work provides a way to model complex, non-thermal transport processes in magnetic materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study provides a sophisticated treatment of non-equilibrium transport and the transition from ballistic to diffusive dynamics, which is highly relevant to the study of open quantum systems and non-equilibrium many-body dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the microscopic origin of ultrafast, non-equilibrium magnon transport and demagnetization in ferromagnetic metals beyond traditional diffusive models.

**Main result.** The discovery of a huge ultrafast spin Seebeck effect driven by a superdiffusive regime where magnon transport transitions from ballistic to diffusive.

**Method.** An ab initio-parameterized microscopic framework using the quantum Boltzmann equation to capture non-thermal magnon scattering and transport.

**Model / system.** Bcc Iron (Fe) thin films subjected to sub-picosecond laser excitation, modeled using an extended (N+2)-temperature model that incorporates non-thermal magnon populations.

**Key observables.** Magneto-optical Kerr angles (rotation and ellipticity), magnetization-versus-time curves, magnon spin current density, and transport exponent (alpha).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Sub-picosecond/femtosecond timescales, superdiffusive regime (1 < alpha < 2), and laser fluence (1 to 4 mJ/cm2).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Electrons and phonons are assumed to be internally thermalized; direct magnon-phonon coupling is neglected; higher-order magnon-magnon scattering is neglected.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the local and nonlocal (N+2)TM frameworks; Figure 2 shows temporal evolution of temperature profiles and heat currents; Figure 3 shows the magnonic spin current color map; Figure 4 shows the transport exponent evolution; Figure 6 compares simulation to experimental data and shows MOKE signals.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the limitations of the 3TM, presents the new (N+2)TM quantum Boltzmann framework, details the numerical implementation, analyzes temperature and magnetization dynamics, characterizes the superdiffusive transport regime, and validates the model against experimental MOKE data.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Subpicosecond laser excitation of ferromagnetic metals induces strongly nonequilibrium dynamics involving scattering and transport of electrons, phonons, and magnons. Widely used theoretical approaches, such as the three-temperature model and diffusion equations, are ill-suited to capture these processes on ultrafast timescales. Here, we present an ab initio-parameterized microscopic framework that incorporates nonthermal magnon scattering and transport via the quantum Boltzmann equation. We apply this approach to simulate ultrafast laser-induced demagnetization in bcc Fe films. The model predicts an ultrafast spin Seebeck effect, characterized by a strong burst of fast-moving magnonic spin current reaching technologically relevant amplitudes. Furthermore, we identify a superdiffusive transport regime: a crossover from initially ballistic magnon transport to a diffusive regime at later times. To connect our theoretical predictions to experimentally accessible observables, we calculate the magneto-optical Kerr angles resulting from the predicted depth-resolved magnetization profiles. Our framework provides a route to describe ultrafast nonthermal magnon transport beyond diffusive models and will aid in the design and interpretation of time-resolved spin-transport experiments.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper derives the exact Ginzburg-Landau parameters for the critical dynamics of holographic superconductors. By matching bulk holographic calculations to the Model F universality class, the author determines precise numerical coefficients for the system's time-dependent behavior.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides an exact mapping of a holographic model to a known universality class (Model F), offering deep insights into the critical dynamics and transport coefficients of strongly coupled systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to identify the exact dual Ginzburg-Landau theory and the specific 'model F' universality class parameters for the critical dynamics of holographic superconductors and superfluids.

**Main result.** The author successfully identifies the dual Model F equations and obtains exact numerical coefficients for parameters such as relaxation time, diffusion constants, and thermodynamic susceptibility.

**Method.** The study uses the AdS/CFT correspondence to solve bulk field equations in a 5D Schwarzschild-AdS black hole background using perturbation theory and the Martin-Siggia-Rose formalism.

**Model / system.** A 5-dimensional Einstein-Maxwell-complex scalar system in the probe limit, which is holographically dual to a 4-dimensional system exhibiting superconductivity/superfluidity.

**Key observables.** Hydrodynamic poles (Maxwell diffusion and second sound modes), electric permittivity, magnetic permeability, conductivity, and the dynamic critical exponent z.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Chemical potential (mu), temperature (T), the deviation from criticality (epsilon_mu), and the large-Nc limit.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the probe limit (no backreaction on geometry), the large-Nc limit (suppressing fluctuations), and focuses on the low-energy, long-wavelength limit near the critical point.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the holographic model and the dual Ginzburg-Landau framework, proceeds to analyze the high-temperature phase dynamics, investigates the low-temperature phase and mode coupling, and concludes with the electromagnetic properties of the medium.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Holographic superfluids/superconductors are one of the most studied systems in the AdS/CFT duality. In the low-energy, in the long-wavelength limit, they should be described by a Ginzburg-Landau theory. For critical dynamics, one expects that they belong to "model F" universality class. We consider a bulk 5-dimensional holographic superfluid/superconductor in the probe limit. For the holographic superconductor, we impose the boundary Maxwell equation to make the boundary Maxwell field dynamical. We identify the dual model F equations where numerical coefficients are obtained exactly.

</details>

### [Destructive interference of second harmonic generation in AA stacked MoTe$_2$/WSe$_2$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21231v1)

**Summary.** This paper identifies that AA-stacked MoTe2/WSe2 heterobilayers exhibit unexpected destructive SHG interference caused by a phase difference between layer-specific exciton resonances. By tuning the twist angle and excitation energy, the researchers demonstrate precise control over the SHG polarization state, including the achievement of near-circular polarization. This work provides a new framework for engineering nonlinear optical functions in moiré materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** The discovery of a geometric polarization singularity and the ability to engineer nonlinear polarization states via interlayer phase engineering is highly relevant to the field of nonlinear quantum optics and the development of tunable photonic components.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to resolve the ambiguity in stacking identification (AA vs. AB) in MoTe2/WSe2 heterobilayers and understand the origin of anomalous destructive Second Harmonic Generation (SHG) interference in AA-stacked configurations.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that AA-stacked MoTe2/WSe2 exhibits unconventional destructive SHG interference due to a pi phase difference in nonlinear susceptibility driven by distinct exciton resonances, and show that the SHG polarization can be controlled via twist angle and excitation energy.

**Method.** The study combines experimental techniques like Multislice Electron Ptychography (MEP) for atomic-resolution imaging, angle-resolved SHG spectroscopy, and reflectance contrast with theoretical GW-BSE calculations and a coherent superposition model.

**Model / system.** The system consists of MoTe2/WSe2 transition-metal dichalcogenide (TMDC) heterobilayers in AA and AB stacking configurations, encapsulated in hexagonal boron nitride (hBN). The optical response is modeled using a coherent superposition of electric fields from individual layers and a two-level Hamiltonian for exciton hybridization.

**Key observables.** SHG intensity, polarization ellipticity, polarization azimuth, Stokes parameters, MEP phase images, and exciton hybridization (energy anticrossing).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Stacking configuration (AA vs. AB), twist angle (alpha), interlayer phase difference (delta-phi), excitation photon energy, and vertical electric field (E).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The SHG susceptibility of the heterostructure is modeled as a linear combination of individual monolayer susceptibilities; a rigid redshift of the WSe2 spectrum is applied to account for stacking effects in calculations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows device fabrication and optical micrographs; Figure 2/3 details the SHG intensity, polarization states, and comparison between experimental and calculated dielectric functions; Supplemental figures include simulated electron microscopy images and polarization-dependent SHG models.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with device fabrication and stacking identification via electron ptychography, moves to the investigation of anomalous SHG interference and its excitonic origin using spectroscopy and GW-BSE calculations, and concludes with the demonstration of polarization control and mapping of polarization trajectories on the Poincaré sphere.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The stacking configuration of two-dimensional materials critically governs their optical and electronic responses. Monolayer transition-metal dichalcogenides (TMDC) lack inversion symmetry and exhibit exciton-enhanced second-harmonic generation (SHG). In TMDC bilayers, 60° (0°) stacking is conventionally expected to suppress (enhance) SHG owing to destructive (constructive) interference of the layer-resolved nonlinear polarizations. Here, we report an unconventional destructive SHG interference in nearly 0°-stacked (AA-stacked) MoTe2/WSe2 heterobilayers using two independent probes: atomic-resolution imaging and stacking-sensitive exciton hybridization measurements. Supported by ab initio GW and Bethe-Salpeter equation calculations, we show that distinct two-photon resonances associated with the WSe2 C exciton and the MoTe2 D exciton generate a nearly $π$ phase difference ($Δφ$) in their second-order nonlinear susceptibilities $χ^{(2)}$, leading to the anomalous destructive interference. We further demonstrate that in small-angle twisted MoTe2/WSe2, the SHG polarization state is governed by the interplay between twist angle $α$ and phase difference $Δφ$, and can be mapped onto trajectories on the Poincaré sphere. At excitation energies satisfying $Δφ$ + 3$α$ = 180°, the SHG output becomes nearly circularly polarized (ellipticity ~ 0.91) and undergoes an abrupt 90° azimuthal rotation, corresponding to a geometric polarization singularity in the parameter space. Our findings open new routes for exciton-resonance engineered nonlinear photonics and stacking-resolved optical functionality in moiré materials.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a method to integrate GaAsSb nanowires onto silicon racetrack resonators to create stable, low-threshold lasers. By using the silicon resonator to provide optical feedback, the researchers significantly reduced the laser linewidth and improved frequency stability. This approach provides a scalable path for integrating light sources into silicon photonic integrated circuits.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work demonstrates high Purcell enhancement and the use of a passive resonator to control the spectral properties of an active medium, which is highly relevant to cavity QED and the engineering of single-photon/single-mode light sources.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving stable, low-threshold, and frequency-stable lasing from GaAsSb nanowires by overcoming the broad emission linewidths and spectral instability caused by weak optical feedback in conventional cavities.

**Main result.** Integrating GaAsSb nanowires onto SOI racetrack resonators provides strong resonator-driven optical feedback, reducing the emission linewidth by more than a factor of four and significantly enhancing spectral stability.

**Method.** The study uses deterministic transfer-printing to integrate nanowires onto silicon resonators, combined with 3D FDTD simulations for mode analysis and micro-photoluminescence spectroscopy for experimental characterization.

**Model / system.** A hybrid photonic platform consisting of GaAsSb nanowire gain media coupled via evanescent waves to a passive Silicon-on-Insulator (SOI) racetrack resonator.

**Key observables.** Lasing threshold (Pth), emission linewidth, spectral stability (peak shift), cavity Q-factor, Purcell factor, and photon lifetime.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Lasing threshold of 8.6 +/- 1.8 uJ/cm2, Q-factor exceeding 10^4, and linewidth reduction of >4x.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the nanowire does not significantly degrade the silicon resonator's performance and that the transfer-printing process maintains high alignment accuracy.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the device architecture, SEM images, and PL spectra comparison; Figure 2 presents FDTD simulations of hybrid modes, coupling efficiency, and field distributions; Figure 3 displays pump-dependent PL spectra, input-output characteristics, and quantitative linewidth/stability comparisons.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the integration problem, describes the fabrication and transfer-printing method, presents numerical simulations of mode coupling, details experimental lasing measurements and stability analysis, and concludes with the implications for silicon photonics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Silicon photonic integrated circuits critically depend on compact on-chip light sources, for which nanowire (NW) lasers are an attractive solution. However, their practical implementation is often limited by broad emission linewidths and poor frequency stability resulting from weak optical feedback. Here, we integrate individual GaAsSb NWs by transfer-printing onto silicon-on-insulator (SOI) racetrack resonators to realize optical feedback at silicon-transparent wavelengths. Finite-difference-time-domain simulations reveal efficient coupling between the hybrid NW-waveguide mode and the fundamental TE resonator mode, with calculated cavity Q-factors exceeding 10$^4$. Experimentally, we observe feedback-induced lasing emission at a low threshold (P$_{th}$) of 8.6 $\pm$ 1.8 $μ$J/cm$^2$. Compared to identical NW lasers without SOI resonator, the linewidth is reduced by more than a factor of four at 3P$_{th}$ and remains stable below 1.8 meV up to 5P$_{th}$. Our results demonstrate NW-based light sources on SOI and show that tailored resonator designs enable improved linewidth control and frequency stabilization.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.21233"></a>
### [Stacking-order-dependent electronic properties of MoTe2/WSe2 moiré bilayers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21233v1)

**Authors:** Zhongdong Han, Wenjin Zhao, Eegene Clara Chung, Chia-Hao Lee, Zui Tao, Zhengchao Xia, Yichi Zhang, Yiyu Xia, Jekwan Lee, Bowen Shen, Ariana Ray, Yu-Tsun Shao, Tingxin Li, Shengwei Jiang, Yihang Zeng, Kenji Watanabe, Takashi Taniguchi, David Muller, Kin Fai Mak, Jie Shan  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21233v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21233_figures/2605.21233_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Stacking order and moiré bands of angle-aligned MoTe2/WSe2 bilayers. a, Side view. b, Top view. The atoms are shown in blue (W), green (Se), cyan (Mo) and brown (Te). c, Spin-resolved topmost moiré valence bands from MoTe2 and WSe2. Left: AA-stacking with two layers parallel to each other; right: AB-stacking with two layers antiparallel to each other. In each valley (K/K’), electron spins (arrows) in the two layers are parallel (AA) or antiparallel (AB) due to spin-valley locking in monolayer TMDs. Interlayer hybridization is spin allowed for AA- stacking and spin forbidden for AB-stacking.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21233_figures/2605.21233_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Stacking order determination. a,b, Optical image of angle-aligned MoTe2/WSe2 sample 1 (a) and sample 2 (b) on PC stamps. The SH intensity (c) exhibits a six-fold rotational symmetry as a function of the excitation polarization angle. The bilayer response is 2-3 times of that of each monolayer. ADF-STEM images...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a definitive way to identify the stacking order in MoTe2/WSe2 moire bilayers using STEM-calibrated optical techniques. It reveals that AA-stacking enables interlayer hybridization, which is the key driver for observing Chern insulators and Kondo lattice physics in these materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper explores how tuning structural symmetry and interlayer hybridization can induce topological phase transitions and Kondo-like physics in a moire lattice, which is highly relevant to many-body dynamics and synthetic quantum matter.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The electronic properties of MoTe2/WSe2 moire bilayers are highly sensitive to interlayer stacking order (AA vs. AB), but previous stacking assignments via optical SHG were unverified, leading to conflicting interpretations of topological and correlated states.

**Main result.** The authors calibrated SHG using STEM to accurately identify stacking orders and demonstrated that AA-stacking enables interlayer hybridization, which is essential for the emergence of Chern insulator, Mott insulator, and Kondo lattice physics.

**Method.** The study combines atomic-resolution STEM for structural verification, polarization-resolved SHG for optical characterization, and low-temperature magnetotransport and MCD spectroscopy to probe electronic phases.

**Model / system.** Angle-aligned MoTe2/WSe2 vertical heterobilayers forming a moire lattice with a 5 nm period, utilizing dual-gated architectures to control hole filling and perpendicular electric fields.

**Key observables.** Longitudinal and Hall resistance (Rxx, Rxy), Magnetic Circular Dichroism (MCD) response, Second Harmonic Generation (SHG) intensity, and Shubnikov-de Haas oscillations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hole filling factor (nu), perpendicular electric field (E), magnetic field (B), and interlayer hybridization strength.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The calibration assumes that the SHG interference patterns can be reliably correlated with the atomic-scale structural data obtained from STEM.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: Schematic of moire bands and stacking-dependent spin alignment; Fig 2: Comparison of AA and AB stacking via optical and STEM imaging; Fig 3: Electronic phase diagrams showing the emergence of new states in AA-stacking.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the stacking problem, structural characterization and SHG calibration via STEM, comparison of electronic phase diagrams for AA and AB stacking, and discussion of the physical implications for hybridization-driven phenomena.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Transition metal dichalcogenide (TMD) moiré bilayers have realized a wide range of strongly correlated and topological phenomena. The physics in these materials is often sensitive to the interlayer stacking order. Polarization-resolved optical second harmonic generation (SHG) is the most used technique for stacking order characterization but unverified for most heterobilayers. Here we calibrate the optical SHG for angle-aligned MoTe2/WSe2 bilayers by the scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM). We directly compare the transport and magnetic properties and the electronic phase diagram for two distinct stacking orders. With the calibrated stacking order assignment, we clarify the interpretation of earlier results, including the nature of the Chern insulator, mechanism of an electric-field-tuned metal-insulator transition at half band filling, and the Kondo lattice physics. Our work provides a consistent picture of the relation between the stacking order and the electronic properties of MoTe2/WSe2 moiré bilayers.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.21271"></a>
### [Dark solitons in the fractional NLS equation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21271v1)

**Authors:** Almudena P. Márquez, Jesús Cuevas-Maraver, Panayotis G. Kevrekidis  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21271v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21271_figures/2605.21271_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Profile of dark solitons in linear (left) and logarithmic scale (right) for different values of α. Blue, red, yellow, purple, and green lines correspond, respectively, to α = 1.7, α = 2, α = 3, α = 3.5, and α = 4.</sub>

</details>

<sub>FIG. 2. Dependence of the equilibrium distance between the dark soliton centers in a bounded pair δ on the L´evy index α &gt; 2. Notice the existence of two families (or three branches) interpolating between α = 2+ and α = 4. Additional branches emerge in the immediate vicinity of the biharmonic limit of α = 4, although given their exponential proximity to the biharmonic limit, we will not explore them here.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

(Right panel) Profiles of the bound states for the lowest (blue line), second (red line) and third (yellow line) branches at α = 3.8. </details>

**Summary.** This paper explores how fractional dispersion affects the stability and interaction of dark solitons. It reveals that unlike the standard NLS equation, all two-soliton bound states in the fractional regime are unstable, leading to complex behaviors like breathing orbits and soliton collisions.

**Why it may be interesting.** The discovery of unprecedented breathing orbits and the ability to model soliton interactions via reduced-order ODEs provides new insights into the complex dynamics of nonlinear waves in engineered dispersion landscapes, relevant to nonlinear optics and nonlinear wave physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the existence, stability, and dynamics of dark fractional solitary waves and their bound states (solitonic molecules) within the fractional Nonlinear Schrödinger (NLS) equation.

**Main result.** The authors find that all branches of two-soliton equilibria in the fractional NLS setting are potentially unstable, exhibiting either oscillatory or exponential instabilities, and discover associated periodic breathing orbits.

**Method.** Numerical analysis using Fourier spectral methods, Floquet analysis for periodic orbits, and a variational approach using a three-parameter ansatz to approximate stationary states.

**Model / system.** A defocusing fractional Nonlinear Schrödinger (NLS) equation utilizing the Riesz fractional derivative, which allows for continuous interpolation between different dispersive regimes via the Lévy index alpha.

**Key observables.** Soliton profiles, stability eigenvalues (real and imaginary parts), Floquet multipliers, and the time-evolution of density and projection onto unstable eigenvectors.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The Lévy index alpha (controlling dispersion), soliton separation/offset delta, and the frequency omega.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes periodic boundary conditions (using soliton pairs to simulate single solitons), focuses on free space without external confinement, and uses an ansatz that fails to capture power-law tails.

**Figures summary.** Figures show dark soliton profiles across different alpha values, the dependence of equilibrium distance on alpha, eigenvalue spectra, and time-dependent density evolution showing breathing and collision dynamics.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the fractional NLS model, analyzes single soliton profiles, investigates the stability and branches of two-soliton bound states, explores the resulting breathing and collision dynamics, and concludes with a semi-analytical variational approximation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In the present work we consider the subject of dark fractional solitary waves in the realm of generalized (fractional) forms of the nonlinear Schrödinger (NLS) equation. While earlier studies have examined such states in the realm of real field theories, we showcase the existence and stability of individual dark solitary waves in such NLS settings and subsequently turn to two-soliton solutions. We find different branches of such two-soliton solution equilibria and contrary to the real field-theoretic setting all possible branches of two-soliton equilibria are found to be potentially unstable, although with different types of instabilities. Odd branches are potentially subject to oscillatory instabilities, while even branches are always exponentially unstable. The dynamics that results from the instabilities is also examined and is found to potentially feature breathing characteristics. This prompts us to seek and find associated periodic (breathing) orbits that are also unprecedented in this context, to the best of our knowledge. The effective particle-like dynamics of the solitary waves also prompts us to seek ordinary differential equation (ODE) descriptions to the dark soliton interaction dynamics. These are shown to hold promise toward providing us with effective reduced order models, indicating some potential directions for further investigation.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21087"></a>
### [Spin Peltier effect in graphene](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21087v1)

**Authors:** Xin Theng Lee, Xin Hu, Yuya Ominato, Masahiro Tatsuno, Takeo Kato, Mamoru Matsuo  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21087v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21087_figures/2605.21087_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic illustration of a graphene/ferromagnetic- insulator (FI) heterostructure. An external magnetic field B is applied perpendicular to the graphene plane. The spin Peltier effect is induced by spin accumulation in graphene, which can be, for example, driven by the spin Hall effect in an adjacent normal metal.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21087_figures/2605.21087_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Schematic illustration of the FI-grphene junction.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21087_figures/2605.21087_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Quantum oscillation of the normalized temperature difference (∆T/δµs)norm = (∆T/δµs)/ max(∆T/δµs) plotted as a function of the inverse dimensionless magnetic field 1/B′. The lines correspond to different values of the temperature- control parameter JS/kBT = 10, 20, 30, 40, 50. The Landau- level broadening is fixed at Γ/JS = 1, with chemical potential µ/JS = 1. The oscillatory peaks originate from successive crossings of graphene Landau levels with the thermally active electronic window.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21087_figures/2605.21087_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Quantum oscillation of the normalized temperature difference (∆T/δµs)norm = (∆T/δµs)/ max(∆T/δµs) plotted as a function of the inverse dimensionless magnetic field 1/B′. The lines correspond to different values of the Landau-level broadening parameter Γ/JS = 0.04, 0.06, 0.08, 0.1. The tem- perature control parameter is fixed at JS/kBT = 10, with chemical potential µ/JS = 1. The oscillatory peaks originate from successive crossings of graphene Landau levels with the thermally active electronic window.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper theoretically explores how spin accumulation in a graphene/ferromagnetic insulator junction generates a temperature difference. It demonstrates that the resulting thermal response oscillates with the magnetic field, providing a way to use thermal measurements to probe Landau levels.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the spin Peltier effect in graphene/ferromagnetic insulator heterostructures and determining if it can serve as a spectroscopic probe for Landau levels.

**Main result.** The spin Peltier response exhibits pronounced quantum oscillations as a function of the inverse magnetic field due to Landau level crossings, which can be used to detect discrete electronic energy levels.

**Method.** A microscopic formalism using the Keldysh formalism and second-order perturbation theory to analyze spin-flip scattering at the interface.

**Model / system.** A bilayer heterostructure consisting of monolayer graphene and a ferromagnetic insulator (FI) under a perpendicular magnetic field, modeled with a tight-binding Hamiltonian for graphene and a spin-wave approximation for the FI.

**Key observables.** Temperature difference (delta T) across the junction, spin current (jS), and energy current (jE).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnetic field (B), Landau level broadening (Gamma), interfacial exchange coupling (JS), and the temperature control parameter (JS/kBT).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Strong spin relaxation in the FI, the spin-wave approximation for magnons, and the neglect of the Zeeman term for electrons in favor of a larger exchange bias.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a schematic of the heterostructure; Figure 2 shows the junction schematic; Figure 3 demonstrates quantum oscillations in temperature difference vs. 1/B for different temperatures; Figure 4 shows the suppression of oscillations due to increased scattering rate.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the spin Peltier effect, defines the microscopic Hamiltonian and Keldysh-based formalism, derives the spin and energy currents, analyzes the temperature response under magnetic field quantization, and discusses the effects of temperature and broadening.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this work, we theoretically investigate the spin-Peltier effect in a heterostructure composed of graphene and a ferromagnetic insulator (FI). Using a microscopic formalism based on the characteristic spin-flip scattering length at the graphene/FI interface, we analyze how spin accumulation in graphene gives rise to a temperature difference across the junction. We show that, in the presence of an external magnetic field, the electronic spectrum of graphene is quantized into Landau levels, which strongly modifies the available spin-flip scattering channels. In particular, crossings between Landau levels significantly enhance the spin-flip scattering amplitude, leading to a pronounced amplification of the spin-Peltier response. Our results suggest that measurements of the spin-induced temperature difference in graphene-FI heterostructures can serve as a sensitive probe of discrete electronic energy levels. More broadly, this work provides a theoretical framework for understanding spin-driven thermal effects in hybrid systems combining Dirac materials and magnetic insulators.

</details>

### [Excitation-Energy-Selective Control of Hot-Carrier Cooling via a Resonant Optical-Phonon Bottleneck in Graphene](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20430v1)

**Authors:** Sachin Sharm, Elliott Walker, Rachael Myers-Ward, Jenifer Hajzus, Yijing Liu, Paola Barbara, Ioannis Chatzakis  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20430v1>

<sub>Figure 2: (a) Relaxation time of photoexcited carriers in graphene as a function of excitation photon energy, extracted from pump-probe THz measurements. The relaxation time exhibits a pronounced non-monotonic dependence on photon energy. Energy-interval averaging yields mean lifetimes of ⟨τ⟩≈3.2 ps for E &lt; 0.42 eV and ⟨τ⟩≈3.8 ps for E &gt; 0.48 eV, indicat- ing predominantly fast relaxation outside the mid-energy region. In contrast, an anomalous energy-selective slowing of carrier relaxation emerges within a narrow spectral window be- tween ≈0.42 and 0.48 eV, where lifetimes increase to τ ≈10-25 ps (⟨τ⟩≈18 ps), reaching values up to ≈20 ps - nearly an order of magnitude longer than the...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.20430_figures/2605.20430_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: (a) Energy-dependent photoinduced change in the THz conductivity, ∆σ, of graphene following ultrafast optical excitation. Each data point represents the average of the frequency-domain conductivity extracted from individual THz spectra collected at the corresponding pump photon energy. The traces (b – e) depict the dynamics of the photoin- duced conductivity that was extracted from the measured ∆T/T for energies approximately 0.42, 0.44, 0.45, and 0.47 eV respectively.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.20430_figures/2605.20430_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Doping- and photon-energy-dependent carrier relaxation pathways in graphene. (a) Low-doping regime (EF = 0.06 eV), where Pauli blocking is absent and interband absorption is allowed. Photoexcitation with photon energies below the resonant window (ℏω &lt; 0.4 eV; red vertical arrow) generates electron-hole pairs. Carrier relaxation proceeds via emission of at most one optical phonon (ℏωOP ≈0.2 eV; purple curve) followed by decay into acoustic phonons (green wavy line), resulting in fast cooling on a few-picosecond timescale. b) High- doping regime (EF = 0.39 eV), where interband transitions are Pauli blocked for ℏω &lt; 0.4 eV and photoexcitation leads to intraband carrier heating (green...</sub>

(b) Raman spectrum of CVD monolayer graphene on SiO2/Si.(c) XPS C 1s core level spectrum of epitaxial graphene.</sub>

**Summary.** The researchers demonstrated that the cooling rate of hot carriers in graphene can be reversibly controlled by tuning the energy of the excitation light. This is achieved by triggering a resonant optical-phonon bottleneck that traps energy in the phonon system, providing a new way to engineer ultrafast energy flow in 2D materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding and controlling the ultrafast hot-carrier relaxation dynamics in graphene, specifically identifying the mechanism behind an anomalous slowdown in carrier cooling at specific excitation energies.

**Main result.** The discovery of an energy-selective control mechanism where carrier lifetime increases by an order of magnitude (up to ~20 ps) within a narrow 0.42-0.48 eV window due to a resonant optical-phonon bottleneck.

**Method.** Mid-infrared pump-terahertz probe spectroscopy combined with an extended two-temperature model (TTM) to analyze energy exchange between electrons, optical phonons, and the lattice.

**Model / system.** The system consists of monolayer and bilayer graphene (CVD-grown on SiO2/Si and epitaxial on SiC). The theoretical framework is a three-subsystem energy-balance model involving the electronic system, a hot optical-phonon bath, and a thermal lattice reservoir.

**Key observables.** Carrier relaxation time (tau), photoinduced change in THz conductivity (delta sigma), and THz transmission change (delta T/T).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Excitation photon energy (0.22-0.73 eV), resonant window (0.42-0.48 eV), Fermi energy (0.06-0.39 eV), and optical-phonon lifetime.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The electronic system reaches a quasi-equilibrium Fermi-Dirac distribution on a sub-100 fs timescale before the probe pulse; the lattice temperature is assumed to be fixed at room temperature.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the THz pump-probe setup and waveforms; Fig 2 plots relaxation time vs. photon energy and time-resolved transmission; Fig 4 illustrates the three cooling regimes (low, resonant, and high energy); Fig 5 shows Raman and XPS characterization of the graphene samples.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of carrier cooling, describes the experimental setup and material characterization, presents the discovery of the energy-dependent relaxation peak, develops a theoretical energy-balance model to explain the phonon bottleneck, and concludes with the implications for optoelectronic control.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Understanding and controlling hot-carrier relaxation in graphene is crucial for advancing ultrafast optoelectronic and terahertz technologies. Here, we investigate carrier cooling dynamics in monolayer and bilayer graphene using mid-infrared pump pulses (0.22-0.73 eV) and terahertz probe pulses. We uncover a pronounced, reproducible, and non-monotonic dependence of the carrier relaxation time on excitation photon energy. Remarkably, within a narrow spectral window (0.42 to 0.48 eV), the carrier lifetime increases by an order of magnitude compared to a few picosecond-scale cooling observed at other energies. We show that this anomalous slowdown originates from a resonant enhancement of the optical-phonon lifetime, causing accumulation and reabsorption of hot optical phonons that suppress energy transfer to the lattice. All observed behaviors are captured within a unified carrier-phonon energy-balance framework, where excitation-energy-dependent variations of the effective optical-phonon decay pathway govern the cooling dynamics. These findings demonstrate excitation-energy-selective control of hot-carrier relaxation in graphene and provide new insight into non-equilibrium carrier-phonon interactions near the optical-phonon bottleneck.

</details>

### [Fröhlich-type Polarons in Isotopically Enriched Hexagonal Boron Nitride](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20428v1)

**Authors:** Ioannis Chatzakis, Timur Abdilov, Elliot Walker, Jaime Freitas, Song Liu, James H. Edgar  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20428v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum optics experiment` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20428_figures/2605.20428_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1 Raman spectrum for hexagonal boron nitride at 295 K. The green (natural) and blue (10B isotope enriched) lines are the experimental data, and the dashed lines are the fits with Lorentzian function. The peak positions are at 1365 𝑐𝑚−1 and 1393 𝑐𝑚−1 respectively.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.20428_figures/2605.20428_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig.2 (Left) Spatial Raman intensity map of the 𝐸2𝑔 mode of the 10B-enriched hBN crystal. The uniform contrast across the scanned region indicates high structural and isotopic homogeneity. (Right) Corresponding histogram of the Raman peak position extracted from the map, showing a narrow distribution centered at the characteristic phonon frequency. The small spread confirms the high spatial uniformity of the sample, which is essential for reliable quantitative analysis of exciton–phonon coupling.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.20428_figures/2605.20428_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3 AFM image of the sample (top) and corresponding height profile (bottom) showing a thickness of ~4μm, confirming its three- dimensional character.</sub>

<sub>Fig. 4 CL spectrum at 6 K. The large amplitude of the D series of lines in the ~ 5.2 to 5.77 eV range, compared to the peak amplitude above 5.8 eV, indicates the existence of structural defects. The positions of each emission band (in eV) are annotated with the corresponding colors.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.20428_figures/2605.20428_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5 Expanded view of the high-energy region of Fig. 4. The green area highlights the transition located at ~5.56 eV. </details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that enriching hBN with the lighter boron-10 isotope modifies the lattice dynamics and dielectric screening, leading to enhanced exciton binding energy and the formation of large Fröhlich-type polarons. By using cathodoluminescence, the researchers provide a quantitative measurement of exciton-phonon coupling strengths. This work establishes a foundation for engineering the optical and quantum properties of hBN through isotopic control.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study provides a direct quantitative characterization of how lattice engineering (isotopic substitution) can be used to tailor phonon-polaritonic and quantum-optical properties in 2D materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The quantitative determination of exciton-phonon interactions and the role of isotopic composition in defining the optical response of hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) remains incomplete.

**Main result.** The study reveals Fröhlich-type exciton-phonon coupling in boron-10-enriched hBN, demonstrating that isotopic engineering can increase exciton binding energy and enhance polaron formation.

**Method.** Low-temperature (6 K) cathodoluminescence (CL) spectroscopy was used to resolve exciton and phonon-replica peaks, complemented by Raman spectroscopy and AFM for structural characterization.

**Model / system.** The physical system is isotopically enriched (99.22 at% 10B) and natural-abundance hexagonal boron nitride (hBN), modeled using the Fröhlich interaction and Feynman's path-integral theory for polarons.

**Key observables.** Indirect exciton energy (5.95 eV), LO phonon replica detuning (184 meV), Fröhlich coupling constant (alpha), exciton binding energy, and phonon energy shifts.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fröhlich coupling constant alpha = 0.159, exciton binding energy of 161 meV, polaron binding energy of ~48 meV, and exciton scattering time of ~97 fs.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the validity of the Fröhlich interaction model for long-range coupling and uses the Kanaya et al. diffusion model for electron penetration depth.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows Raman spectra comparing natural and 10B-enriched hBN; Figure 3 shows AFM thickness profiles; Figure 4 shows CL spectra with defect series; Figure 5 shows high-energy CL features; Figure 6 illustrates the phonon-mediated transition mechanism.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with material synthesis and structural verification (Raman/AFM), proceeds to the identification of excitonic and defect-related spectral features via CL, and concludes with a quantitative analysis of exciton-phonon coupling and isotopic effects.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Exciton-phonon interactions play a central role in defining the optical response of hexagonal boron nitride (hBN), yet their quantitative determination has remained incomplete. Here, we reveal the Fröhlich-type exciton-phonon coupling in boron-10-enriched hBN using low-temperature cathodoluminescence. We resolve the indirect exciton 5.95$\pm$0.02 eV together with its longitudinal optical (LO) phonon replica detuned by 184$\pm$56 meV, enabling the extraction of a Fröhlich coupling constant $α$=0.159 and a larger exciton binding energy of 161 meV, larger than previously reported values for natural-abundance hBN, which is attributed to isotope enrichment. The inferred polaron radius exceeds the lattice constant, indicating large-polaron behavior. We deduced an exciton scattering time ~of 97 fs, corresponding to a homogeneous linewidth of ~6.76 meV. We further obtain a polaron binding energy of ~48 meV and an effective mass of 1.045 $m_0$. These results provide a direct quantitative characterization of exciton-phonon coupling in isotopically engineered hBN and establish a foundation for tailoring its phonon-polaritonic and quantum-optical properties.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.20631"></a>
### [Probing Lattice Dynamics in Real-Space and Real-Time](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20631v1)

**Authors:** Navdeep Rana  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20631v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20631_figures/2605.20631_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1.1: (a) A representative high-harmonic spectrum from gaseous medium. (b) The three-step recollision mechanism of HHG from gases in real-space, adapted from Ref. (Thomson et al., 2013).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.20631_figures/2605.20631_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1.2: The underlying mechanism of HHG in solids. The energy band structure of monolayer graphene along high-symmetry directions within two band picture in which the conduction (valence) band is shown by blue (orange) in momen- tum space.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.20631_figures/2605.20631_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2.1: Hexagonal honeycomb lattice structure of graphene. (a) Real-space structure of graphene with a1 and a2 as primitive lattice vectors. The A and B represent two non-equivalent atoms of graphene arranged in the honeycomb lattice. (b) Brillouin zone in the momentum space with Γ, M, K, and K′ as the high symmetry points.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.20631_figures/2605.20631_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2.2: Energy dispersion in graphene obtained by simulating the tight-binding Hamiltonian in Eq. (2.1.4). (a) Valence and conduction bands along the high-symmetry points in one dimension, and (b) energy band structure in three dimensional.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.20631_figures/2605.20631_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2.3: High-harmonic spectra of monolayer graphene. (a) Red (blue) color corre- sponds to the polarization of emitted radiation parallel (perpendicular) to the polarization of the harmonic generating probe pulse. (b) Intra- and in- terband resolved high-harmonics.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work explores using high-harmonic spectroscopy to sense the impact of lattice vibrations on electronic properties in solids like graphene. It also proposes a new method using inelastic scattering to image atomic-scale lattice dynamics in real-space and real-time.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research aims to develop and validate methods for probing coherent lattice dynamics (phonons) in real-space and real-time, specifically investigating how these vibrations influence electronic responses in solids.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that High-Harmonic Spectroscopy (HHS) can characterize phonon energy, polarization, and chirality via spectral sidebands, and shows that inelastic scattering techniques can serve as a viable alternative to time-resolved diffraction for imaging lattice dynamics.

**Method.** The work combines theoretical frameworks (tight-binding, DFT, and semiconductor-Bloch equations) with simulated and experimental comparisons involving High-Harmonic Spectroscopy (HHS) and inelastic scattering.

**Model / system.** The primary physical system is monolayer graphene, with secondary focus on silicon and germanium. Models include time-dependent tight-binding, dynamical matrix calculations, and Floquet Hamiltonians.

**Key observables.** High-harmonic spectra, sideband separation, phonon frequency, polarization, phase difference, chirality, dynamical structure factor, and response function.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Attosecond and femtosecond timescales, phonon frequency, atomic displacement amplitudes, and probe pulse polarization.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study utilizes approximations such as the truncated expansion of band structure energy dispersion and the semiclassical three-step model for HHG.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the HHG recollision mechanism, graphene band structures, phonon dispersion, high-harmonic spectra with sidebands, and real-space/time visualizations of lattice vibrations in silicon.

**Paper structure.** The thesis begins with the fundamentals of HHG in gases and solids, moves to the use of HHS to probe coherent phonon modes in graphene, and concludes with the development of inelastic scattering methods for 4D imaging of lattice dynamics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The coherent lattice vibrations significantly impact physical and chemical processes in solids, such as heat transfer, displacive phase transitions, and thermal conductivity. Thus, probing lattice dynamics in real-space and real-time is essential for understanding ubiquitous phenomena in solids. High-harmonic spectroscopy (HHS) has emerged as a preferred technique for investigating static and dynamic properties of solids on ultrafast timescales. Yet, despite these accomplishments, the applicability of HHS to probe the influence of coherent lattice vibrations on electronic responses has remained unexplored.   In this thesis, we explore the impact of coherent lattice dynamics on attosecond electronic responses in solids using HHS. We observe that coherent excitation of the in-plane phonon mode in graphene results in sidebands in the harmonic spectrum, separated by the frequency of the excited phonon mode. Additionally, we demonstrate the capability of HHS to characterize energy, polarization, phase difference, and the "chirality" of phonon modes. This thesis offers an avenue to probe phonon-driven processes in solids with sub-cycle temporal resolution.   In the later segment, our focus shifts toward probing coherent lattice dynamics in real-space and real-time. We demonstrate that inelastic scattering techniques, combined with theoretical analysis, yield comparable results to those from time-resolved diffraction and imaging measurements within pump-probe configurations. Our findings exhibit excellent agreement with results from a time-resolved diffuse x-ray scattering experiment. Our proposed method serves as an alternative to time-resolved diffraction and imaging methods for probing lattice dynamics in real-space and real-time with atomic-scale spatiotemporal resolution.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates through simulations that ultrafast electric pulses can reconfigure the fractional topological charge within a ferroelectric nanodomain. By applying specific pulse patterns to vortex cores, researchers can program the system into 64 distinct, stable, and distinguishable metastable states. This provides a computational foundation for using topological textures as a multi-state degree of freedom in future nano-electronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether the internal fractional polar topology of a ferroelectric nanodomain can be actively and locally reconfigured using ultrafast electric-field pulses to serve as a programmable multistate degree of freedom.

**Main result.** All 64 possible pulse-mask configurations lead to distinct, stable, and distinguishable metastable topological configurations that persist for at least 1 ns in a cryogenic regime.

**Method.** Effective-Hamiltonian molecular-dynamics (EHMD) simulations using a first-principles-parameterized framework and a binary pulse-mask protocol.

**Model / system.** An ordered 12.5% Zr-substituted barium titanate (BT) nanodomain featuring a coupled Q = -2 antiskyrmionic and Q = +4 skyrmionic texture.

**Key observables.** Sector-resolved topological fingerprints, real-space polarization fields, local topological charge density, and pairwise mean angular mismatch.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cryogenic temperature (10 K), picosecond pulse duration (0.45 ps), electric field amplitude (6 MV/cm), and nanodomain diameter (2.6 nm).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study is a computational proof of concept performed in an idealized cryogenic setting and uses a simplified effective Hamiltonian approach rather than full atomistic complexity.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the nanodomain structure and topological quarks; Figure 3 displays topological charge fingerprints and inter-slice charge transfer; Figure 4 presents matrices of angular mismatch and RMS vector difference to demonstrate state distinguishability; Supplemental Figure S1 shows the potential-energy surface and switching path.

**Paper structure.** The paper defines the topological nanodomain structure, describes the pulse-driven switching mechanism and simulation protocol, quantifies the resulting 64 metastable states via topological and polarization metrics, analyzes the energetic barriers and thermal stability, and concludes with a proof of concept for topological reconfiguration.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Polar topological textures in ferroelectrics can host internal structure beyond a single integer topological charge. Here, effective-Hamiltonian molecular-dynamics simulations are used to examine whether such internal fractional topology can be reconfigured by local electric excitation in ordered 12.5% Zr-substituted barium titanate. Chemical doubling along the polar axis stabilizes a coupled nanodomain texture consisting of alternating Q = -2 antiskyrmionic and Q = +4 skyrmionic slices, in which the local topological charge fragments into six -1/3 and six +2/3 localized contributions, denoted here as topological quarks, separated by Bloch-point-like singular conversion regions. Picosecond local electric-field pulses applied to selected vortex-core columns drive reconfiguration of the internal dipolar texture of a 2.6 nm nanodomain. Under a binary pulse-mask protocol addressing the six vortex cores, all 64 masks lead, within the chosen low-temperature simulation protocol, to distinct relaxed metastable configurations. The switching calculations are performed in a cryogenic regime, and the programmed states remain stable over at least 1 ns of field-free evolution on the simulation timescale. The resulting configurations are distinguishable both by sector-resolved topological fingerprints and by their real-space polarization fields. These results provide a computational proof of concept that fractional polar topology in a ferroelectric nanodomain can be locally reconfigured by ultrafast electric excitation and used as a multistate configurational degree of freedom in an idealized low-temperature setting.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents an intelligent robotic system that automates the complex assembly of 2D van der Waals heterostructures using reinforcement learning. By achieving high-precision control over twist angles, the system successfully produced twisted bilayer graphene that exhibits magic-angle superconductivity. This technology enables high-throughput, scalable fabrication of moiré superlattices for studying emergent quantum phenomena.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The fabrication of 2D van der Waals twistronic heterostructures, such as twisted bilayer graphene, is currently a manual, low-throughput, and high-variability process that limits the exploration of moiré physics.

**Main result.** The development of an AI-driven robotic system that achieves high-precision, automated assembly of TBLG, successfully demonstrating unconventional superconductivity and correlated insulating states in robot-fabricated samples.

**Method.** An automated 'stack-bot' platform using a hierarchical computer-vision pipeline (YOLO and CRM) for flake recognition and a Soft Actor-Critic (SAC) reinforcement learning framework for self-optimizing motion and temperature control.

**Model / system.** An AI-driven robotic assembly system featuring 8 degrees of freedom (XY, xyz, theta, Z-optical, and Temperature) used to stack h-BN encapsulated twisted bilayer graphene (TBLG).

**Key observables.** Twist angle (theta), lateral displacement error, moiré wavelength, Newton's ring wavefront motion, longitudinal resistance (Rxx), and differential resistance (dV/dI).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magic angle (~1.1 degrees), twist angle range (0 to 5 degrees), temperature (down to 50 mK), and magnetic field (up to 12 T).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system assumes that the metadata from completed cycles (image residuals, drift, etc.) is sufficient for the reinforcement learning agent to optimize the assembly process.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the robotic system and the dual-level AI workflow; Figure 2 details the machine-vision and Newton's ring tracking pipeline; Figure 4 demonstrates high-throughput fabrication and the resulting electronic phase maps (superconductivity and insulating states); Extended Data figures provide statistical error distributions and algorithm details.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the fabrication challenge, description of the robotic hardware and AI algorithms (Level-1 perception and Level-2 reinforcement learning), demonstration of high-precision assembly, physical validation via transport measurements in TBLG, and statistical analysis of fabrication accuracy.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Nanomaterials stacked on-demand, such as rotationally assembled two-dimensional (2D) van der Waals (vdW) layered compounds, provides a versatile platform for quantum simulation and the exploration of exotic electronic phases. Currently, however, such nanoassemblies remain largely confined to inefficiency, manually operated process, limiting their potential for probing emergent physical phenomena. There is a pressing need in the field for high-precision, automated assembling techniques, especially for the scalable fabrication of 2D twistronic heterostructures. Here, we present an intelligent automation system dedicated to the fabrication of van der Waals stacks, following the state-of-the-art protocol for dry transfer of exfoliated 2D materials. The system further employs metadata generated from each automated stacking procedure to perform reinforcement learning, thereby continuously bettering its performances. As a concrete demonstration, we fabricate twisted bilayer graphene (TBLG) -- known for its challenging preparation -- and exhibit its unconventional superconductivity near the magic angle. Our work may pave the way for high-throughput fabrication of low-dimensional nanomaterials including twistronic heterostructures, where integrating data mining and artificial intelligence can accelerate the discovery of novel physical phenomena.

</details>

### [Designer Quantum States in Magnetic Topological Insulator Multilayers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21287v1)

**Authors:** Deyi Zhuo, Han Tay, Cui-Zu Chang

**Summary.** This review article details how magnetic topological insulator multilayers can be used as building blocks to design custom quantum states. By controlling layer thickness and magnetic doping, researchers can engineer complex phases like high-Chern-number insulators and axion insulators. This platform provides a versatile toolkit for studying topological magnetoelectric effects and 3D topological physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of engineering and controlling specific topological quantum states, such as high Chern number QAH states, axion insulators, and parity anomaly states, by manipulating the interplay between magnetism and topology.

**Main result.** The review demonstrates that magnetic TI multilayers act as 'topological Legos,' allowing for the precise engineering of designer quantum states, including high Chern number (C up to 5) states, axion insulators, and synthetic Weyl semimetals.

**Method.** The paper reviews experimental progress using Molecular Beam Epitaxy (MBE) for atomic-layer precision growth and transport measurements (Hall resistance and longitudinal resistance) to identify quantized states.

**Model / system.** Magnetic Topological Insulator (TI) multilayers and heterostructures, specifically using Cr-doped or V-doped (Bi,Sb)2Te3 layers combined with undoped (Bi,Sb)2Te3 layers as building blocks.

**Key observables.** Chern number (C), Hall resistance (rho_yx), longitudinal resistance (rho_xx), Hall conductance (sigma_xy), and magnetic exchange gaps.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Layer thickness (in Quintuple Layers), magnetic doping concentration, magnetization alignment (parallel vs. antiparallel), and interlayer coupling.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The review focuses on the regime of low temperatures (down to 25 mK) and assumes high-quality growth via MBE to minimize disorder.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the C=1 QAH effect in doped films; Figure 2 illustrates the 'Topological Legos' concept; Figure 3 details QAH in trilayers; Figure 4 shows high Chern number (C=1 to 5) engineering; Figure 5/6 covers phase transitions and SOT switching.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the realization of C=1 QAH effect, progresses to modulation-doped trilayers and high-C states, moves to mesoscopic devices and electrical switching, and concludes with the realization of axion insulators, parity anomaly states, and 3D topological phases.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Magnetic topological insulators (TIs) provide a highly tunable platform for engineering quantum states that emerge from the interplay between topology and magnetism. In this review article, we summarize experimental progress over the past decade in designing magnetic TI multilayers by molecular beam epitaxy (MBE). By treating magnetically doped and undoped TI layers as topological Legos, we discuss how layer thickness, magnetic doping, heterostructure architecture, and stacking sequence can be used to control magnetic exchange gaps, interlayer coupling, and the Chern number C with atomic-layer precision. We first briefly review the realization of the C = 1 quantum anomalous Hall (QAH) effect in uniformly Cr-doped (Bi,Sb)2Te3 films in 2013 and uniformly V-doped (Bi,Sb)2Te3 films in 2015. We then discuss how Cr-doped and undoped (Bi,Sb)2Te3 layers can be combined to realize the C = 1 QAH effect in magnetically modulation-doped trilayers, including its extension into the three-dimensional (3D) regime. Next, we review the development of high-C QAH states, engineered plateau phase transitions, mesoscopic QAH devices, and electrical switching of chiral edge-current chirality. Finally, we discuss the realizations of axion insulator and C = 1/2 parity anomaly states in asymmetric magnetic TI trilayers. These advances establish magnetic TI multilayers as a versatile materials platform for creating new designer quantum states, including synthetic Weyl semimetal and QAH metal phases, and for probing the topological magnetoelectric effect in thick axion insulators and 3D QAH insulators.

</details>

### [Designing Magnetic Topological Insulator Trilayers for Highly-Efficient Spin-Orbit Torque Switching](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21297v1)

**Authors:** Ling-Jie Zhou, Deyi Zhuo, Han Tay, Zi-Jie Yan, Pu Xiao, Xiaoda Liu, Bomin Zhang, Cui-Zu Chang  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21297v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21297_figures/2605.21297_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3| DC-pulse-induced SOT switching in magnetic TI (m4n) trilayers under different 0Hx.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21297_figures/2605.21297_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4| Origin of SOT switching in magnetic TI (m4n) trilayers. a, Left: Schematic of the</sub>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates how to achieve efficient magnetization switching in magnetic topological insulator trilayers by leveraging substrate-induced chemical potential asymmetry. By controlling the heterostructure design and gating, the researchers show that the switching of edge current chirality in the Quantum Anomalous Hall state can be effectively manipulated. This work provides a pathway for developing energy-efficient spintronic logic and memory devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the mechanism behind Spin-Orbit Torque (SOT)-driven magnetization switching and the associated switching of edge current chirality in magnetic topological insulator trilayers.

**Main result.** The researchers identified that a substrate-induced charging effect from SrTiO3 creates an asymmetric chemical potential between the top and bottom layers, preventing SOT cancellation and enabling efficient magnetization reversal.

**Method.** Molecular Beam Epitaxy (MBE) was used to synthesize magnetic TI trilayers, followed by electrical transport measurements using DC current pulses and gate voltage tuning in a Physical Property Measurement System (PPMS).

**Model / system.** Magnetic topological insulator (mTI) trilayers consisting of Cr-doped (Bi,Sb)2Te3 layers separated by a 4 quintuple layer (QL) (Bi,Sb)2Te3 spacer, grown on heat-treated SrTiO3(111) substrates.

**Key observables.** Hall resistance (rho_yx), longitudinal resistance (rho_xx), Hall angle, and the normalized switching ratio (delta_rho_yx/rho_yx(0)).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Layer thicknesses (m, n), gate voltage (Vg), in-plane magnetic field (mu_0Hx), and chemical potential (Ef) asymmetry.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the trilayer/bilayer structure and transport results (rho_xx, rho_yx) vs magnetic field and gate voltage; Fig 2 illustrates the SOT switching process and the dependence of the switching ratio on pulse current.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the SOT switching problem in magnetic TIs, describes the MBE growth and device fabrication, presents transport measurements characterizing the QAH state, details the SOT switching experiments, and concludes with a mechanistic explanation based on substrate-induced chemical potential asymmetry.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Spin-orbit torque (SOT) enables efficient electrical control of magnetization, offering a pathway towards low-power spintronic devices. Magnetic topological insulators (TIs), with spin-momentum-locked surface states and intrinsic ferromagnetism, provide a unique platform for realizing SOT switching of edge current chirality in quantum anomalous Hall (QAH) insulators. In this work, we employ molecular beam epitaxy to synthesize a series of magnetic TI trilayers with controlled layer thicknesses on heat-treated SrTiO3(111) substrates. Electrical transport measurements reveal that SOT-driven magnetization reversal and the associated switching of edge current chirality are governed by the SrTiO3(111) substrate-induced charging effect, which generates an asymmetric chemical-potential alignment between the top and bottom magnetic TI layers. Furthermore, we demonstrate that the switching polarity and efficiency can be tuned through heterostructure design, gate voltage, and in-plane magnetic field, consistent with SOT symmetry. These findings identify chemical potential asymmetry as the origin of the large SOT switching ratio in magnetic TI trilayers and establish a route for electrical control of edge current chirality in QAH insulators. This work advances the understanding of SOT switching mechanism in magnetic topological materials and paves the way for next-generation, energy-efficient QAH-based logic and memory devices.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that palladium deuteride exhibits multiple superconducting phases characterized by a unique resistivity anomaly, which is absent in palladium hydride. The researchers attribute this difference to the nuclear-spin isotope effect, suggesting that the superconducting order parameter in PdDx may have non-s-wave symmetry.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the origin of multiple superconducting phases in palladium deuteride (PdDx) and determining whether the difference from palladium hydride (PdHx) arises from mass-dependent tunneling or nuclear-spin isotope effects.

**Main result.** The study identifies a unique rectangular-shaped resistivity anomaly in PdDx that is absent in PdHx, concluding that the effect is driven by the nuclear-spin isotope effect (I=1 for D vs. I=1/2 for H) and suggesting non-s-wave pairing.

**Method.** The researchers used low-temperature absorption to prepare high-quality PdDx and PdHx thin films and performed temperature- and magnetic-field-dependent electrical resistivity measurements.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of high-quality palladium deuteride (PdDx) and palladium hydride (PdHx) thin films where hydrogen/deuterium atoms occupy interstitial sites in the palladium lattice.

**Key observables.** Electrical resistivity (rho-T and rho-H), superconducting transition temperatures (Tc1, Tc2), and characteristic magnetic field values (HLC, HAL, HAH).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Nuclear spin (I=1 for deuterium, I=1/2 for hydrogen), deuterium concentration (x), and temperature/magnetic field ranges.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The authors assume the observed anomalies are intrinsic to the PdDx system rather than due to sample inhomogeneity or impurities.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows resistivity-temperature curves highlighting the rectangular anomaly; Fig 3 shows resistivity-magnetic field dependence at various temperatures; Fig 4 presents the T-H phase diagrams comparing PdDx and PdHx.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of multiple phases in PdDx, describes the experimental preparation of films, presents resistivity measurements showing the anomaly, compares PdDx to PdHx to isolate the nuclear-spin effect, and concludes with a theoretical hypothesis regarding non-s-wave pairing.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the superconducting properties of high-quality PdD$_{x}$ films. The resistivity shows a sharp drop at $T$ $\sim$1.7 K, marking the superconducting transition. However, a finite resistivity persists and vanishes at $\sim$0.6 K. The temperature and magnetic-field dependences of the resistivity exhibit multiple anomalies within the superconducting state, revealing distinct superconducting phases. Such anomalies are absent in PdH$_{x}$ films. These results demonstrate a clear qualitative difference between the superconducting phase diagrams of PdD$_{x}$ and PdH$_{x}$, highlighting the role of nuclear-spin isotope effects.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Pressure-induced superconductivity in epitaxially-stabilized Pr$_3$Ni$_2$O$_7$ films](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20653v1)

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a method to stabilize otherwise unstable bilayer nickelates using epitaxial strain in thin films. It reports the discovery of pressure-induced superconductivity in Pr3Ni2O7 with a Tc of 66 K, providing a way to expand the family of high-Tc nickelates.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of exploring new bilayer nickelates (Ln3Ni2O7) due to thermodynamic instability and the need to understand how lanthanide substitution and pressure influence superconductivity.

**Main result.** The researchers successfully used epitaxial stabilization to create Pr3Ni2O7 thin films, which exhibit pressure-induced superconductivity with an onset Tc of 66 K at 22 GPa.

**Method.** Thin films were grown via pulsed-laser deposition (PLD) on LaAlO3 substrates and subjected to ozone-annealing; structural characterization used XRD and STEM, while electronic properties were measured under high hydrostatic pressure up to 22 GPa.

**Model / system.** Epitaxially-stabilized Ln3Ni2O7 (where Ln = La, Pr, Nd) thin films, specifically focusing on the bilayer nickelate structure under high pressure and epitaxial strain.

**Key observables.** Superconducting transition temperature (T_c_onset and T_c_zero), temperature-dependent resistivity (rho_xx), and lattice parameters.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pressure up to 22 GPa, lanthanide ionic radius, and epitaxial strain.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of DFT with the PBE functional and treating Pr 4f-orbitals as frozen core.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: Epitaxial stabilization, crystal structure, and structural characterization (XRD, STEM). Fig 2: Resistivity vs temperature at ambient and high pressure. Fig 3: Comparison of resistivity changes across different lanthanide substitutions.

**Paper structure.** Introduction to nickelate instability, experimental methods for film growth and high-pressure transport, structural characterization and stability analysis, results of pressure-induced superconductivity, and a comparative discussion of lanthanide trends.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The discovery of high critical-temperature $T_{\mathrm{c}}$ superconductivity in La$_3$Ni$_2$O$_7$ under high pressure has led to a rapid expansion of the $T_{\mathrm{c}}$ range through lanthanide $Ln$ substitution, and to ambient-pressure superconductivity in strained thin films, yet the exploration of new bilayer nickelates remains strongly constrained by thermodynamic stability. Beyond the difficulty of synthesis of bulk single-crystals, here we report on the pressure-induced high-$T_{\mathrm{c}}$ superconductivity in epitaxially-stabilized Pr$_3$Ni$_2$O$_7$ thin films. While the Pr$_3$Ni$_2$O$_7$ films exhibit insulating behaviour at ambient pressure regardless of ozone-annealing treatment, they show $T$-linear metallic transport and superconductivity reaching an onset $T_{\mathrm{c}}$ of 66 K and zero-resistance at nearly 40 K at 22 GPa. Furthermore, Nd$_3$Ni$_2$O$_7$, with the smaller rare-earth ion Nd, can also be stabilized, however, superconductivity is not observed in the measured pressure range. Epitaxial stabilization enables us to examine the dependence of $T_{\mathrm{c}}$ and the critical pressure $P_{\mathrm{c}}$ for superconductivity on the $Ln$ ion in $Ln_3$Ni$_2$O$_7$ ($Ln$ = La, Pr, Nd). These results suggest that a higher $P_{\mathrm{c}}$ is required for smaller $Ln$ ions, consistent with trends observed in bulk studies of $Ln$ substitution. This study demonstrates that epitaxial stabilization is a powerful technique to further expand the family of superconducting bilayer nickelates.

</details>
