**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the nonlinear Hall effect can be used to detect topological phase transitions in a twisted bilayer dice lattice where traditional Hall measurements fail due to time-reversal symmetry. By applying uniaxial strain, the researchers show that the Berry curvature dipole undergoes a sign reversal at phase boundaries, providing a clear experimental signature for band inversion.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Detecting and enumerating valley-specific topological phases in systems where time-reversal symmetry is preserved, which renders the conventional linear Hall effect ineffective.

**Main result.** The nonlinear Hall effect (NLHE) serves as an effective probe for topological phase transitions, exhibiting a characteristic sign reversal across phase boundaries and significant enhancement in the broken chiral symmetry regime.

**Method.** Numerical simulation using a continuum model based on the Bistritzer-MacDonald formalism to compute Berry curvature density, Chern numbers, and the Berry curvature dipole (BCD).

**Model / system.** A twisted bilayer dice lattice (TBDL) under applied uniaxial strain, modeled via a continuum Hamiltonian that includes interlayer hopping and broken symmetries (C3 and inversion).

**Key observables.** Nonlinear Hall response (NLHE), Berry curvature dipole (BCD) components (Dx, Dy), Chern number, and Berry curvature density.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Twist angle (theta), uniaxial strain (epsilon), staggered/onsite mass (Delta), carrier density (mu), and chiral symmetry regimes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Neglect of spin-orbit coupling and specific assumptions regarding interlayer hopping hierarchy.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: Schematic of dice lattice and moire Brillouin zone; Fig 2: Band structures under strain; Fig 3: Chern number phase diagram; Fig 4: Berry curvature density changes across phase transitions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of detecting topology in TRS systems, describes the strained TBDL model and symmetry breaking, presents numerical results for band structures and Chern phases, and demonstrates the NLHE as a diagnostic tool via BCD analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Valley polarized twisted bilayer dice lattice hosts topologically nontrivial flat bands far from charge neutrality due to broken time reversal symmetry, whereas the ones in the vicinity of it remain topologically trivial. However, when both valleys are taken into consideration, the time reversal symmetry is preserved, which poses a serious hindrance to enumerate the valley specific topological phases that rely on the detection of the Berry curvature. In this work, we demonstrate that such a twisted structure with an applied uniaxial strain exhibits a nonlinear Hall effect far from charge neutrality. We ascertain that the nonlinear anomalous Hall signals can serve as a probe for topological phase transitions associated with a specific energy state that is constrained to reside at the lower edge of the middle subband and controlled via a staggered mass. Specifically, we show that the nonlinear anomalous Hall response undergoes a sign reversal across the topological phase boundaries. By tuning the carrier density, we compute the nonlinear Hall response obtained from the Berry curvature dipole, both in the chiral limit, and also when the chiral symmetry is broken. It is further seen that the nonlinear Hall effect is significantly enhanced in the broken chiral symmetry regime.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21010"></a>
### [Properties of the skyrmion crystal SkX-2 in the Heisenberg triangular lattice with scalar chirality](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21010v1)

**Summary.** This paper explores a new type of skyrmion crystal (SkX-2) that is stabilized by scalar chirality rather than an external magnetic field. It details how this phase transitions between different types of order and how its melting is driven by the unbinding of topological defects. The work provides a complete picture of the phase diagram and thermal stability of these textures on a triangular lattice.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper explores topological phase transitions (KTHNY and Z2-vortex unbinding) and the emergence of complex topological textures in a highly symmetric model, which is relevant to the study of many-body dynamics and topological order.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the stability, phase transitions, and thermal properties of a specific type of skyrmion crystal, SkX-2, in a magnetic system without an external magnetic field.

**Main result.** The researchers identify a SkX-2 phase with two topological charges per unit cell and demonstrate that it undergoes a first-order transition in the lattice regime and a continuous KTHNY-type transition to a floating solid in the free regime.

**Method.** The study combines numerical optimization (basin hopping), Monte Carlo simulations (with replica exchange), and analytical approaches including Ginzburg-Landau theory and continuum expansion.

**Model / system.** A classical SO(3)-symmetric Heisenberg model on a triangular lattice, augmented with a scalar chirality interaction term.

**Key observables.** Topological charge density, magnetization, heat capacity, Binder cumulant, spin-spin correlation functions, and defect density (5-fold, 7-fold, etc.).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Exchange interaction (J), scalar chirality interaction (J_chi), and the parameter theta = arctan(J_chi/J).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a classical spin model and uses a continuum expansion for the energy density and vortex energy derivations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows ground-state energies vs. theta; Figure 2 illustrates various spin configurations (AFM, tetrahedral, SkX-2, FM); Figure 4 shows the Binder parameter dip; Figure 5 shows heat capacity peaks; Figure 7 shows vortex energy scaling.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the identification of the SkX-2 ground state and its energy landscape, moves to the evolution of topological charge density via continuum expansion, then explores thermal phase transitions and defect-driven melting using Monte Carlo simulations, and concludes with analytical derivations of vortex energy.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Skyrmion crystals have been primarily discovered under a magnetic field for materials with non-centrosymmetric interactions. More recent developments have investigated the stability of skyrmion crystals in itinerant magnets without magnetic field. In this study, we find that a type of skyrmion crystal with two topological charges per unit cell and no magnetization at the ferromagnetic point in reciprocal space, SkX-2, is naturally stabilized in an $SO(3)$-symmetric model with short-range interactions realized by the Heisenberg model on the triangular lattice with scalar chirality. We complement our numerical results with a theoretical analysis that quantitatively describes the transition from the ferromagnetic ground state to the SkX-2 and the evolution of the topological charge density. Despite the constraints given by the Mermin-Wagner theorem at finite temperature, the SkX-2 exhibits both a first-order phase transition associated with translation symmetry breaking and a continuous transition to a floating solid, depending on the charge density controlled by the model parameters. Finally, the tetrahedral phase supported by an antiferromagnetic interaction in our model is found to host $\mathbb{Z}_2$-vortices at finite temperature, suggesting the existence of an additional vortex topological transition.

</details>

### [Shear-Mode Raman Imaging of Ferroelectric Switching in Multilayer 3$R$-MoS$_2$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21210v1)

**Summary.** This paper uses Raman imaging to visualize how ferroelectric domains switch in 3R-MoS2 via interlayer sliding. It demonstrates that structural defects and boundaries act as pinning sites that control the pathways and stability of different stacking states. The work provides a non-invasive way to study the dynamics of domain walls in 2D sliding ferroelectrics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the microscopic mechanism of ferroelectric switching in 3R-MoS2, specifically how domain walls and pinning sites influence interlayer sliding and polarization reversal.

**Main result.** The study establishes shear-mode Raman imaging as a tool to track switching, revealing that mechanical boundaries and pinning sites cause independent switching in different regions and create partially polarized end states.

**Method.** Shear-mode Raman spectroscopy and spatial mapping are used alongside second-harmonic generation (SHG) and dual-gate field-effect transistor measurements.

**Model / system.** Multilayer 3R-MoS2 (rhombohedral stacking) in a dual-gate field-effect transistor architecture, acting as a sliding ferroelectric.

**Key observables.** Shear-mode Raman frequency and intensity, source-drain current (Ids) hysteresis, and second-harmonic generation (SHG) orientations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Vertical electric field (E_perp), domain wall orientations (zigzag, armchair, and chiral), and switching regimes (superlubric vs. pinned).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The Raman measurement timescale is much slower than the superlubric regime DW velocity, making intermediate states invisible in that regime.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows Raman maps and stacking schematics; Fig 2 illustrates the device, current hysteresis, and Raman-detected domain evolution; Fig 3 and 4 detail domain wall orientations and the impact of structural steps.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the 3R-MoS2 system, describes the Raman imaging technique, presents experimental evidence of domain-dependent switching pathways, analyzes the role of structural boundaries and pinning, and concludes with the utility of Raman spectroscopy for studying sliding ferroelectrics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We use shear-mode Raman imaging to track ferroelectric switching in multilayer 3$R$-MoS$_2$. Within a single flake, mechanically segmented regions respond independently and follow distinct pathways. Partially polarized end states indicate that domain walls can reside between selected layer pairs, producing partial stacking transformations. The dwell time of intermediate states varies widely, indicating that pinning sites strongly influence the dynamics. Second-harmonic generation measurements further reveal three characteristic sample-boundary and domain-wall orientations, including a prevalent chiral direction near the zigzag-armchair bisector. These results provide a direct, noninvasive view of domain-wall-mediated switching in a prototypical sliding ferroelectric and identify pinning and exfoliation-created boundaries as key factors governing its dynamics.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.20437"></a>
### [Superconducting PdTe Thin Film Via Topotactic Transformation, Toward Topological Superconductors](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20437v1)

**Authors:** Hee Taek Yi, Min Ge, Renjie Xie, Colby J. Stoddard, David H. Yi, Xiaoyu Yuan, Xiong Yao, Seongshik Oh  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20437v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20437_figures/2605.20437_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. RHEED and transport properties of a PdTe film. (a) RHEED patterns along two high-symmetry directions for a 40 nm-thick PdTe film (right panels) and an Al2O3 substrate (left panels). A lattice match is observed between 2d and √3d’ (see Figure S1, Supporting Information). (b) semi-log plot of the temperature-dependent longitudinal sheet resistance, Rxx, of a PdTe film. The inset shows an enlarged graph of the normalized Rxx versus temperature, measured on a 10 × 10 mm2 sample using the van der Pauw geometry. The dashed line indicates half the normal state resistance. Red symbols and line represent the remeasured Rxx(T) after 3 months of air exposure. (c) Upper critical fields...</sub>

</details>

<sub>Figure 2. RHEED spaces and XRD analysis of Pd-telluride samples with varying r values. (a) As the r- value decreases, the RHEED spacing reduces and saturates at r = 0.25. Star symbols represent another sample prepared under an identical condition, demonstrating the reproducibility of the synthesis method. <sub>Figure 4. STEM images of Pd-telluride films with varying r values. (a) r = 0.25 sample shows the PdTe structure with a stacking angle of 47°. (b) r = 0.5 sample exhibits two distinct regions: the upper region displays a PdTe-like stacking with an angle of 50°, while the lower region shows a PdTe2-like stacking with an angle of 52°, presumably due to some compositional mixing between the two phases. </details>

**Summary.** The researchers developed a method to grow high-quality PdTe thin films using a topotactic transformation from a PdTe2 buffer layer. These films exhibit superconducting properties nearly identical to bulk crystals, providing a new platform for studying topological superconductivity and Majorana physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty in growing high-quality PdTe thin films with superconducting properties comparable to bulk crystals, which is essential for realizing topological superconductivity and Majorana zero modes.

**Main result.** High-quality, superconducting PdTe thin films were successfully grown via a topotactic transformation from a PdTe2 buffer layer, exhibiting a sharp superconducting transition (Tc = 4.43 K) and 2D superconducting behavior.

**Method.** Molecular Beam Epitaxy (MBE) was used to grow Pd on a PdTe2 buffer layer under Te-deficient conditions to drive a topotactic phase transformation.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of PdTe thin films (NiAs-type structure) and PdTe2 buffer layers (CdI2-type structure) grown on Al2O3 (0001) substrates.

**Key observables.** Superconducting transition temperature (T_c), transition width, Residual Resistivity Ratio (RRR), upper critical field anisotropy (B_c2), and carrier mobility/density.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Te/Pd flux ratio (r), lattice constants, stacking angles, and the diffusivity ratio (eta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of a multi-band Werthamer-Helfand-Hohenberg (WHH) model to fit upper critical field data and a four-carrier model to fit Hall effect data.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows RHEED images, temperature-dependent resistance, and B_c2 anisotropy. Figure 2/3 details the phase evolution, structural changes, and transport properties as a function of the Te/Pd ratio. Figure 4 presents STEM images of stacking angles.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the motivation for PdTe films, describes the MBE growth and topotactic transformation mechanism, presents structural characterization (XRD, RHEED, STEM), details the transport and superconducting properties, and concludes with the implications for topological physics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Topological superconductors (TSCs) hosting Majorana zero modes (MZMs) offer a pathway to fault-tolerant quantum computation. PdTe is a promising TSC candidate due to its topological surface states and a reasonable superconducting critical temperature of ~4.5 K. However, it has been challenging to grow PdTe thin films with bulk-like superconducting properties. Here, we show that high-quality, superconducting PdTe thin films can be grown using molecular beam epitaxy (MBE). The films exhibit a sharp superconducting transition (T_onset = 4.43 K with transition width of 0.06 K), comparable to that of bulk crystals. This was made possible via a topotactic transformation from a PdTe_2 buffer layer to a PdTe phase by growing Pd on top under Te-deficient conditions. Structural and transport analyses confirm the NiAs-type structure of PdTe, as well as its two-dimensional superconducting behavior and excellent air stability. These findings suggest that the MBE-grown PdTe films and their heterostructures are a promising platform for topological superconductivity and Majorana physics.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.20869"></a>
### [The Topography Trap: Sifting Interlayer Excitons from Strain-Related Artifacts in Real-World 2D Hetrostructures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20869v1)

**Authors:** Pablo Hernández López, Luka Pirker, Astrid Weston, Arijit Kayal, Rafael Nadas, Adrián Dewambrechies Fernández, Álvaro Rodríguez, Roman Gorbachev, Kirill I. Bolotin, Otakar Frank, Sebastian Heeg  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20869v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20869_figures/2605.20869_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Screening protocol for interlayer exciton photoluminescence on TMDC-based</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.20869_figures/2605.20869_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Screening interlayer excitons in TMDC-based heterostructures. Sequential</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.20869_figures/2605.20869_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Resolving the topography trap with TEPL. TEPL provides the nanoscale spatial</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.20869_figures/2605.20869_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3f, the energy range of these strain-induced PL features almost perfectly matches the</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a systematic protocol to distinguish true interlayer excitons from strain-induced artifacts in 2D heterostructures. By correlating spectroscopy with topography, the authors prove that certain widely reported excitonic species are actually signals from strained monolayers trapped in interface bubbles. This work provides a much-needed framework for reliable spectroscopic identification in the field of van der Waals materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty in distinguishing genuine interlayer excitons (IXs) from spurious signals caused by strain and topographical defects (bubbles/wrinkles) in 2D TMDC heterostructures.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that previously reported bright, momentum-indirect GammaK-IX in MoS2-WSe2 is actually a strain-induced artifact of WSe2 intralayer excitons, and they provide a decision-tree protocol to prevent such misidentifications.

**Method.** A three-step decision-tree protocol involving intralayer exciton quenching analysis, energy thresholding, and spatial correlation between photoluminescence (PL) and AFM topography, supplemented by Tip-Enhanced PL (TEPL).

**Model / system.** 2D transition metal dichalcogenide (TMDC) heterostructures, specifically MoS2-MoSe2 and MoS2-WSe2, featuring Type-II band alignment and topographical irregularities like bubbles and wrinkles.

**Key observables.** Photoluminescence (PL) intensity, emission energy, intralayer exciton quenching factor, AFM topography, and sub-diffraction-limited TEPL maps.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Twist angle, energy separation between peaks (threshold of 200 meV), quenching percentage (threshold of 90%), and strain levels (0.2-0.7%).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The protocol assumes that high quenching of intralayer excitons is a reliable proxy for efficient interlayer charge transfer.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the decision-tree protocol flowchart; Figure 2 demonstrates the application of the protocol to MoS2-MoSe2 and the identification of strain artifacts in MoS2-WSe2 via PL/AFM correlation; Figure 3 uses TEPL to resolve the nanoscale origin of the strain-induced signal.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the identification problem, presentation of the three-step decision-tree protocol, experimental validation using MoS2-MoSe2, critical analysis/refutation of the GammaK-IX in MoS2-WSe2 using AFM and TEPL, and conclusion on the necessity of robust characterization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Novel excitonic phenomena emerging in transition metal dichalcogenide (TMDC) heterostructures belong to the most exciting topics in contemporary physics of van der Waals materials. Interlayer excitons (IXs) stand out among those due to their long radiative lifetimes and tunability by electric fields, strain, and twist angle. However, many ambiguities persist in the optical identification and manipulation of IXs, highlighting the need for reliable spectroscopic criteria that distinguish interlayer species from spurious signals. Here, we present a decision-tree protocol that evaluates interlayer coupling via intralayer exciton quenching and correlates photoluminescence (PL) with atomic force microscopy (AFM) to correctly assign room-temperature PL features in TMDC-based heterostructures. Applying this protocol, we identify momentum-direct IX between the K valleys of the two layers (KK-IX) in MoS2-MoSe2 and MoS2-WSe2 heterostructures at room temperature. In contrast, our protocol contests the reported bright, momentum-indirect, twist-angle-independent $Γ$K-IX in MoS2-WSe2. Comprehensive experimental data, including infrared and tip-enhanced photoluminescence (TEPL) with sub-diffraction-limited resolution, show no compelling evidence for this excitonic species, despite numerous reports. Instead, the spectroscopic features previously assigned to this $Γ$K-IX originate from locally strained WSe2 at topographical inhomogeneities of the heterostructure interface, underscoring the need for robust, spatially resolved characterization of real-world samples in this highly accessible field and providing a generally applicable framework for identifying interlayer excitons in 2D semiconductor heterostructures.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.20671"></a>
### [Tuning the low-energy band structure in twisted bilayer WSe2](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.20671v1)

**Authors:** T. -H. -Y. Vu, O. J. Clark, N. H. Jo, J. Blyth, Q. Li, C. Jozwiak, A. Bostwick, J. B. Muir, L. Jia, J. A. Davis, I. Di Bernardo, A. Grubisic Cabo, K. Xing, W. Zhao, S. H. Ryu, S. H. Lee, Z. Mao, K. Watanabe, T. Taniguchi, B. A. Chambers, S. L. Harmer, E. Rotenberg, M. S. Fuhrer, M. T. Edmonds  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.20671v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.20671_figures/2605.20671_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1 - (a) Schematic nano-ARPES measurements on the twisted bilayer WSe2 device. (b)</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.20671_figures/2605.20671_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2 - Determining the relative twist angles for homo-bilayer WSe2 devices. Bottom (left)</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.20671_figures/2605.20671_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3 - (a-d) High symmetry ARPES K- Γ-K band dispersions for four representative twisted</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the twist angle in bilayer WSe2 can be used to precisely tune the energy separation between different hole bands. This tuning capability allows for the control of band gaps and electron-phonon coupling efficiency in TMD-based electronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to understand how the twist angle in bilayer WSe2 tunes the low-energy electronic band structure, specifically the energy separation between the K and Gamma points.

**Main result.** The momentum positioning of the valence band maxima is independent of the twist angle, but the energetic separation between the K-point and Gamma-point hole bands can be tuned by over 100 meV via the twist angle.

**Method.** The researchers used nano-angle-resolved photoemission spectroscopy (nano-ARPES) with nanoscale spatial resolution to track electronic structure evolution.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a 2H-structured twisted bilayer WSe2 (a transition-metal dichalcogenide) van der Waals heterostructure.

**Key observables.** Momentum positioning of valence band maxima, energy separation between K and Gamma hole bands, band dispersion, and spin-orbit splitting.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Twist angle (ranging from 0 to 120 degrees), binding energy, and interlayer hopping channels.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes that the observed band shifts are driven by interlayer hopping of d-orbitals rather than moire-induced flat bands or correlated phases.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a schematic of the nano-ARPES setup; Figure 3 shows the K-Gamma energy separation findings; Supplementary Figure S1 shows band dispersions and Energy Distribution Curves (EDCs) with fitted peaks.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of band tuning in TMDs, describes the experimental fabrication and nano-ARPES setup, presents the systematic measurement of band evolution across twist angles, discusses the underlying mechanism of interlayer hopping, and concludes with implications for electron-phonon coupling.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Tuning the electronic structures of two-dimensional (2D) material-based heterostructures is of crucial importance for their use in functional next-generation electronics. Here, through angle-resolved photoemission spectroscopy with nanoscale spatial resolution (nano-ARPES), we systematically track the evolution of the near-Fermi-level electronic structure of bilayer WSe2 over a large range of twist angle. While the momentum positioning of the valence band maxima is independent of twist angle, we find that the energetic separation between the hole bands at the K point of the Brillouin zone and the higher binding-energy hole band at Γ can be varied in excess of 100 meV. We explore the mechanisms underpinning this evolution and discuss the implications for tuning both the size of the band gaps, and the efficiency of the spin-dependent electron-phonon coupling channels in homobilayer transition metal dichalcogenide devices.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.21345"></a>
### [Ultra-Confinement of Polaritons in Single Atomic Layer Ag Photonic Quantum Dots](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21345v1)

**Authors:** Xinyi Li, Tetyana Ignatova, Chengye Dong, Krishnan Mekkanamkulam Ananthanarayanan, Rinu Abraham Maniyara, Arpit Jain, Furkan Turker, Vinay Kammarchedu, Aida Ebrahimi, Joshua A. Robinson, Slava V. Rotkin  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21345v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21345_figures/2605.21345_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Material composition of 2D-Ag/EG plasmonic structures. (a) Illustration of the</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21345_figures/2605.21345_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Surface polariton waves revealed by eikonal analysis. (a) Schematics of sSNOM</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21345_figures/2605.21345_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2e shows the Argand plot, i.e., the correlation plot between imaginary and real parts</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21345_figures/2605.21345_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Spectral analysis of eikonal wave model. (a) Sketch of the hyperspectral sSNOM</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21345_figures/2605.21345_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. Dispersion of material properties of bare Ag vs. oxidized belt. (a) 3D</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a new analytical technique using Argand diagrams to study surface polaritons in nanostructures smaller than the polariton wavelength. It demonstrates that a single atomic layer of silver can ultra-confine electromagnetic fields in both vertical and lateral directions. This method overcomes the limitations of standard near-field microscopy in resolving wave properties in sub-diffraction-sized objects.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Quantitatively analyzing and extracting the propagation constant of surface polaritons in sub-diffraction-sized 2D nanostructures where standard sSNOM analysis fails due to lack of interference patterns and background interference.

**Main result.** Development of an eikonal-based analytical approach using Argand diagrams that successfully reveals the local propagation constant and demonstrates ultra-confinement of polaritons in both vertical (~lambda/50) and lateral (~lambda/40) directions.

**Method.** An eikonal-based representation of the surface polariton wave using Argand plots (real vs. imaginary parts of the sSNOM signal) to trace phase increments and identify wave properties via circular arc fitting.

**Model / system.** A 2D van der Waals heterostructure consisting of a single atomic layer of Silver (Ag) encapsulated by epitaxial graphene (EG) on a Silicon Carbide (SiC) substrate, patterned into nano-disks (photonic quantum dots).

**Key observables.** Third-harmonic demodulated sSNOM signal (amplitude and phase), local propagation constant (k_SP), and confinement factors.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mid-infrared range (963-1040 cm^-1), nano-disk diameter < 1 um, vertical confinement ~lambda/50, lateral confinement ~lambda/40.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The photonic dot possesses axial symmetry (radial eikonal wave); the variation in amplitude is slow enough to be ignored when calculating the propagation constant.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: Fabrication and atomic-scale morphology (STEM/AFM). Fig 2: sSNOM imaging and raw amplitude. Fig 3: Boundary identification and eikonal wave maps. Fig 4: Dispersion relations. Supplemental: Argand plots, validation against hBN/graphene, and gradient maps.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the analytical challenge -> Fabrication and characterization of Ag/EG/SiC nanodisks -> Presentation of the eikonal/Argand analytical method -> Demonstration of confinement and boundary detection -> Validation of the method using different material systems and bulk comparisons.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Light scattering by two-dimensional (2D) van der Waals heterostructures (vdWHs) is immense, especially given their infinitesimal volume, thus enabling strong light-matter interactions. Surface 2D polariton waves manifest through large concentration of electromagnetic field in vertical direction, normal to their propagation. By confining vdWH materials into 2D photonic shapes, one can manipulate and compress light in lateral directions. Scattering-type scanning near-field optical microscopy is a perfect tool for direct imaging of the propagating polaritons and studying the properties of confined polaritons in nanostructures. Though, thus far the quantitative analysis, such the wavelength extraction, has been challenged for confined polaritons by incapability of mapping of the wave period on sub-wavelength scale and difficulty of identifying an adequate substrate's "background" to subtract. Here, an analytical approach is developed to reveal the local propagation constant of confined polaritons under abovementioned constraints and map it with the sub-wavelength resolution. Applied to analysis of the SiC/2D-Ag/EG (epitaxial graphene) photonic nanostructures, the technique uncovered that the polaritons are highly confined in both vertical ($\simλ$/50) and lateral directions ($\simλ$/40) by 2D metal.

</details>

</details>
